and a post-appraisal mission in February and May, 1989, of Messrs. M.
Saddington, M. Woldu and S. Freiberg. Mr. H. Akhavan assisted with processing
project cost estimates, and Miss G. Moray typed and produced the report.

Credit and Project Summary
&2Borrower:    Islamic Republic of Pakistan.
Beneficiaries: Pakistan Agricultural Research Council and Agricultural
Research Systems of Punjab, Sind, Baluchistan and North-West
Frontier Provinces.
F   Amount:    Credit:  SDR 44.4 million (US$57.3 million equivalent)
[   Terms:     Credit:  Standa_d, with 35 years maturity.
Project        The project would be the second phase in a long-term program
Descripti2n:   to strengthen Pakistan's agricultural research.  Under the
first phase, the Agricultural Research I Pviject (Cr.1158-PAK)
strengthened the federal research system to eTable it to guide
and coordinate research throughout Pakistan. This project
would concentrate on the provincial research services,
reorganizing them to introduce more autonomy and flexibility
into staff and financial administration, and basing staff
promotion on merit and research achievement. It would
establish the structures and procedures for research planning,
prioritizing, resource allocation, programming, monitoring and
evaluation. It would assist the provinces to prepare long-term
Provincial Research Master Plans during the project's first two
years. It would finance priority research programs in
horticulture, livestock, soil science, integrated pest
management and social sciences which the Government has
identz.fied as areas of research to support the national
development objectives of the Seventh Five Year Plan.
Following completion of the Provincial Research Master Plans,
the project would also provide research funds for identified
provincial priority research. Finally, the project would
provide considerable training to upgrade research scientists'
skills and efficiency, and allow their movement into priority
areas for research, and technical assistance to help with
project implementation.
Benefits       The project would upgrade and strengthen the provincial
and Risks:     research effort, improve the efficiency and effectiveness of
resources allocated to research, and build sustainable research
systems, sensitive to national and provincial priorities.
Strengthening of key research programs, and closer research-
extension-farmer linkages should translate into technology to
help farmers increase production and raise their incomes. Main



- iv -
risks are problems with institutional changes, inadequate
funding and farmers possibly not adopting research
recommendations. Technical assistance and training would
facilitate the institutional changes. Improved research
planning, resource allocation, monitoring and evaluation would
strengthen research agencies' funding requests. Formulating
research programs with a farming systems perspective and
attention to close research-extension-farmer linkages should
ensure research program relevance, and farmer responsiveness.

i                                 ~~~~~~~~~~PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
|                       ~~~~~~~I.  PROJECT AND SECTOR BACKGROUND
I* _
A. Introduction
1.01      The Government of Pakistan (GOP) has requested International
Development Associat..on (IDA) assistance to further develop and strengthen its
agricultural research services.  The project is the second phase of an IDA
assistance program, and is mainly directed towards upgrading provincial
research services.  The project would reorgasuise provincial research
institutions to provide more autonomy and greater administrative flexibility
in staff recruitment, promotion and financial management, and establish
mechanisms for planning, programming, monitoring, and evaluating research. It
would support high priority research programs, provide staff training and
technical assistance, and improve research/extension linkages. The provincial
Departments of Agriculture and Pakistan Agricultural Research Council (PARC)
would implement the project which, by improving research relevancy and
quality, would eventually increase farmer productivity and incomes throughout
Pakistan.
1.02      GOP identified and prepared the project with the assistance of the
Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO)/IBRD Cooperative Program and the
International Service for National Agricultural Research (ISNAR).  The project
reflects national needs and priorities as ou.tlined in the Seventh Five-Year
Plan (FY89-FY93).  This report is based on GOP's feasibility report
(Agricul.itral Research II Project, No. 165/87 CP-PAK 41, December 1987), and
the findings of an appraisal mission which visited Pakistan during October
1988, and follow-up missions in February and May, 1989.
B. Agricultural Sector
1.03      Structure.  Pakistan covers an area of some 80 million ha of mainly
arid or semi-arid land of which about 39X or 31.0 million ha is suitable for
agricultural and forestry uses. Approximately 20.0 million ha are cultivated
(16.2 million ha being irrigated), 3.0 million ha are exploitable forest, and
8.0 million ha are rangelands. In about 951 of the country the mean annual
rainfall is less than 600 mm, and is concentrated in two seasons (the summer
monsoons which fall in July-August and winter rains which fall in
January-February). These two rain seasons have helped define Pakistan's two
crop seasons, karif (summer) and rabi (winter). Much of the central Indus
plain, the main agricultural producing region, receives less than 150 mm
annually, and hence agricultural production and development are heavily
dependent on irrigation supplies. The Indus Basin, which covers two-thirds of
the country, has the largest contiguous irrigation system in the world. While
951 of its command area (12.0 million ha) has been classified as having
moderate to excellent inherent capability for crop production, it is being
increasingly affected by waterlogging and salinity. Some 221 of the command
area now has a water table less than 2.0 m from the surface.



-2-
1.04      Agriculture is still the mainstay of Pakistan's economy.  It
accounts for 22% of GDP, employs 52% of the labor force, and contributes 55%
of export earnings. Arable crops provide 65% of agricultural GDP, livestock
31%, and fishing and forestry 4%. The major crops are wheat, cotton, rice and
sugarcane which cover 62% of the annual sown area.  Irrigated land produces
90% of total farm output.
1.05      Over 70% of the population lives in the rural areas.  There are 4
million family farms, with an average family size of six and an average farm
size of 4.7 ha. Despite the efforts under the land reform acts of 1959, 1972
and 1977 to redistribute productive assuts to the landless, farm distribution
is still characterized by inequity with 75% of the farm population owning 35%
of the area.  As this group's average farm size is 2.2 ba, many are
subsistence farmers.  Today 50% of farmers are owner operators, and 26% are
pure tenants; however, the size of the landless (wage labor) class is not
accurately known.
1.06      Performance.  During the Sixth Five Year Plan (FYP)(FY84-FY88),
agriculture grew at 3.8% annually against a plan target of 4.9% per year.
Pakistan, however, attained self-sufficiency in wheat production, and
increased its co!ton and rice exports, in particular, becoming a major
exporter of cotton and cotton products. Despite the large area of irrigation,
the sector is still quite vulnerable to climatic factors, and output in FYs
84, 86, and 87 was adversely affected by untimely rains and drought, as well
as pest attacks on cotton. Generally, major crop production met plan targets,
however, minor crop production has not. The sector is constrained by
structural and institutional weaknesses which contribute to its uneven
performance.
C. Development Objectives. Strategies and Issues
1.07      DeveloRment Objectives.  GOP's major agricultural sector objectives
are to: (a) maintain adequate food security to meet the basic food
requirements for the growing population; (b) diversify agricultural production
into minor crops (mainly fruits, vegetables and oilseeds) and livestock for
import substitution and export promotion; (c) generate employment
opportunities for the rapidly increasing on-farm rural households who largely
depend on livestock production and other farm-related activities; (d) assure
the participation of small farmers in agricultural development, and (e)
strengthen institutional support and provide incentives for regional
specialization.
1.08      Strategies.  Although it has been nearly a decade since the Revised
Action Plan for Irrigated Agriculture (RAP) and the National Agricultural
Policy (NAP) were issued, these reports' sector strategy recommendations
continue to guide the sector's development program.  The-main elements of the
strategy include: (a) raising crop procurement prices and keeping them in
line with international prices to maintain farmers' production incentives
while phasing out subsidies; (b) introducing quick impact investment programs
to improve the efficiency of existing assets such as rehabilitating existing
irrigation and drainage systems, upgrading the seeds industry, and
constructing farm-to-market roads; (c) increasing farm input availability;
(d) substantiallv increasing agricultural credit availability; (e) enhancing



-3-
the role of the private sector in supplying inputs and processing and
marketing commodities, and (f) improving cultural practices througl; better
research and extension services.
1.09      Issues.  Faced with a rapidly growing population (3.1% per annum),
and limited opportunities for increasing tha area of land under irrigated or
dryland agriculture, Pakistan must increase the productivity of existing
farmland to maintain its self-sufficiency in food crops, such as wheat and
maize, and meet the increasing demand for other agricultural commodities. As
well, Pakistan's agriculture is still characterized by low productivity and
yields throughout most of its subsectors. Weak agricultural support services,
institutional constraints, inefficient water management, poor agronomic
practices and pricing policies all constrain the growth of crop yields and
productivity.
1.10      Weak Agriculttsral SupRort Services and Institutions.  While GOP has
upgraded and strengthened federal research services during the 1980s,
provincial research services, whic'l service the majority of farmers, still
remain weak. In most crop production provincial research services are not
resolving the productivity problems which farmers face, particularly small
farmers (see para 1.12). Relevant research work and results are essential for
the efficient operation of extension services. Additionally, tertiary level
agricultural education institutions are weak, and in most areas unable to
fully train scientists in research methodology.
1.11      Inefficient Water Resource Management.  Irrigated agriculture in
Pakistan contributes 90% of agricultural value added. Hence, the management
of scarce water resources is vital to sustainable agricultural growth. Past
agricultural growth has been attributed to both acreage expansion and rises in
crop yields. Because of limited scope for further area expansion, future
sources of growth would be largely confined to productivity increases through,
among other things, efficient utilization of land and water resources. GOP
recognizes the need to reexamine the current formula for water allocation
among the four provinces and also among competing canal systems within a
province. The politics of water apportionment has so far prevented a more
rational water allocation, based on crop water requirements. Continuation of
the current water allocation would exacerbate the problems of waterlogging and
salinity, and overall water shortages. In dryland agriculture more research
is required on water harvesting and agronomic practices to improve crop water
availability and use.
1.12      Unegual Incote Distribution.  Land distribution in Pakistan is
highly skewed, with 75X of farmers having an average farm size of 2.2 ha and
the remaining 25% operating an average 11.6 ha. As a consequence, incomes are
similarly skewed, with a large proportion of small farmers operating at
subsistence levels. Additionally, many of them are tenant farmers. Their
access to institutional credit is much poorer than that of large farmers,
while government advisory services (research and extension) have been mainly
targeted away from them towareq the larger farmer, particularly in the
livestock subsector. The &vs-e-nment wishes to improve small farmer
productivity and incomes by orienting research, extension, credit, marketing
and small tubewell development toward them; encouraging d'versification into
higher value crops such as horticulture, inland fisheries, livestock and
village forestry production; constructing rural infrastructure (roads, power,



- 4
communications) and supplying basic services (potable water, sanitation,
health, education and training).
1.13      Insufficient Agricultural Diversification.  While during the last 25
years agriculture has grown at around 4.0% per annum, the main crops have
grown at a much faster rate, for example, cotton 5.7%; wheat 5.0%; and rice
4.4%. Thus minor crops, livestock, forestry and fisheries have grown at a
slower rate than the overall average. Since Pakistanis already consume a
reasonable annual amount of basic food grains per capita, the f"ture demand
for higher value commodities is expected to grow more rapidly as incomes and
dietary standards improve. To meet this demane. and to increase farmer
incomes, GOP has emphasized the diversification of farmer production into
higher value horticulture and livestock products.  GOP also hopes to develop
export markets in these products, thus increasing the range of export crops
and lessening the country's dependence on cotton and rice export returns, and
to substitute local oilseed production for imports.  Although GOP has pursued
this cbjective in the Sixth FYP, it has achieved little success so far, mainly
because the public and private sectors have not undertaken the necessary
investment in infrastructure and research and extension (production,
harvesting, storage, transport and marketing processes).
1.14      Degradation of Agricultural Resource Base.  Increasing population
densities and intens:ty of land use is causing considerable degradation of
Pakistan's agricult,ir.l resource base from the watersheds and rangelands of
the north to the plains and coastline of the center and south. Excessive
grazing and timber cutting in the higher areas is causing heavy soil erosion
which is silting up the irrigation dams and service systems. Overwatering and
poor drainage on the plains is increasing soil salinity and waterlogging, and
contributing, along with fertilizers and pesticides, to the pollution of water
resources, land, and food production. While strong institutions are required
to correct this situation, data collection and research will also be
essential, particularly in watershed and rangeland rehabilitation, saline
agriculture, soil/water/crop relationships, integrated pest management and
other areas.
1.15      Agricultural Pricing Policies.  While GOP has done much to bring its
agricultural pricing policies into line with world market trends, further
policy changes could lead to a m^re optimum use of resources and higher
productivity. Except for sugar. aver recent years GOP has generally
maintained producer prices for its major agricultural commodities (wheat,
rice, cotton) at or near world market levels. It has removed subsidies from
some inputs (Llitrogenous fertilizer, 1esticides, weedicides), and is gradually
phasing out others (phosphatic and potasic fertilizers, irrigation water
charges). Nevertheless, GOP is maintaining wholesale wheat prices at below
storage cost and edible oil p.ices at below processing cost. This, plus
sugarcane prices considerably above world market levels, results in large GOP
expenditures on subsidies for wheat storage and edible oil processing (plus
remaining subsidies on fertilizers and irrigation water supplies), an
inefficient, high-cost sugar industry, inhibiting of the private sector from
entering the cereal storage market, and small overall welfare benefits.



I5-
BRank's Agricultural Strategy
1.16      The Bank agrees with and has been supportive of GOP's agricultural
objectives and strategies. The Bank has been an active partner with GOP in
formulating the sector's strategy starting with the first Indus Basin Study in
the 1960s followed by an agricultural sector review and RAP in the 1970s.
Over the years, the Bank has witnessed the transformation of the agricultural
sector from a small beginning to one which generates domestic food grain
requirements and exportable surpluses in rice and cotton. Notwithstanding the
achievements of the past, the agricultural sector now faces critical
productivity constraints, such as weak service institutions (research and
extension), limited additional irrigation water supplies and poor agronomic
practices, which limit its ability to evolve as a dynamic and competitive
sector.  The Bank recognizes that future agricultural growth would largely
come from productivity enhancing measures and the improved utilization of
existing irrigation systems. Hence, the Bank's lending program is formulated
to assist GOP develop a competitive agricultural sector. While GOP should
continue to allocate a major share of investment funds to improving irrigation
and drainage efficiency, it must do more to strengthen public sector
institutions providing support to agriculture, particularly in research,
extension and education. Improved agricultural research and extension
services would play a critical part in increasing the sector's productivity
through providing new, improved crop varieties, better agronomic practices,
more efficient crop water use and drainage practices, and sustainable
agricultural systems which would not degrade Pakistan's agricultural
resources. GOP also should continue to align agricultural input/output prices
with world market prices, reduce subsidies, direct its investments towards
small farmers, and do more to protect, manage, and conserve its agricultural
resources. This project would be a key component in the Bank's lending
program to support GOP's agricultural developme-it strategy.
D. Bank Group Lendinz
1.17      Agricultural Projects.  Over the last decade Bank Group lending
operations have been on three broad fronts, the water subsector, other
agriculture, and policy-based projects. To improve the efficiet.cy of GOP's
large investment in irrigation inf_astructure, water subsector projects have
concentrated on drainage, irrigation structure rehabilitation, and water
management programs. ether agriculture projects have aimed at increasing
productivity through strengthening support institutions and services,
improving farming practices, increasing input supplies, and environmental
protection. The two largest programs in these areas are a series of one
agricultural research project plus four agricultural extension and adaptive
research projects, and six agricultural credit projects. Policy-based
operations have pursued a more optimal resource allocation in agriculture
through the cligning of input/output prices with world market prices, and the
reducing of subsidies on pesticides, fertilizers, and water charges. Future
Bank lending would continue to be directed at improving the productivity of
agriculture resource use, with investments to improve the performance of
installed irrigation systems, mitigate waterlogging and salinity, expand
credit services, improve marketing and distribution of food grains ard protect
the agricultural environment. Policy dialogue would continue with investments
aimed at improving pricing policies. A renewed emphasis, however, would be



placed on investments in research and extension services to break the
productivity constraints of inefficient resource use, low yields and
degenerating resources which many farmers face.
1.18      Research and Extension Projects.  Bank Group lending to strengthen
agricultural advisory services began in the late 1970's with two agricultural
extension and adaptive research projects (Crs. 813 and 922-PAR), one each in
Punjab and Sind Provinces. Simul aneously the Bank undertook the Agricultural
Research Subsector Review (WB No. 2961-PAK, May 1980), which was the basis for
the Agricultural Research I Project (ARP I) (Cr. 1158-PAK), the first phase of
the Bank Group's assistance to upgrade Pakistan's agricultural research. The
initial extension projects were slow to start-up, but as the recent Project
Performance Atudit Report (No. 7288, June 23, 1988) concludes, the projects
successfully: (a) introduced some discipline into the management of extension
services, for example, ensuring that extension staff made regular farm -;isits;
and (b) provided advice to small and medium sized farmers as well as to large
farmers on whom extension staff had concentrated in the past. The projects,
however, did contain some conceptual flaws, of which the most important with
ramifications for agricultural research was: intensive farmer coverage but
too few technical messages to transmit. This highlights the need for research
results relevant to farmer's problems. The proposed project is designed to
improve research planning to ensure research programs are directed at farmers'
priority productivity problems, and strengthen research/extension linkages to
make certain proven research results are transmitted to farmers.
1.19      ARP I (USS 40.0 million), which became effective in 1982 and ended
in December 1987, was primarily directed at strengthening federal research
capabilities, that is, the Pakistan Agricultural Research Council (PARC) and
its institutes, to enable PARC to provide research leadership, and to
coordinate and guide research throughout Pakistan. It emphasized
strengthening PARC through increas3d autonomy of operat'cn and improved
permanent staff benefits to attract and retain qualified scientists, and it
fostered an increased coordina-ion role for PARC in federally supported
research programs, including crop varietal yield trials and ongoing national
cooordinated research programs. The project achieved much in institutional,
civil works, and research program development (see Project Completion Report,
June, 1989). Major institutional developments include: (a) enabling PARC to
become fully autonomous witn its own Board of Governors within the Ministry of
Food, Agriculture, and Cooperatives (MINFAC), and introducing a satisfactory
scheme of service for PARC officers; (b) the overseas training of some 97
Ph.D. and 18 M.Sc. postgraduate students; and (c) establishing Agricultural
Economic Research Units (AERUs) at NARC and the provinces which are helping to
introduce economics, and a farming systems perspective, into research planning
and programming. However, this is a large, difficult task which will require
considerable additional resources and efforts to complete. In civil works,
the project built and equipped a considerable amount of modern offices
(including PARC's headquarters building), laboratories and staff housing for
PARC and NARC, plus a smaller amount for the provincial institutes and
agricultural universities. PARC and its institutes have also expanded their
work programs, and have already undertaken some good research and obtained
useful results, particularly in combatting plant diseases (wheat, rice, maize,
gram and sunflowers), and in plant introduction (wheat and fodders). PARC has
introduced a national agricultural research library and reference service for
researchers, and initiated a laboratory equipment maintenance and repair unit



to centrally service equipment from federal and provincial institutes. A
contract research scheme, while slow in starting, enabled some provincial
researchers to undertake good work in several areas, including buffalo
reproducticn, integrated control of major oilseed pests, survey and use of
medicinal plants, and control of soil-borne diseases. However, ARP I was less
effective in strengthening PARC's overall coordinating role in agricultural
research, mainly because it did not provide or institute suitable procedures
and mechanisms for continuous research prioritizing, planning, and
programming. This, plus formal monitoring and evaluation of research
programs, should now be introduced at both the federal and provincial levels
in this second phase development. Insufficient and untimely funding on
occasions constrained ARP I implementation. Improved research long and short-
term planning, which the proposed project will introduce, would allow research
administrators to press more effectively for adequate, timely funding.
II. AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH IN PAKISTAN
A. Research Institutions and Organization
2.01      Overview.  The agricultural research system in Pakistan is complex.
While the federal government is responsible for research, high-level manpower
training, and coordination, the Constitution delegates responsibility for
agriculture to the provinces. As a result, the provinces have controlled most
applied or adaptive research, while the federal government has retained
responsibility for basic and long-term research (including most forestry
research). Federal organizations have also initiated high priority research
which the provinces have neglected. The position is further complicated
because GOP has distributed national responsibility for research among several
ministries and semi-autonomous institutions, such as MINFAC, Ministry of Water
and Power, Ministry of Commerce, Ministry of Education, the Atomic Energy
Commission (AEC) and others. In total there are 24 research establishments
operating at the federal level, while in the provinces government agencies
operate in excess of 70 research establishments. Three major agricultural
universities also undertake research.
2.02      In 1984 (latest year complete figures are available), a total of
3,431 scientists were involved in agricultural research and related
activities. Table 2.1 indicates the location of these scientists, and their
academic qualifications.

2.03      Since 1984, the number of scientists is estimated to have increased
considerably to around 4,500, of whom some 1,900 are working in the provincial
research services, 1,800 in the federal services, and 800 in universities.
However, not all of these scientists are involved full time in research. Many
are involved in nursery stock or seed production, and mo.st livestock
scientists deal with disease diagnosis, vaccine production, or other farmer
services.
2.04      Federal Research Institutions:  Pakistan Agricultural Research
Council.  PARC is the major federal agricultural research organization.  Its
functions broadly include promoting and coordinating agricultural research in
line with Pakistan's economic and social development policies, initiating and
conducting basic research, and developing new research in priority areas
currently neglected. PARC is an autonomous body within MINFAC, and works
closely with the provincial Agricultural Departments, provincial Agricultural
Research Institutes (ARIs), and Agricultural Universities. It is directed by
a Board of Governors, which is headed by the Minister of Food, Agriculture and
Cooperatives, and selected from federal and provincial research organizations
and farmers. An Executive Board, consisting of a chairman and five full- tme
members representing crops, natural resources, animal sciences, social
services, and finance controls PARC's day-to-day operations.
2.05      PARC Institutes.  PARC operates eight institutes, and employs 568
scientists, of whom 94 have PhD degrees (May, 1989). NARC, situated on 560 ha
just outside Islamabad, is PARC's newest (established 1984) and largest
institute, and main operational arm.  Its staff undertake a wide range of crop
and livestock research, with emphasis on plant introduction and breeding,



plant protection, agricultural machinery development, economics, and livestock
feeding and crossbreeding.
2.06      National Coordinated Research Programs.  Under its mandate to
promote, aid _nd coordinate agricultural research, PARC currently funds some
30 National Coordinated Research Programs (NCRPs), covering crop production.
land and water resources, forestry and environment, and animal health and
production. Several of the larger NCRPs have substantially contributed tc
increasing production and/or efficiency in their areas, and have also
interacted well with the international research institutes (particularly
CIMMYT and IRRI under the wheat and rice programs). NCRPs usually expand or
add to the basic research programs the pLovinces and universities are
undertaking. While some NCRPs do not meet the basic criteria for coordinated
programs (too small or narrowly focussed, or do not cover complementary,
coordinated work), PARC is currently reviewing these programs, and will either
redesign ,r discard them.
2.07      Other Federal Agricultural Research Institutions.  Other important
institutions undertakirg agricultural research at the federal level include:
the Pakistan Central Cc*_ton Committee (PCCC), which is responsible for
research and development in cotton growing, manufacturing and marketing; AEC
with three institutes studying the application of nuclear techniques to
agriculture and biological research; Pakistan Forestry Institute (PFI), which
is responsible for forestry research throughout Pakistan; Water and Power
Authority (WAPDA) which does research on waterlogging and salinity, and the
Ministry of Science and Technology which undertakes research on irrigated
agriculture through the Pakistan Council of Research in Water Resources
(PCRWR).
2.08      Provincial Researc'a Institutes.' All the provinces have their own
agricultural research services covering crops, livestock, forestry, and in
some instances, fisheries. These services are generally administered within
separate provincial line departments, which usually conduct research in
isolation from each other. In North West Frontier province (NWFP) the
Agricultural University is responsible for crop and livestock research. All
provinces place major emphasis on crop research, and each has an apex
agricultural (that is, crop) research institute (ARI) with a supporting
network of substations and experimental farms. Some have commodity-oriented
institutes. While several research institutes employ large numbers of staff,
they have few experienced researchers with PhD qualifications, or trained
research leaders.
2.09      Of the four provinces, Punjab has by far the largest and best
trained agricultural research staff. The main crop research institutes2 are
Ayub Agricultural Research Institute (AARI), Punjab; ARI Tandojam, Sind; ARI
Sariab, Baluchistan; and ARI Tarnab, NWFP.  Each ARI is headed by a
'/   See Annex 2, Appendix 4 for more detail concerning Provincial Research
Institutes.
2/   Total scientific staff are AARI Faisalabad 820, including 25 PhDs (Oct.
1988); ARI Tandojam 196, including 2 PhDs (1984); ARI Sariab 43, including
1 PhD (Oct. 1988); and ARI Tarnab 220, including 9 PhDs (Oct. 1988).



- 10 -
Director-General of Agricultural Research (DGAR) who reports to the Secretary
of Agriculture. Separate livestock research institutes are headed by
Director-Generals of Livestock Research who report to the Secretary of
Agriculture (Sind) or to the Secretary of Livestock (Punjab and Baluchistan).
Livestock research is usually limited, with most of these staff producing
vaccine or supplying farmers' veterinary services. Recently, in NWFP, with
assistance from the USAID-financed Transformation and Integration of
Provincial Agricultural Network Project (TIPAN), the Government of NWFP
(GONWFP) transferred responsibility for crop and livestock research from the
Department of Agriculture to the Agricultural University, Peshawar (AUP).
This is an evolving situation and will take several years yet to complete.
2.10      Agricultural Universities.  Punjab, Sind and NWFP have agricultural
universities. While the University Grants Commission (a federal body) funds
the universities, the provincial governments administer them (Agricultural
Department in Punjab and NWFP, Education Department in Sind). As AUP is now
responsible for crop and livestock research in NWFP, it also receives
provincial funds for agricultural research. The Agricultural University,
Faisalabad, in Punjab (AUF) and the Sind Agricultural University at Tandojam
(SAU) do not receive provincial funds, and their research programs are limited
to MSc and PhD thesis preparation.
2.11      International Sunnort for Agricultural Research.  Several other
donors are also supporting agricultural research in Pakistan. Besides IDA,
USAID is the other major donor, and is currently financing two projects, that
is, Management of Agricultural Research and Technology Project (MART) and
TIPAN. MART (US$30 million) is assisting to improve federal research
management and administration and the transfer of research results to
farmers. TIPAN (US$40 million, see para 2.09) is supporting the upgrading of
AUP, including the integrating of agricultural teaching, research and outreach
activities, and the transfer of responsibility for crops and livestock
research frum NWFP Agricultural Department to the University. USAID has also
supported research (mainly PARC-sponsored contract research) with funds from
Public Law-480 foodstuff sales (US$14.5 million).
2.12      Other assistance includes the Canadian International Development
Agency's (CIDA) support to resear.:h in rainfed agriculture (US$11 million);
Italy's support (US$10 million) for introducing improved farming practices
into three districts in different areas of Pakistan; and the Swiss
Government's support of research in potato seed production. Additionally,
IRRI, CIMMYT, CIP and ICARDA have made available training facilities,
germplasm and the services of experts. The proposed project has been designed
to complement, and its activities to integrate with, those of other donors,
particularly MART and TIPAN projects.
B. Constraints to Research
2.13      Substantial inputs from GOP, with IDA and bilateral assistance, has
gone far to establish a viable federal agricultural research system,
particularly in terms of facilities, equipment, and in developing a cadre of
well-trained scientists. Nevertheless further development is needed in
research planning, management, coordination, monitoring, evaluation and
funding. At the provincial level, however, severe constraints exist to
undertaking efficient, effective research. The major constraints include:



- 11 -
(a)  ineffective planning, programming, monitoring, and evaluation of
research pertinent to farmers' problems;
(b)  insufficient training and research qualifications of many of its
scientists;
(c)  inadequate operating funds to conduct meaningful research;
(d)  rigidities of the research service schemes since they are tied into
provincial government civil service rules which reward seniority and
not merit, with concomitant low morale of scientific staff;
(e)  little on-farm research to determine the farming systems in
practice, and research to remedy productivity problems; and
(f)  ineffective linkages with the ex;tension services for effective
transmission of demonstrated improved technology to farmers.
Annex 2 and the following sections discuss these constraints in more detail.
C. Policy Guidance. Planning. and Coordination
2.14      Determining a national agricultural research program3 is at least a
three stage sequential process, including: (a) determining broad research
priorities at the national and provincial levels, and allocating resources to
these; (b) at the research institute level, within the broad priority
allocations determining the sub-allocations to high priority technical
research thrusts, and developing long-range plans (10-15 years) involving
manpower development and additional required resources; and (c) at the
research station or program level deciding the most urgent and relevant
experiments and studies to be done in the next year or two with available
manpower and financial resources.
2.15      At the federal level, while several ministries and agencies are
responsible for policy guidance and budget control of agricultural research
(para 2.01), MINFAC, through its Agricultural Research Division (ARD) and
PARC, is the main body responsible to develop overall research planning and
coordination. To translate government development policies into research
topics, and develop research planning and coordination, PARC has produced a
National Agricultural Research Plan (NARP, 1986). However, while NARP
identifies broad goals and objectives for future research and lists many areas
for research (including horticulture, livestock, soil science, integrated pest
management and social science to support the Seventh FYP development
objectives), it does not quantify the available resources to be allocated to
the various research thrusts, nor show the long-term plans to achieve research
objectives. Thus, at the national and provincial levels, there still needs to
be a ranking and allocation of resources between research priorities, as well
as developing, at the institute level, long-term research plans.
2.16      PARC's Board of Governors, with its associated Policy Advisory and
Agro-Business Relations committees, and with assistance from PARC's Planning
Directorate in analyzing ongoing needs and opportunities in agriculture, is
the appropriate body to identify b:oad national research thrusts and allocate
available resources between them. In the absence of unified federal and
provincial research services, however, there can be no completely
3/   For more detail on reseach planning and programming, see Annex 2, para 29.



- 12 -
hierarchical, authoritative allocations, and "agreed guidelines" would be a
more appropriate target. The provinces, of course, would have their own
priorities to guide their provincial allocations for research, but would do
this within national guidelines. At the provincial level, however, suitable
bodies generally are not in place whtich can undertake this planning stage. As
well, coordination of research is minimal with the various departments
responsible for crop, livestock, forestry, and fishery research develoning
their programs in isolation from each other, and without considering the
integration of various activities on the farm. Punjab has tried to integrate
and coordinate its provincial research through a Provincial Research
Coordination Board, but has failed because the Board has no control )ver
funding, has tried to directly control every facet of the research process,
and is too large (67 members) to be effective. NWFP, in the merging of its
crop and livestock research under AUP, has recently established a University
Research Coordination Board, which should have the authority to successfully
delineate provincial research priorities and allocate resources between them.
2.17      At the research institute level, except for NARC (with assistance
from MART project), no institutes are undertaking long-term research planning.
All research programming is on a seasonal or yearly basis, and institutes use
a variety of procedures and criteria to select their programs. Few programs
are based on a farm..ng systems approach to discover farmers' productivity
constraints. This diversity of procedures and criteria leads to inconsistency
in the standards of research proposals, and research which is irrelevant to
the users of research results, that is, farmers.
2.18      Monitoring and Evaluation.  While there always has been a
considerable amount of informal monitoring and evaluation of research at
various levels in Pakistan, formal monitoring, and evaluation, has usually
been limited to donor-assisted projects.  As well, Punjab's Provincial
Research Coordination Board has also monitored and evaluated research, but,
for the reasons listed before (para 2.16), has been unable to generate
follow-up to this work. PARC and the provinces have now resolved, with
assistance from this project, to introduce formal monitoring and evaluation
into all agricultural research in Pakistan.
D. Research Management and Financine
2.19      Management.  The federal research institutions such as PARC, PCCC,
and AEC, have independent budgets, a reasonable independence in operations,
and freedom to hire and promote staff according to need and merit. The staff
have a scientific scheme of service focused primarily on the research task.
PARC also has direct responsibility for its budget and has been able to raise
operational and capital funding per scien-ist to acceptable levels. The
provincial research institutions are, in most cases, part of development
departments responsible for agriculture, livestock, forestry and fisheries,
and oriented primarily to development and production. The research
institutions operate under hierarchical, in-line control from the head of the
department, within departmental budgets, and with a scheme of service that
relates to hierarchical values of years of experience, number of staff
supervised, and resources controlled, rather than research capabilities and
performance. In contrast to PARC, the provincial system of management is too
rigid, does not reward staff or promote them on merit, and has led to the
development of a poor research environment.



-13 -
2.20      Financing.  In FY84, the last year for which figures are available,
the total investment in agricultural research was Rs786 million (US$60.5
million), equivalent to about 0.2X of Gross Domestic Product (GDP) or 0.8X of
agricultural GDP (AGDP).  While GOP has increased its agricultural research
investment from 0.5% of AGDP .-n FY81 to 0.81 of AGDP in FY84, it is still well
below the Bank suggested rate of 1X of AGDP.  Additionally, Pakistan's
expenditures per research scientist are low when compared to the need for
operational expenditures, and those of other developing countries.  In terms
of operational funding for materials, labor, support staff, office space and
transport, research scientists require a certain minimum to allow the
efficient use of their time and research creativity.  In many of Pakistan's
research institutes, operational funding levels have fallen well below this
target.  Actual average operational funding level per research scientist in
FY84 was equivalent to about US$3,000 compared to an estimated basic
requirement of US$8,000.  Other developing countries are spending from
US$8,000 to US$20,000 per research scientist.4 Even when Pakistan's total
spending per research scientist (scientists salaries and operational
expenditures) is compared to that of 67 other developing countries, its
expenditure is amongst the bottom 10% (unpublished data, ISNAR, 1980).
Additional operating funds allocated to priority research would have a high
rate of return.
E.  Trainina and Research/Extension Linkages
2.21      Agriculture Universities.  The agriculture universities at
Faisalabad (AUF), Tandojam (SAU) and Peshawar provide undergraduate courses in
most aspects of agriculture (including animal sciences), and a wide range of
master degree courses.  There is no shortage of staff at the primary degree
level, and university capabilities for BSc training are in excess of
anticipated requirements. A shortage of staff with research experience, and
limited resources, largely restricts postgraduate teaching to master degree
courses. There is an urgent need to upgrade many of the university faculties
so that they can provide doctoral degree courses, particularly in social
sciences.
2.22      Research Scientist Oualifications.  The quality of research staff's
training is a crucial factor to undertaking efficient research. While over
601 of research staff in 1984 had post-graduate training to miasters levels
(see Table 2.1), a large number (near 301) had only a bachelor's degree with
no formal training in research methodology. More importantly, the number of
staff trained to PhD level was only some 300 (91), half of whom were in the
universities. Although ARP I, MART and TIPAN projects would help to nearly
double the number of PhDs, a large demand would still remain for staff with
PhD and post-doctoral training, particularly for staff who could lead and
guide research teams or groups.
2.23      kesearch/Extension Linkages.  A two-way flow of information between
researchers, extension staff, and farmers is important to ensure that farmers'
problems are communicated to researchers and tested, new technologies are
4/   See Annex 2 and Project Preparation Report, No. 165/87 CP-PAK 41, 18 Dec,
1987, Annex 2.



- 14 -
disseminated to farmers. The IDA-assisted extension projects (para 1.18) have
att.mpted to improve these linkages, mainly through promoting technical
committee meetings (which include research and extension staff), informal
contacts between the groups, and ad-hoc arrangements for cooperating in
undertaking some field trials. These arrangements have certainly improved
research/extension linkages, however, more needs to be done as on one hand
some pertinent research results are not getting to extension workers, and on
the otter, many researchers do not have a good understanding of farming
systems in the various agroecological zones, and the farmers' main
productivity problems.
2.24      AERUs established under ARP-I (para 1.19), the MART project, and
recently the two ongoing Bank-assisted extension projects (Crs. 1533 and
1762-PAK), have introduced, and are using and promoting, a methodology of
diagnostic studies using a farming systems perspective when identifying
on-farm research and extension programs. This approach should be expanded to
become the basis for formulating research in all agroecological zones, and by
using multidisciplinary teams of research and extension staff, it would
provide further opportunities and linkages for research and extension staff to
work together in identifying farmer constraints and formulating appropriate
research and extension programs.
F.  Restructuring Pakistan's Research Systems
2.25      The project would support Pakistan's development of a strong,
decentralized research system (viable organizations at both the federal and
provincial levels), which is necessary to adequately service Pakistan's large
area and varied agricultural geography. Pakistan needs strong provincial
systems capable of undertaking some applied and a wide range of adaptive
research to handle the varied local conditions, and a strong federal system,
capable of handling more basic research problems, to complement the provinces'
work and to coordinate Pakistan's overall agricultural research.
2.26      In guiding and coordinating overall research, PARC cannot, however,
make all the decisions needed in planning and selecting a national research
program, but must decentralize most of the decision making to the provinces
and to individual institutes and research teams. Central coordination must
come through PARC translating national development objectives into national
research priorities, with guidelines for expenditures on these priorities,
introducing standard procedures and formats for detailed research programming,
and ensuring that all groups monitor and evaluate their researchl.  The
provinces must identify provincial research priorities (taking account of
national priorities) and allocate resources between them, while individual
research institutes and research teams must identify the detailed long-term
and annual research programs.
2.27      To strengthen the provincial research services,  the 1988 Report of
the National Commission on Agriculture (NCA) has recommended that the
provincial research institutes be reorganized, giving them more autonomy in
staff recruitment and promotion and in financial management, and the necessary
mechanisms for planning and resource allocation. NCA suggested two
reorganization alternatives, namely: merging the provincial agricultural
research institutes with the agricultural universities; or establishing
semi-autonomous bodies along the lines of AEC or PARC. While GONWFP is



- 15 -
already merging its crops and livestock research institutes with AUP, Sind has
decided on the second alternative, and Punjab and Baluchistan have opted for a
third route, that is, establishing provincial agricultural research boards
which would have control over research resource allocation. For the most
efficient use of research resources, the ultimate goal should be the merger of
provincial research institutes with the agricultural universities. However,
establishing semi-autonomous research organizations, or research boards with
control over research resource allocation, can be considered steps on the way
to meeting this goal.
2.28      While ARP-I helped greatly to establish a viable federal research
system, this project concentrates on strengthening the provincial research
services. This, however, could take considerable time (10-15 years), and
could require future IDA assistance, particularly to complete and consolidate
institutional changes. Subject to GOP's plans, the next IDA intervention
could be in some three years to assist in financing the additional research
resources (training, vehicles, equipment, infrastructure) that the provincial
research master planning would identify (see paras 3.10 & 3.23).
G. Rationale for IDA Involvement
2.29      The project forms an integral part of the Bank Group's agricultural
sector development strategy, would expand the ongoing program of assistance to
agricultural research and extensior., and through this support investments in
irrigation rehabilitation, drainage, and agricultural resource protection and
management. It focuses on improving provincial research planning,
administration, and execution and, together with ARP I and future assistance
to research, is part of a long-term program to substantially improve
agricultural research throughout Pakistan. The strengthening of research and
extension is pivotal to improving agricultural productivity, output, and
income, expanding diversification, and increasing export earnings and import
substitution. IDA's involvement in the project will help ensure that GOP's
program to improve overall research capabilities moves ahead as quickly as
possible, provincial research administrations are reorganized, and a high-
priority research program emerges which will support GOT's investment in
agricultural extension. USAID, the only other donor likely to s-ipport this
sized program, is already assisting two large research projects (which the
proposed project would complement), and is unlikely to finance another in the
near future.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Project Objectives
3.01      The project's main objecttves are to:
(a) improve and strengthen research capabilities in the provinces;
(b) consolidate and complement the advances achieved under ARP I at the
federal level, that is, with PARC and NARC;



- 16 -
(c) support high priority research programs at the federal and
provincial levels; and
(d) improve and strengthen research/extension linkages.
B. Summary Description
3.02      The project has three main thrusts:  assistance for the provinces to
reorganize their research systems and improve research planning, resource
allocation, monitoring and evaluation; assistance for identified priority
research programs which would support the Seventh FYE development objectives;
and funds to support provincial priority research identified during the
preparation of Provincial Research Master Plans (PRMPs). The project would
also continue the upgrading of federal research systems commenced under ARP-I
through strengthening federal. research's planning, monitoring and evaluation,
and its capability to undertake priority research which would complement
provincial research. The project would expend some 86X of funds on provincial
research (601 going directly through the provincial government), and 141 on
federal research. It would include the following components:
Strengthening Provincial Research
(a) strengthening provincial research management and planning by:
(i) establishing new agricultural research boards in Punjab and
Baluchistan, and a semi-autonomous provincial research organization
in Sind (including secretariats and units for research planning,
monitoring and evaluation); (ii) preparing PRMPs for Punjab, Sind,
Baluchistan and NWFP; (iii) undertaking diagnostic studies of the
main agro-ecological zones to identify farmers' principal
productivity problems, and the needed research to solve them, and
(iv) providing additional equipment, operating funds, training, and
technical assistance;
(b)  developing provincial research programs and facilities by:
(i) upgrading and expanding research programs in horticulture,
livestock and soil science, and establishing research programs in
integrated pest management (IPM) through additional staff, minor
civil works, equipment, incremental operating funds, training and
technical assistance; and (ii) providing funds to support priority
research identified in PRMPs;
(c)  initiating or expanding through equipment, operating funds and
technical assistance:  (i) national/provincial coordinated research
programs in dairy ^attle cross-breeding, buffalo breeding, nutrition
and health, soil physics and soil fertility/soil chemistry; and
(ii) collaborative research programs with international institutes;
(d) expanding AERUs in Punjab, Sind, Baluchistan and NWFP, and
establishing an AERU in Azad Jammu Kashmir through civil works,
additional staff, equipment, operating funds, training and technical
assistance;



-17 -
(e)  expanding research facilities in Pakistan's high northern areas at
Jaglot (Gilgit district) and at Kaghan in NWPP through civil works,
staff, equipment and operating funds;
(f)  developing post-graduate teaching and training in social sciences at
the agricultural universities of Punjab and Sind; and
(g) conducting a mid-term review of project progress.
Strengthening Federal Research
(a) strengthening PARC's Social Science Division and Planning
Directorate, and establishing units for economic analysis of
research, monitoring and evaluation to improve PARC's capabilities
to define national research priorities and set the overall
parameters within which the provinces plan their research
(additional staff, equipment, operating funds, training and
rechnical assistance);
(b)  upgrading and expanding programs and facilities at NARC for more
basic research in horticulture, livestock, soil science and social
sciences, and establishing a research program in IPM (through
similar support as to provinces) to enable NARC more fully
complement provincial research programs, plus providing incremental
research funds co assist NARC implement its long-term research plan;
(c)  constructing at NARC a new crop sciences office block, a community
center, and about 5 km of link road from the livestock research
station to the main farm road.
C. Detailed Proiect Features
Reorganization and Strengthening of Research Administration and Management
(US$9.1 million)'
3.03      Provincial Research Reorganization.  Sind would reorganize its crop,
livestock and fisheries research activities to be administered as a whole, and
to provide for: (a) autonomy in staff recruiting; (b) promotion of
professional staff based on objective criteria of merit and research
achievement; (c) financial management along administrative lines followed by
universities and other autonomous bodies in Pakistan; (d) collegiate style of
management; and (e) planning, programming, resource allocation, monitoring,
and evaluation of research.  It would establish a semi-autonomous research
organization, to be called the Sind Agricultural Research Organization (SARO),
merging the research activities of the Sind Department of Agriculture (crops,
livestock and fisheries) by June 30, 1991. Annex 2 (Appendix 1) suggests a
structure for such a provincial research organization similar to that of PARC
and other semi-autonomous bodies in Pakistan. An assurance was obtained from
Sind that it would establish SARO by June 30. 1991.
a/   Base cost estimates, and include training and technical assistance.



- 18 -
3.04      As a first step in a longer reorganization program, Punjab and
Baluchistan would reorganize their agricultural research activities, providing
for: (a) improved staff recruiting and management; (b) promotion of
professional staff based on objective criteria of merit and research
achievement; and (c) planning, programming, resource allocation, monitoring,
and evaluation of research. Punjab and Bal-uchistan would change existing
Public Service operating regulations for agricultural research staff to:
(a) improve the recruit4.ng of research staff with regard to speed of
appointment, and weightage given to research experience and technical ability
in selecting applicants (b) increase the opportunities for promotion within a
researcher's area of expertise by increasing the number of higher research
positions within different specialties, and introducing floating posts awarded
on merit; and (c) give more weightage to merit and research achievement in
promotion of staff. Objective criteria would be introduced to measure merit
and research achievement. Outstanding merit could be recognized by promotion
to a higher position or by more than usual annual salary increments. To allow
an integrated approach to research planning and resource allocation, the two
provinces would establish Agricultural Research Boards', that is, Punjab
Agricultural Research Board (PARB) and Baluchistan Agricultural Research Board
(BARB), chaired by the provinces' Planning and Development Department. The
B3oards would have limited membership including the Secretaries of Agriculture,
Livestock, Forestry, Irrigation and Finance, Vice-Chancellor of the
Agiicultural University, Directors General of Research (Agriculture and
Livestock) and Extension, and the Chief of PARC's Planning Directorate. the
Boards would be responsible for: (a) identifying provincial research
priorities and broad thrusts (within national guidelines), and establishing
guidelines for allocating research funds to these priorities; (b) overall
productior and annually updating of PRMPs; (c) monitoring and evaluating
provincial research; and (d) monitoring and overall coordination of the
provinces' project implementation. The provincial Finance Departments would
directly fund the Research Boards ane all project funds would flow through the
Boards.  Annex 2 (Appendix 5) suggests TORs for the Boards.  The project would
provide an office block to accommodate PARB in Lahore. Assurances were
obtalnad from Punjab and Baluchistan that they would by June 30. 1'991:
Mi) change existing Public Service regulations to imRrove agricultural
research staff recruitment and provide for promotion of professional research
staff to be based on merit and research achievement as measured by objective
criteria: and (ii) establish the Agricultural Research Boards.
3.05      Provincial Planning. Programming. Honitoring and Evaluation.  The
project wculd strengthen research planning, programming, monitoring and
evaluation at the provinci.al and federal levels. In each province the projeLt
would provide small management support units (MSU) (two to six professional
staff) to advise on and assist with research planning, resource allocation,
programming, monitoring and internal evaluation. In Sind the unit would be
initially attached to DGAR, ARI, Tandojam. Following SARO's establishment,
the unit would become its secretariat. ln NWFP the unit would be attached to
the office of AUP's Director of Research, and would also serve as a
secretariat for AUP's Research Coordination Board.
6/   Punjab would reconstitute its existing Provincial Research Crordination
Board, while Baluchistan would establish a new entity.



- 19 -
3.06      Punjab would have separate MSUs for its Research Board and for the
Director Generals of Agricultur-1 and Livestock Research. The Research
Board's MSU would function as its Secretariat and would have wider functions
than the other two MSUs (see para 4.03). Baluchistan wculd establish an MSU
to serve as its Research Board's Secretariat. To establish and operate MSUs,
the project would provide funds for hiring incremental staff; trairing to
Masters degree level in planning, programming, monitoring, and evaluation; and
for computers, furniture and technical assistance. Annex 9 details the
staffing of all MSUs.
3.07      Assurances were obtained from GOProvinces that by December 31. 1990:
(a) Sind would establish an MSU initially to be attached to the office of DGAR
ARI Tandojam. and following SARO establishment, to become its secretariat:
(b) NWFP would establish MSU attached to the office of AUP's Director of
Research. which would also serve as a secretariat for A'JP's Research
Coordination Board: (c) Punjab would establish three separate MSUs to service
PARB. and the Director Generals of Agricultural and Livestock Research. and
(d) Baluchistan would establish an MSU to service BARB.
3.08      Federal Planning. Programming. Monitoring and Evaluation.  At the
federal level PARC would amalgamate and reorganize its present Planning
Directorate and Monitoring and Evaluation Cell into an expanded and
strengthened Planning Directorate. The Directorate would have four functions,
that is, planning, project implementation, monitoring and evaluation, and
inventory (see Chart I). Separate units within the Directorate would be
responsible for each function. While three of the units are already
established, the Inventory Unit would be a new unit, responsible to develop
and maintain a data base on all agricultural research personnel in the
country, and total resource allocation to research. This would be essential
information for long-term planning and PARC's coordination of national
agricultural research. The Planning Unit, among other things, would assist in
preparing and updating a National Master Agricultural Research Plan (NMARP).
Annex 2 (paras 49-51) details the responsibilities of the four units.
3.09      To enable the proposed new Planning Directorate undertake its
expanded functions, the project would provide additional professional and
support staff, computers, office furniture, vehicles, training, and technical
assistance. Annex 2 lists terms of reference (TORs) for the technical
assistance. An assurance was obtained from GOP that PARC would establish its
new Planning Directorate by December 31. 1990.
3.10      Provincial Research Master Plans.  Punjab, Sind, Baluchistan and
NWFP would each prepare an overall PRMP based on the provincial government's
delineation of its crop, livestock, fishery and forestry priorities, and
consistent with national goals and priorities. Such PRMPs would determine
programs, manpower requirements, training needs, facilities, equipment,
operation and maintenance, and capital and recurrent budgets on a five year
basis, plus an additional indicative five years. Such planning would be an
ongoing process in AUP, SARO, PARB and BARBs' secretariats, and would be
updated annually. These plans would only include provincially-funded
research. Annex 2 (Appendix 2) gives TORs for preparation of PRMPs.
3.11      The provinces would complete their first PRMP in about two years (by
August 31, 1992).  The preparation of the first PRMPs would require



- 20 -
considerable time ard effort including establishing functional provincial
planning, programming, monitoring and evaluation. Accordingly, the project
would provide internationally and locally recruited technical assistance to
assist provincial staff prepare PRMPs. Annex 2 (Appendix 3) gives TORs for
this technical assistance.
3.12      Standardization.  Since the project would introduce new
methodologies at the national and provincial levels in planning, programming,
monitoring, evaluation, and the preparation of master research plans, the
techniques used should be standardized. PARC would coordinate such activities
by holding periodic meetings of concerned staff and consultants to ensure a
degree of uniformity in approach. These meetings should be at least once
every three months during ARP-II's first two years, and rotated around the
provinces. An assurance was obtained from GOP that during kRKP pRXearation
PARC and the nrovinces would meet Reriodically to ensure that standard
technigues are adopted in PRMF or eRaration. During ARP II's first two years
these meetings would be every three months.
3.13      Diagnostic Surveys.  To introduce a farming systems perspective into
research planning and programming, pry mote on-farm research, and improve
research/extension linkages, the project would support multidisciplinary teams
of research and extension staff undertaking diagnostic surveys and reviewing
the farming systems and practices within each province's various
agroecological zones. This work would include collection of secondary data,
then exploratory and verification surveys which would identify farmers'
productivity and socio-economic constraints, and assist scientists to develop
appropriate research programs to solve farmers' priority productivity
problems. Diagnostic surveys would be an important part of the research
planning process and formulating of research programs at the individual or
research team level. In Punjab, Sind, and Baluchistan the main crops research
institute (in NWFP, the agricultural university) would appoint a social
scientist as an adaptive research coordinator. The coordinator would arrange
for small multidisciplinary teams of technical scientists (including livestock
researchers) extension staff from the extension adaptive research farms and
social scientists (from AERUs) to undertake diagnostic surveys, ensure that
all agroecological zones or districts of a province were covered by diagnostic
studies, and the results of such studies published and disseminated. He would
also act as the contact point for this work between all groups involved. To
enable ARIs to undertake this work, the project would provide additional
professional and support staff, computers, office furniture, vehicles,
operating costs and technical assistance. Annex 8 gives further details
concerning this program.
Developing Priority Research Programs (Total US$43.4 million)7
3.14      The project would support and develop research programs in
horticulture, livestock, soil science, IPM, and social sciences. PARC and NCA
have identified these as priority research areas to support the national
development objectives of the Seventh FYP. On average project expenditures
for the programs would cover training (44X), incremental operating funds
7/   Excluding additional priority research identified in PRNPs, and other
research in NARC's long-term research plan (see paras 3.24-3.25).



- 21 -
(18%), equipment and vehicles (171), incremental salaries (10), technical
assistance (9X), and civil works (2X). Annexes 3 through 7 give program
details, Annex 12 lists participating research institutes and research
programs to be funded, while the Project Implementation Volume lists all
expenditures by individual research stations.
3.15      Horticulture (US$9.2 million).  Annex 3 details the horticultural
research programs that NARC and the four provinces would develop under the
project. NARC would become a center of excellence for horticultural research
by establishing facilities for biotechnology and post-harvest horticulture to
conduct basic research in maintaining fruits, vegetables and ornamentals in
good cordition between producers and the ultimate consumers. For greater
effectiveness, it would reorganize its horticultural research staff into three
sections (hor-iculture, biotechnology, and post-harvest physiology). PARC
would have the role of coordinating horticultural research throughout
Pakistan. It would establish a horticultural coordinator at PARC, who working
through key contact people in the provinces, would plan, organize and review
discussion groups, meetings and conferences. He would also collect data,
monitor horticultural research p.ograms and disseminate results and
information. Joint projects would be developed on the provincial and national
levels and regular conferences held to discuss programs and results in similar
research.
3.16      In Punjab, research programs would concentrate on citrus production,
while in Sind, research would focus mainly on tropical fruits (mango, banana,
chiku, papaya) and vegetables. NWFP would concentrate on deciduous fruit and
vegetables, and Baluchistan on vegetables and ornamentals. The project would
provide funds for AUF and SAU to undertake research on mango and citrus. As
the universities are still preparing their research programs, they would
submit their research proposals to IDA for review and approval before
proceeding with them. To support these programs, the project would provide
additional staff, equipment, vehicles, buildings, operating funds, technical
assistance and training. Annex 3 (Appendix 1) gives TORs for technical
assistance.
3.17      Livestock (US$8.8 million).  Livestock research programs would
concentrate on animal production and nutrition. The project would support two
livestock NCRPs, one for dairy cattle cross-breeding which PARC would
initiate, and the other an expansion of the ongoing program for buffalo.
However, before the first program goes ahead, government policies on dairy
cattle cross-breeding must be reviewed, and the project would provide funds
for an internationally recruited expert to undertake this review. The present
buffalo reproduction NCRP would be expanded to include research not only on
reproductive physiology but also on buffalo nutrition, breeding, and health.
Annex 4 describes individual livestock research programs in more detail, while
its Appendix 1 lists TORs for livestock technical assistance.
3.18      Soil Science (US$7.1 million).  The soil science component is
designed to strengthen existing provincial research in soil fertility/soil
chemistry, and add a new capability to do research on soil and crop
environment physics. The project would establish soil physics units as part
of the Soil Section of NARC and each province's major ARI (with two units in
Punjab), and initiate NCRPs in soil physics and soil fertility/soil chemistry.
It would also support the Pakistan Council of Research in Water Resources



-22-
(PCRWR) undertake research on water use efficiency at farm level. Annex 5
describes individual soil science programs in more detail, while its Appendix
2 contains TORs for soil science technical assistance.
3.19      Integrated Pest Management (US$8.1 million).  The project would
establish and support IPM research at the federal and provincial levels, and
would establish a coordinated program of planned research and training. It
would fund scientists at NARC and th_ provincial ARIs to develop IPM
strategies for various crops and areas, particularly for the cotton crop, the
largest user of pesticides. Because of the importance of coordinating the
work, PARC would appoint and provide a National IPM network coordinator and a
small coordinatUon staff trained in entomology and pathology . These would be
based at PARC headquarters and would be the nerwcrk's facilitators, plann'ers
and advisors.. While having no executive authority over the network, they
would provide leadership and administration for organizing workshops and
seminars, coordinating techrLical assistance and so on. Annex 6 describes the
component in detail.
3.20      Social Science (USSIO.2 million):  Agricultural Economic Research
Units. The project would continue to support and further strergthen the five
existing AERUs, and would establish a new AERU in Azad Jammu Kashmir. The
AERUs have added a much needed economic dimension to research in Pakistan.
They would assist in research planning, design of experiments, analysis of
experimenttal results, and the identification of farmers' problems amenable to
research. AERU staff would play a key role in the diagnostic studies of
agroecological zones (para 3.13), and would integrate their work with
technical scientists at every opportunity. While PARC presently finances and
supervises all AERUs, at ARP-II's completion the provinces would decide
whether to assimilate the AERU in their province into their provincial
research system. To strengthen AERUs and enable them to undertake their work
programs, the project would provide funds for staff, houses, vehicles,
computers, office equipment, training, and technical assistance. Annex 7
gives more details concerning the social science programs.
3.21      Social Science:  PARC's Social Science Division.  The project would
strengthen PARC's Social Science Division to improve its supervising of AERUs
and other social science research, and enable it to provide PARC's Planning
Directorate economic, social and political analyses of research investments.
The project would provide the Division additional staff, training, and
technical assistance, and it would be responsible for strengthening and
developing AERUs, including ensuring additional staff are recruited,
developing work programs, and obtaining technical assistance.
3.22      Social Science:  Agricultural Universities' Social Science
Faculties. To provide well-trained staff in agricultural economics and rural
sociology, the project would strengthen the Faculty of Agricultural Economics
and Rural Sociology at AUF, and the Faculty of Agricultural Social Sciences at
SAU. To upgrade these faculties, each university would enter into a twinning
arrangement with an eminent, appropriate overseas university, which would
provide assistance such as, among other things, teaching inputs, staff
training including MSc, PhD and post-doctoral studies, collaborative research,
and technical assistance for establishing curricula and research programs.
The exact form and content of these twinning arrangements is not presently
specified, but would be negoJiated by each university before the end of the



-  3
project's first year. The project would provide a lump sum, through PARC, for
each university to finance the twinning arrangement, plus some additional
staff positions, equipment, vehicles, textbooks and journals, and travel by
the university Vice-Chancellors, or Deans of the Social Science Faculties, to
v4sit overseas universities short-listed for the proposed twinning
arrangement. The project would also provide funds through PARC for research
in social sciences (AUF, Rs 6.8 million; SAU, RS 4.5 million). PARC and the
universities would enter into a memorandum of understanding (MOU) concerning
the supply of funds for the twinning arrangement and for social science
research. Regarding social science research, the MOU would also cover such
points as research priorities, submission of research proposals, monitoring,
evaluation, and publication of results. Assurances were obtained from GOP
that AVF and SATJ would each enter into and sign a twinning agreement with an
overseas university concerning assistance for their social science faculties
by June 30. 1991. and would enter into a MOU with PARC concerning funds for
the twinning arrangement and social science research by December 31. 1990.
3.23      Additional Provincial Priority Research.  While at the national
level PARC has identified broad priority areas for research to support the
Seventh FYP development objectives, at the provincial level the provinces
still have to state their priorities within the national parameters. This
would lead to additional research required other than that already listed in
the five priority research programs. The preparation of PRMPs would identify
this additional research, and to ensure work in these areas is not constrainrd
by insufficient research funds, the project would provide each province a lump
sum to be allocated to this work. Project funds allocated to each province
are Rs 115.2 million for Punjab, Rs 43.2 million for Sind, Rs 9.1 million of
Baluchistan, and Rs 24.3 million for NWFP. Provinces could use these funds to
finance incremental direct operational costs of research (such as materials,
fuel, labor, communications), and some capital expenditures (maximum 20%)
assoriatee with specific subprojects. Capital expenditures could only be for
items such as laboratory equipment, vehicles and livestock. Institute
overhead costs such as staff salaries, utilities, and building maintenance,
and building construction costs, would not be eligible for financing. PRMP
preparation would determine appropriate levels of recurrent and capital
research expenditures. Assurances were obtained from GOProvinces that they
would use these additional research funds only for research identified and
specified in PRMPs as apnroved by IDA.
3.24      The project would also provide Rs 12.4 million to assist PARC
implement long-term research plans developed for NARC.8 NARC could only use
these funds to finance the incremental direct operating costs and some capital
costs (as defined in para 3.24) of research listed in its long-term research
plan.
jB/ As contained in the Research Master Plan for the National Agricultural

This master plan would be part of the overall NMARP.
