Collaborative Research (US$0.5 million)
3.25      Collaboradive Research Program.  PARC would operate a collaborative
research program between eminent overseas research institutes and federal or
provincial research institutes doing similar work. The program would
facilitate the international exchange of information and material, give
impetus to Pakistan's research programs, train Pakistani research scientists,
and allow them to keep up-to-date in advancing fields of knowledge. To
support the program, the project would finance transport, accommodation and
other costs associated with visits of Pakistani scientists to overseas
research institutions, and of foreign scientists to Pakistan; and research on
Pakistani problems contracted to foreign institutes. To be eligible for the
program, a local institute must be working on either national or proviacial
priority research, and must have a nucleus of well trained staff who would
benefit from the program. PARC would operate the program following procedures
for selecting, approving, monitoring, accounting and auditing individual
collaborative research programs similar to those developed for contract
research under ARP I. Follow.ng PARC's approval of a proposed collaborative
research program the local and international institutes or agencies would
enter into an agreement with terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA. At
least 601 of the funds would be allocated to collaborative research in which
the provinces would participate. Annex 11 specifies the guidelines for using
collaborative research funds.
Expansion of PARC/NARC Research Facilities (US$4.0 million)
3.26      NARC Infrastructure.  To provide suitable office accommodation for
its Plant Sciences Division, under the project, at its Islamabad campus NARC
would construct a new crop sciences building (some 2,415 in) with offices for
about 60 staff. As well, for all staff stationed on its campus, NARC would
construct a community center (about 1,175 nR) in the basement area of a new
cafeteria it is constructing with USAID assistance. To link its new livestock
farm center (conszructed under ARP I) to the main farm road, NARC would build
about 5 km of connecting road.
3.27      High Northern Areas Research.  To initiate and expand research
programs in the high northern areas and undertake research for farming
communities presently not serviced by provincial research systems, PARC would
expand its facilities and staff at Jaglot in the federally administered
northern areas (Gilgit district) and Kaghan in NWFP. At Jaglot (annual
average rainfalls from 100 to 500 mm) PARC, through its Agricultural Research
Institute for the Northern Areas (ARINA), would construct offices,
laboratories and housing, purchase equipment and vehicles, and hire technical
and support staff as a first stage to undertaking multidisciplinary research
in a variety of programs. These include land and soil management,
horticulture, crop, livestock and fisheries production. In formulating its
research programs, ARINA should coordinate closely with the Aga Khan Rural
Support Program, which, among other things, is assisting to develop
agricultural production in the Gilgit, Skardu and Chitral areas. At Kaghan
(average rainfall in excess of 1,500 mm) PIARC would expand its hill station,
-Ahich cereal breeders currently use during summer to raise two crops in one
year. The project would provide additional offices, laboratory, hostel
accommodation, equipment, vehicles, farm development, and operational costs.
Cereal breeders would expand their work, and station staff would develop



- 25 -
research programs for the surrounding farming communities. The station would
function as a department or division of NARC, and for six months of the year
when it is not accessible because of snow, staff would return to existing
offices at Abbottabad or NARC.
Training (U'S$20.2 million)
3.28      Trainirng is a key component of the project.  To considerably
increase the number of staff trained in research methodology and research
program leadership, and to introduce new ideas and concepts to Pakistani
researchers, the project would provide local and overseas long-term training
in MSc and PhD studies, and various modes of short-term training, such as
post-doctoral studies, refres'ier courses, or in-service training programs
conducted by local staff and overseas lecturers. Overseas PhD training is
necessary because of the dearth of staff well trained in research methodology
and research team leadership, and the non-availability of suitable programs at
Pakistani agricultural universities. A future project, planned to strengthen
higher level agricultural education is expected to rectify this situation.
Each province and PARC has specified funds for long- and short-term training,
which are not interchangeable between the provinces or PARC. Except in food
crop plant breeding, all long-term PhD training would be done overseas, and
this training would be spread between eminent universities throughout the
world. If necessary, MSc and PhD candidates would be given in-country
training in English language, mathematics or other subjects considered
necessary before proceeding overseas.  PhD candidates would take fov  years on
average to complete their studies, and during this period would return to
Pakistan to do their thesis research. The project would provide funds for the
students' research supervisor to visit them while they are in Pakistan. All
training would be either in research planning and management or in the
priority programs of horticulture, livestock, soil science, IPM or social
sciences. It would support actual work which the project would finance, and
is based on reviews of skills required. Annexes 3 through 7 and the Project
Implementation Volume give details on long-term training, while Annex 10,
Table 1 lists the aggregate training program by research service and area of
training.
Technical Assistance (USt7.3 million)
3.29      To assist PARC, other federal institutes, and the provinces
implement project works and programs, the project would provide
internationally and locally recruited consultant services to assist:
(a) assessment of research investment priorities; (b) research monitoring and
evaluation; (c) preparation of PRMPs; (d) use of program budgeting in research
planning; (e) diagnostic studies of agroecological zones; and (f) research
programs in horticulture, livestock, soil science, IPM, and social sciences.
Annex 10, Table 2 lists the consultant months allocated to each of these
items, while Annexes 2 through 6 list consultant tasks and TORs. By

of Pakistan would internationally recruit and sign a contract for engaging a
consultant to assist in computerizing soil survey data by December 31, 1990.
PARC would locally recruit consultants to assist with the design and
construction of the crop sciences building and other implementation matters as

Imolementation Schedule
3.30      GOP and GOProvinces would implement the project during the seven
years from July, 1990, to June, 1997. Annex 13 shows the start of most major

D. Cost Estima'es
3.31      The total estimated project cost is US$81.9 million, of which
US$51.8 million (63X) is the foreign exchange component. Project costs are
expressed in June 1990 prices, and include duties and taxes estimated at
US$6.9 million. Base cost estimates were derived from detailed cost analyses
of recent PARC training programs, federal and provincial research programs,
and office, laboratory and house construction contracts in the project areas.
Contract quantities for building construction are based on standard GOP or
GOProvince designs. Consultant services cost are based on recent contracts
for similar services in Pakistan. The cost estimates include allowances for
physical contingencies of 15X for civil works and 10% for machinery,
equipment, training and technical assistance. Additionally, the cost
estimates include allowances for price escalations during implementation (16%
of base cost plus physical contingencies) based on IDA's estimated domestic
and foreign inflation rates as shown in Table 3.1.

E.   Financing
3.33          Table 3.3 shows the proposed project financing plan.  The proposed
IDA credit of SDR 44.4 million (US$57.3 million) would finance 70X of project
costs, including 100X of foreign exchange costs and 181 of local costs.  GOP
and GOProvinces would provide the balance of project funds, Rs 531.4 million
(US$24.6 million) to the project implementing agencies out of annual budget
appropriations. Funds for provincial expenditures will flow from the Ministry
of Finance directly to the provinces as annual development plan funds. Other
funds for strengthening provincial research would flow from MOF through PARC
to the concerned institute or AUF or SAU. Excluding duties end taxes, IDA
would finance 76X of project costs. Full aDproval by the Executive Committee
of the National Economic Council (ECNEC) of the project's Planning Commission
Form No. 1 (PC-1} is a condition of Credit effectiveness.

3.35      Civil Works (USS4.8 million).  The Crop Sciences Offices (US$0.9
million) would be constructed following international competitive bidding
(ICB) in accordance with IDA guidelines.  Local contractors participatir.g in
this ICB procurement would have a 7-1/2% preference. All other project
buildings, however, would be small and dispersed geographically throughout
Pakistan. They would consist mainly of standard design housing, offices and
laboratories, and would not be of interest to international contractors.
These works (US$3.9 million) would be undertaken following local competitive
bidding (LCB) procedures. PARC and the provinces' LCB procedures are



- 29 -
generally consistent with the need for economy and efficiency in the execution
of the project. There are, however, a few procedures which are inconsistent
with Bank procurement guidelines and others which require clarification.
During negotiations, agreement was reached regarding the adoption of
procedures acceptable to IDA.
3.36      Motorcycles. Machinery. Equipment. Office Furniture. Books and
Journals (USS9.3 Million). Farm, laboratory and office equipment, farm
machinery, motorcycles, and office furniture costing US$5.1 million would be
bulked into contracts of US$50,000 equivalent or more, and procured following
IDA ICB guidelines. A preference limited to 15% of the c.i.f. bid price or
the actual customs duty applicable to non-exempt importers, whichever is
lower, would be extended to qualified local manufacturers in the evaluation of
bids. However, as these goods, equipment and materials would be required in
various amounts, at different times, and at many different sites in the four
provinces, items which cannot be bulked into contracts of US$50,000, and
totalling about US$3.2 million, could be locally or internationally procured
through shopping involving at least three price quotations. Proprietary spare
parts, scientific journals and textbooks (US$1.0 million) may be purchased
directly from the suppliers. The total value of items procured through
shopping, and by direct purchase, would be limited to an aggregate US$4.2
million. Vehicles (US$3.2 million), would be reserved procurement and,
therefore, not reimbursable under the Credit. Vehicle costs have been
excluded from project costs when establishing the project Credit.
3.37      Consultant services (US$8.7 million) would be procured according to
IDA guidelines, and overseas and local training arrangements (US$27.0 million)
would be subject to IDA approval. Collaborative research expenditures,
comprising travel, accommodation, materials and supplies, would follow normal
government practices under agreements subject to IDA approval. Project
expenditures on incremental research operating costs (US$19.3 million, which
includes fuel, fertilizer, seed, labor, repairs and maintenance to vehicles
and machinery and so on in small purchases for 40 or more research stations
throughout Pakistan) would be handled following normal government practices.
3.38      Contracts for US$50,000 and US$70,000 equivalent or more for civil
works and machinery/equipment, respectively, would require IDA review of
documentation before tendering and award, and this would cover 45% of the
value of works, and 55% of equipment. Other contracts would be subject to
selective post-award review.
G. Disbursements
3.39      The Credit would be disbursed over eight years, and would be
completed by June 30, 1998. The proposed disbursement schedule (Annex 1,
Table 3) is slightly longer than the actual disbursement profiles for ARP-I
(Credit 1158-PAK) and the first two Extension and Adaptive Research projects
(Crs. 813/922-PAK), which were disbursed over approximately seven years.
Seven years is also the length of the standard disbursement profile for all
Pakistani agricultural projects. The project disbursement period has been
slightly extended over the standard profile to allow additional time for long-
term training.

H. Accounts and Audits
3.43      Implementing agencies would maintain separate project accounts which
independent auditors, acceptable to IDA and using procedures satisfactory to
IDA, would audit annually. The auditors would issue separate opinions on
disbursements from the special accounts and SOEs and supporting documents used
as the basis for disbursements from the Credit. Implementing agencies would
provide their auditors unaudited project accounts within three months of the
fiscal year's end (September 30), and each would submit an audit report of
project accounts to IDA within six months (December 31). Assura.ices were
obtained from GOP and GOProvinces that implementing agencies would follow
these auditing Dractices.

IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEKENT
A. Provincial Implementation
4.01      At the provincial level responsibility for implementing the project
would be as follows:
(a)  AUP in NWFP, with AUP's Director of Research the provincial project
manager responsible for planning, guiding, coordinating and
supervising project activities;
(b)  initially the Agricultural Department in Sind, then SARO, following
its establishment, with DGAR ARI Tandojam as the provincial project
manager and then the Clairman SARO; and
(c)  the Agricultural Departments in conjunction with the Livestock
Departments in Punjab and Baluchistan, with DGAR ARI Faisalabad and
Director of Research ARI Sariab as the provincial project managers.
4.02      Research Planning.  AtTP's Research Coordination Board, SARO, PARB
and BARB would be responsible for defining each respective province's broad
research priorities and guidelines for the allocation of resources to these
priorities. They would also establish, in conjunction with PARC (para 3.12)
the criteria and procedures for long and short-term research planning. Within
each province individual research divisions or institutes would determine the
su'i-allocations to high priority technical research thrusts, and develop long-
term research plans, while at the research program level individual
researchers or research teams would prepare their atunual work programs
determining the most relevant work to be done with available manpower and
financial resources.
4.03      Provincial Secretariats/MSUs.  The four Secretariats established
under the project to support SARO and the three Research Boards (Punjab,
Baluchistan and NWFP, see paras 3.05-3.07) would be responsible for monitoring
and coordinating project implementation within the respective provinces, and
for preparing disbursement applications and half-yearly reports on project
progress which they would send to IDA through PARC's project implementation
unit. Each Secretariat would also advise its Board or SARO on provincial
research priorities and the allocation of resources to these priorities;
oversee and assist the preparacion of PRMPs (if necessary, assisting research
institutes undertake long- and short-term research plarning); establish and
maintain registers of all provincial research staff and p..ojects; and monitor
and externally evaluate provincial agricultural research.
4.04      In Punjab, MSUs attached to the Directors-General of Agricultural
and Livestock Research would advise and assist with annual ani long-term
research planning, and internal monitoring of research programs within the
Institutes.  In all the provinces, to better identify resources needed in
research programs, the project would introduce program budgeting into
researchers' preparation of estimated research program costs.
4.05      Provincial Research Master Plan Preparation.  Each of the four
provincial project managers would form a Research Working Group (headed by a
staff member from the respective Secretariats), and Subject Matter Subgroups,



- 32 -
to prepare PRMPs. Senior research staff would form the -bgroups, with major
inputs from research directors and internationally and locally-recruited
consultants. The subgroups would review in detail and prepare research
programs and projects, manpower training requirements, staff incentives
improvements, research facilities and equipment needs, improvements to
effective linkages with extension and internal and external research
o;ganizations, improvements to research and management information systems,
and capital and operating budgetary requirements. In particular, the
subgroups would review unfilled, sanctioned research staff positions, which if
not in priority research areas, should be transferred to areas where positions
are needed, or cancelled before new staff positions are created.  The first
PRMPs should be completed by August 31, 1992, and then AUP, SARO, PARB and
BARB would update PRMPs every year. To ensure the provinces complete the work
on time, and then annually updated PRMPs, assurances were obtained from
GOProvinces that they would: (a) annoint the working garties by October 31.
1990: (b) submit the completed PRMP to IDA for review by August 31. 1992:
(c) submit the updated PRMP to IDA for review by March 31 each year: and
(d) annually provide sufficient capital and ogerating and maintenance-funds to
successfully implement PRMPs.
4.06      Provincial Priority Research Funds.  In accordance with provincial
research priorities identified in PRMPs, AUP, SARO, PARB and BARB through
their Executive Committee, or a special committee, would allocate the
additional provincial priority research funds unich the project would provide.
These funds would be used mainly for incremental operating expenditures and
would either augment operating funds the provinces are already providing for
existing programs, or allow the province to initiate new research programs,
and would allow researchers' time and creativity to be utilized to the
maximum. The funds would only be allocated to specific research proposals or
projects which have been fully justified, prepared, budgetted and included in
the province's PRMP (para 3.23).
4.07      Each of the provinces would implement any civil works construction
through its provincial Communications and Works Departments (C&W). As civil
works are minimal, C&W Departments would be able to handle this work.
4.08      Agriculttirai Universities.  PARC would disburse funds to AUF and SAU
to enable them to undertake their project activities. The AUF and SAU
Directors of Research would be responsible for preparing annual, overall work
plans and budgets covering each universities' annual project implementation
program, and would meet and discuss these with PARC each year to ensure PARC
includes the necessary funds in its annual budget.
B. Federal Implementation
4.09      At the federal level PARC would have overall responsibility for
executing the project. PARC's institutes, NARC, AZRI, and ARINA would be
responsible for developing and implementing individual research programs and
activities. PARC would also administer funds provided for administration of
nationally coordinated research programs, collaborative research, university
research, the mid-term review and development of AERUs. A project
implementation unit (PIU), established under ARP I, would be responsible for
coordinating PARC's and its institutes activities.



- 33 -
4.10      Project Implementation.  The Five Members of PARC's Executive Board
would plan, coordinate, supervise and g-_ide project activities within the
relevant sections of PARC, and at the institute level the institute directors
and program leaders would implement project programs and activities. However,
PIU would be responsible for the day-to-day coordination of project
activities. This unit has now become part of PARC's Planning Directorate, and
expanded to include five sciertific level officers (originally three) plus
support staff. PIU would monitor and review project progress, prepare
quarterly reports (including collating those of the province) for IDA, liaise
with the provinces and agricultural universities on matters such as training,
collaborative, nationally coordinated, and university research, and prepare
federal disbursement applications, while collating those of the provinces, for
sending to IDA. Within six months of Credit completion PIU would prepare a
completion report, acceptable to IDA, reporting on all aspects of project
implementation.
4.11      Federal Research Planning.  PARC's Planning Directorate would be
responsible for advising PARC's Board of Governors on the determ!'.ation of
broad national research priorities and the allocation of research resources to
these priorities. The Social Sciences Division would assist the Planning
Directorate on the economic, social, and political aspects of determining
national research priorities (see para 3.21). It is important that PARC makes
an early statement on broad national research priorities and establishes
guidelines for the allocation of resources to these priorities to allow
long-term research planning to begin (see PPR'1, Annex 2, pp 17-20). An
assurance was obtained that PARC would define broad national research
priorities and establish guidelines for the allocation of resources to these
Driorities by November 30. 1990.
4.12      In preparing the National Master Agricultural Research Plan, the
Planning Unit of the Planning Directorate (para 3.08) would arrange for PARC
institutes to prepare their long-term plans. It would then collate these with
the provincially produced PRMPs to produce the overall master research plan.
As other federal ministries or agencies doing agricultural research presently
do not plan long-term, it is impossible to include their proposed programs in
the national master plan. However, PARC and the provinces, by undertaking
long-term planning, would be able to discuss their future work programs with
these other agencies art, in this way, encourage complementary approaches by
all agencies and reduce duplication of work. An assurance was obtained from
GOP that it would provide adeguate capital and operating funds to PARC to
allow it and its institutes to successfully implement their long-term research
Rlans as contained in NMARP.
4.13      The Planning Directorate's Monitoring and Evaluation Unit would
establish data processing and appropriate procedures to monitor PARC projects,
establish evaluation methods and procedures, assist in the evaluations of
other research institutions and, if requested, assist in -.stablishing
monitoring units and internal evaluation procedures at other research
institutions.
IO/  Project Preparation Report, op. cit.



- 34 -
4.14      Civil Works and Procurement.  PARC's Directorate of Works and
Procurement Section would undertake all civil works and equipment procurement
respectively at the federal level. ARP-I strengthened both these groups, and
they are suitably staffed to undertake this work.
C. Training
4.15      Project preparation work has already broadly identified training
requirements in research planning, programming, resource allocation,
monitoring, and evaluation, and in the priority areas of horticulture,
livestock, soil science, IPM, and social sciences. Project management would
firm-up training activities, particularly for short-term training, during
project implementation. While PRMP preparation and long-term research
planning at the federal level would identify other training needs, this would
have to be financed from other sources, possibly an ARP-III.
4.16      Each province would be responsible for its own training program,
while PARC would be responsible for training at the federal level. Besides
selecting those with high academic qualifications for PhD studies, to ensure
all PhD trainiag slots are filled, the provinces would also consider those
with an acceptable academic career and proven research ability (for example,
published research papers, influential reports, outstanding work on a
program).  However, seniority or length of time in a department would not be a
selection criteria. The provinces would arrange and implement any in-country
training their candidates require before overseas universities would accept
them, while Economic Affairs Division, GOP, would clear all successful
provincial candidates through federal procedures necessary before leaving
Pakistan. An internationally recruited firm would then place and administer
provincial PhD candidates in overseas universities. To ensure cohesion
between the technical assistance and training programs, a province would use
the same firm to supply services in both areas. This firm could also supply
short-term training services as needed. NWFP, which already has a successful
overseas long-term training program underway under the USAID assisted TIPAN
project, would expand this program using its present contractor, the
University of Illinois. PARC, which has developed a capacity to implement
overseas training programs under ARP-I and MART, would only require limited
technical assistance to place and administer federal PhD candidates overseas.
To ensure that the long-term training commenced early in the project's life,
PARC and the provinces would identify the first group of trainees by March 31,
1991.
D. Project Assessment. Mid-Term Review and Supervision
4.17      Project Assessment.  Because of the project's complexity, and the
large number of agencies involved in project execution, PARC and the provinces
would each, jointly with IDA, assess implementation of its c-mponent towards
the end of the first two implementation years (this is expected to be around
October/November 1991 and 1992, respectively). The assessment would be done
during a supervision mission, and PARC and the provinces would prepare short
reports before the supervision listing implemer.cation constraints and
shortfalls. Following field visits, PARC, provincial and IDA mission members'
would discuss their findings, and issue a short report reviewing project
progress and any needed changes in implementation or project design, which
would be adopted.



- 35 -
4.18      PARC, assisted by internationally recruited consultants, would
review the project's progress no later than 42 months after Credit
effectivene_s (around January 31, 1994). The review would concentrate on:
(a) provincial research reorganization; (b) PRMP's recommendations and
allocation of incremental operating funds; (c) additional research resource
needs of provinces identified by PRMPs; (d) strengthening of PARC to plan,
coordinate, monitor and evaluate research; (e) progress in the priority
research programs funded by the project; and (f) progress in training
programs. PARC would obtain IDA's approval of the review team's TORs and
composition by June 30, 1993. Based on the review's results, GOP, GOProvinces
and IDA would agree on, and implement, any changes needed to improve the
project's impact. To support the review, the project would provide technical
assistance (7 months) and funds to produce and publish the review's report.
Assurances were obtained from GOP that: (a) PARC and the provinces with IDA
would iointly assess Rroiect implementation towards the end of the project's
first two implementation years. thac is. not later than November 30. 1991 and
1992. and would groduce a report reviewing project progress and listing needed
changes in implementation and design. which subsequently would be adopted: and
(b) PARC. assisted by internationally recruited consultants with
gualifications and under conditions acceRtable to IDA. would undertake a mid-
term review of the Rroiect no later than January 31. 1994. in accordance with
terms of reference which would be agreed by June .0. 1993. and would implement
agreed changes to proiect design or implementation.
4.19      Supervision.  The project would need a heavy annual supervision
input, particularly during the first two years when the provinces are
reorganizing their research administration, establishing MSUs, and preparing
PRMPs. Annex 14 contains a proposed supervision plan for the project, which
details the required annual staff inputs, and expected skill requirements
needed on supervision missions. During the project's initial three years
approximately 15 staff weeks per year would be required for supervision. This
requirement should fall to 13 staff weeks in FY94, and then settle at 11 staff
weeks per year for the remaining three years. During supervision IDA staff
should pay particular attention to research administration reorganization,
PRMP preparation, procurement of technical assistance, and commeacement and
implementation of the long and short-term training programs.
V. BENErITS. JUSTIFICATIONS AND RISKS
A. Benefits and Justification
5.01      The project would considerably improve the management and
organization of Pakistan's provincial and federal agricultural research
systems, leading to more efficient and effective utilization of resources
allocated to research. Under the project, GOP and COProvinces would put in
place new procedures and structures to determine research priorities, and to
allocate resources among and within programs. Additional major project
benefits .?ould be to enhance the quality of experimental work through staff
training and creating a more conducive environment for research; sharpen the
focus on farmers' problems and improve the dissemination of results through
better research/extension linkages; and through supplying additional
resources, expand federal and provincial agencies' capability to undertake



- 36 -
research in priority areas. The project would complement IDA-assisted
Agricultural Extension projects in Punjab, Sind and Baluchistan; and support
GOP's policy of increasing agricultural yields and diversification, orienting
research towards small farmers, and ensuring a productive and sustainable use
of land and water resources.
5.02      The technological improvements, the rate and extent of their
adoption by farmers, and the productivity and output gains due to their
application, cannot be accurately predicted and are likely to vary
significantly across agro-ecological zones. Moreover, future gains in
productivity would be attributable not merely to the adoption of technological
innovations, but to the combined impact of various support services (input
supply, extension services and so on) as well as to that of the general
macroeconomic environment. It is therefore planned that the federal research
monitoring and evaluation unit would develop a reliable data base for
generating an indicative analysis of the economic returns to various programs,
as a planning tool for adjusting research priorities among and within
programs.
5.03      The just'fication for the proposed level of additional research
expenditures is reinforced by ex-post evaluations of investments in
agricultural research in both developed and developing countries which have
virtually always shown high returns, exceeding 402 in most cases. Studies in
India show that returns to agricultural research during the green revolution
ranged from 40 to 60%." While later work disputes some of these high
returns, well planned high-priority research in Pakistan should give economic
rates of return attractive enough to justify this investment. As well,
Pakistan, which depends heavily on agriculture, must have access to constantly
advancing agricultural technology, to provide food for its growing population,
improve rural incomes, and earn foreign exchange. Even if some technology can
be imported, it needs to be tested and, in most cases, modified and adapted.
Most often considerable research is required to adapt such technology to local
conditions and the country cannot afford to be without effective research
services.
5.04      The project would have r jor institution building benefits,
including strengthening provincial research services so they can better
interact with federal services and overseas institutes; consolidating the
various crop, livestock, and fishery rcsearch services in Sind so that a more
unified approach is taken to agricultural research; and improving research
planning capacity, identifying of priorities, and allocating of resources to
those priorities so that research programs fit the available resources, and
concentrate on priority research. The project would improve the coordination
of research, reduce the duplication of work, and introduce monitoring and
evaluation to all research systems. PRPMs would provide a suitable framework
for channeling donor assistance to priority provincial research programs.
"/   Evenson  R.E.,  "Benefits  and  Obstacles  to  Appropriate  Agricultural
Technology", Yale University, Economic Growth Center, Paper No. 313, 1982.



- 37 -
B. Risks
5.05      The main project risks include administrative difficulties arising
from the reorganization of research services, inadequate budgetary resources,
possible irrelevance of proposed research and farmers not adopting research
results. To minimize these risks the project firstly would provide technical
assistance to help GOProvinces design and implement organizational changes,
plus supply intensive management training. Secondly, in preparing PRMPs,
GOProvinces and the consultants would review in detail all important aspects
of provincial research services to ensure administrative changes and improved
management systems fit local needs and customs. The project-introduced
research planning, priority setting, and program budgeting would enable
research administrators to direct available resources towards priority
research, and show in detail the costs of undertaking the proposed research
programs, thus allowing them to more strongly present their case for adequate
and timely financing. The provision of such financing also is required as a
condition under the project (paras 4.05 and 4.12). Lastly, the project should
help to maintain the relevance of research programs and encourage farmer's
adoption of research results by improving planning to relate research to
national and provincial priorities, promoting better linkages between
research, extension and farmers, and formulating research programs taking a
farming systems perspective while increasing on-farm research. The
introduction of uniform procedures and criteria to select research programs,
and screening by local multidisciplinary groups, should also help to maintain
the relevance and quality of research programs.
C. Envircnmental Effects
5.06      Droject activities would contribute substantially to improving and
safeguarding the agro-e_osystems. The project would establish integrated pest
management programs throughout Pakistan which would reduce the use of
pesticides and pesticide contamination of food and pollution of water
resources. The soil science programs would concentrate on work which would
increase the efficiency of irrigation water use, reduce water-logging, reclaim
saline soils, improve the design of drainage systems, and design techniques
for water harvesting and storage in rainfed areas. Livestock programs would
look at regenerating rangelands and other grazing areas, and introducing
livestock production systems which allow the sustainable use of these areas.
Lastly, the project would finance multi-disciplinary research programs in the
Northern area.s of Pakistan directed at not only increasing agricultural
production but also improving and protecting the land and water resources of
the area.
VI. AGREEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
6.01      During negotiations agreement was reached with GOP and GOProvinces
on the following points:
(a) Sind would establish a new research organization which would provide
for: (i) autonomy in staff recruiting; (ii) promotion of
professional staff based on objective criteria of merit and research
achievement; (iii) financial management along administrative lines
followed by universities and other autonomous bodies in Pakistan;



- 38 -
(iv) collegiate style of management; and (v) planning, programming,
resource allocation, monitoring, and evaluation of research, by
June 30, 1991 (para 3.03);
(b) Punjab and Baluchistan by June 30, 1991 would: (i) change existing
Public Service regulations to improve agricultural research staff
recruitment and provide for the promotion of professional research
staff to be based on merit and research achievement as measured by
objective criteria; and (ii) establish Agricultural Research Boards
(para 3.04);
(c) by December 31, 1990: (i) Sind would establish an MSU initially to
be attached to the office of DGAR ARI Tandojam, and following SARO
establishment, to become its secretariat; (ii) NWFP would establish
an MSU attached to the office of AUP's Director of Research, which
would also serve as a Secretariat for AUP's Research Coordination
Board; (iii) Punjab would establish three separate MSUs to service
PARB, and the Directors General of Agriculture and Livestock
Research; and (iv) Baluchistan would establish an MSU to service
BARB (para 3.07);
(d) PARC would establish its new Planning Directorate by December 31,
1990 (para 3.09);
(e) during PU P preparation PARC and the provinces would meet
periodically to ensure that standard techniques and adopted in PRMP
preparation. During ARP-II's first two years these meeting would be
held every three months (para 3.12);
(f) AUF and SAU would each enter into and sign a twinning agreement with
an overseas university to assist upgrade their social science
faculties by June 30, 1991, and would enter into and sign a MOU with
PARC concerning funds for the twinning agreement and social science
research by December 31, 1990 (para 3.22);
(g) GOProvinces would use the additional research funds allocated for
provincial priority research only for programs identified and
specified in PRMPs as approved by IDA (para 3.23);
(h) PARC would locally recruit consultants, with qualifications and
under conditions acceptable to IDA, to assist with the design and
construction of the crop sciences building, and other implementation
matters as needed (para 3.29);
(i) by December 31, 1990, the Soil Survey of Pakistan would sign a
contract for engaging an internationally recruited consultant with
qualifications and under conditions acceptable to IDA, to assist in
computerizing soil survey date (para 3.29);
(j) Implementing agencies would: (i) maintain separate accounts for all

from the special accounts, and statements of expenditure and other

supporting documentation used as the basis for disbursements from

Matter Subgroups by October 31, 1990; (ii) submit completed PRMPs to
IDA for review by August 31, 1992; (iii) submit updated PRMPs to IDA
for review by March 31 of each year; and (iv) annually provide
sufficient capital and operating and maintenance funds to
successfully implement PRMPs (para 4.05);

conditions acceptable to IDA, would undertake a mid-term review of
the project no later than January 31, 1994 in accordance with terms
of reference which would be agreed by June 30, 1993, and would
implement agreed changes to project design or implementation (para
4.18).
6.02      Agreement was reached with PARC and GOProvinces that disbursing
credit funds in excess of the initial payments into the special accounts, plus
SDR 251,000 for PARC, SDR 194,000 for Punjab, SDR 155,000 for Sind, SDR 96,700
for NWFP, and SDR 77,000 for Baluchistan, for civil works, equipment,
furniture, books and journals, training and incremental operating costs would
be conditional on PARC and GOProvinces each signing, by December 31, 1990, a
single contract to engage an internationally recruited firm of consultants,
with qualifications and under conditions acceptable to IDA, to assist:
(a)  the provinces to:  (i) assess the priorities of research
investments; (ii) establish research monitoring and evaluation
programs; (iii) prepare PRMPs and introduce program budgeting into
research planning; (iv) undertake and train staff in diagnostic
surveys of agroecological zones; (v) initiate and strengthen
research programs in horticulture, livestock, soil science and IPM;
and (iv) place and administer long and short-term trainees in
foreign universities and programs; and
(b)  PARC to:  (i) assess the priorities of research investments;
(ii) establish research monitoring and evaluation programs;
(iii) introduce program budgeting into research planning;
(iv) initiate and strengthen research programs in horticulture,



- 40 -
livestock soil science, IPM and social science; and (v) place and
administer long and short-term trainees in foreign universities and
programs (para 3.29).
6.03      The IDA Credit would not become effective until ECNEC has approved
the project's PC-1 and sanctioned project expenditures (para 3.33).
6.04      With the above assurances and conditions, the project would be
suitable for an IDA Credit of SDR 44.4 million (US$57.3 million equivalent' on
standard terms with a 35-year maturity. The Borrower would be the Islamic
Republic of Pakistan.
