PAKISTAN
ACRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Introduction
1.          Agricultural research in Pakistan has its origins in the days
prior to Independence (1947) when Punjab Province had a viable agricultural
College ard Research Institute, a Veterinary College at Lahore, and Sind
Province had research activities extending back some 60 years. Since
Independence, provincial departments of agriculture, livestock, fisheries,
forestry and irrigation have developed their own research institutes and
facilities with 36 separate units in Punjab, 19 in Sind, 11 in NWFP, and 8 in
Baluchistan. Agricultural Research at the federal level has also expanded to
24 entities and 13 Agricultural Universities and Colleges, also carry on some
research (Table 1).
2.          Several teams of international and national scientists have
reviewed the agricultural research system in Pakistan for the purpose of
recommending ways of strengthening its operations. One of the most definitive
studies was conducted in late 1979 by a Joint World Bank, USAID, CIDA, FAO and
Australian Development Assistance Bureau mission which resulted in the May 21,
1980 World Bank document (2961-PAK) "Pakistan Agricultural Research Sub-sector
Review". It highlighted the complexities of conducting agricultural research
where the Pakistani Constitution mandates responsibility for agriculture to
the provinces so that most adaptive and applied agricultural research is
operated and controlled at the provincial levels. Federal Government
responsibility has been mainly for long term research and for coordination,
planning and high level manpower training. It has resulted in a fragmented
research structure with minimal interprovincial cooperation for research
programs, no accountability of resources or effective monitoring of research
progress. Furthermore, effective linkages with extension is lacking and
researchers have limited awareness of farmers' problems with little on-farm
research to verify trials on research stations.
3.          Tne study recommended, inter alia, that the Pakistan Agricultural
Research Council (PARC) should become a truly autonomous body with major
responsibilities for coordination of all federal and provincial funded
agricultural research. Furthermore, that PARC in cooperation with the
provinces should define national agricultural research objectives and
priorities.
4.          External Supgort to Agricultural Research.  These recommendations,
as well as several others, were incorporated into the first World
Bank-assisted Agricultural Research Project (Cr. 1158-PAK) ($40.OM) which
became effective in 1982 and ended in December, 1987. It emphasized
strengthening PARC through increasing autonomy and improved permanent staff
benefits to attract and retain qualified scientists.  It also fostered an
increased coordination role for PARC in new research programs receiving



- 53 -
ANNEX 2
Page 2
federal support including crop varietal yield trials and ongoing national
coordinated research projects and collaborative research programs. Funds were
made available to build a PARC headquarters secretariat, developing the
research station at the National Agricultural Research Center (NARC), training
of staff to M.Sc. and Ph.D levels, and technical assistance.
5.          The first agricultural research project has assisted in developing
PARC into a viable natior.al agricultural research center with excellent
headquarters and NARC station facilities, and the training of staff abroad for
about 97 Ph.Ds and 18 M.Scs. The establishment of PARC as a completely
autonomous organization in December 1981 and a scheme of scientific service
conducive to retaining highly qualified staff, improved its morale and
provided for adequate operational funding of scientists. Research facilities
have also been constructed at the Agricultural Universities in Sind and Punjab
and Agricultural Economic Research Units established at Research Institutes in
Punjab, Sind, Northwest Frontier and at NARC and another is underway in
Baluchistan. PARC has also contracted research out to provincial research
institutes, and has funded 24 subprojects.
6.          Other external donors have provided support to develop Pakistan's
agricultural research capability, especially USAID. In their agriculture
research project ($25.0 million) which ended in 1985, USAID provided support
mainly for strengthening the research capability of NARC with some assistance
to the provinces. In the 1986-1990 Management of Agricultural Research and
Technology (MART-$30.0 m) project, USAID is assisting PARC to create a unified
and integrated national system for effective planning and management of
agricultural research and transmission of improved technology to Pakistani
farmers. USAID, through Public Law 480, has further provided some $14.5 m to
PARC for various research projects. USAID also is assisting in the
Transformation and Integration of the Provincial Agriculture Network
(TIPAN-$40.0 m) Project in the Northwest Frontier Province (NWFP) for
integrating teaching, research and outreach of the Agricultural University of
NWFP. It has led to the merging of the provincial agricultural research
institutes with the University. The Canadian International and Development
Assistance (CIDA) provides $11.0 m to PARC and NARC for Barani Agricultural
Research and Development in rainfed areas. Italy has also provided $10.0
million to PARC to conduct large scale proven production packages in three
farming localities mainly through the effective use of farm machinery.
7.          Constraints.  The substantial inputs from GOP with external
multilateral and bilateral assistance has gone far to establish a viable
federal agricultural research system through PARC and NARC in facilities,
equipment and especially in creating a cadre of highly trained scientists.
However, such is not the case at the provincial level, and it is clear that if
Pakistan is to have an effective agricultural research service to benefit most
of its farmers, the provincial agricultural research services need to be
strengthened. This was clearly enunciated in several reviews including the
1979 study and the 1983 USAID review of its first research projeLt, and has
become increasingly more evident in the intervening years. Some of the major
constraints in the provincial agricultural research systems are:



- 54 -
ANNEX 2
Page 3
(1)   insufficient training and research qualifications of many of its
scientists;
(2)   inadequate operating funds to conduct meaningful research;
(3)   rigidities of the research service schemes since they are tied
into provincial government civil service rules which rewards
seniority and not merit with concomitant low morale of scientific
staff;
(4)   insufficient planning, programming, monitoring and evaluation of
research pertinent to solving farmers' problems;
(5)   little on-farm research to determine farming systems in practice
and research to remedy productivity problems. An example of the
type of research which needs to be expanded is illustrated in the
PARC/CIMMYT collaborative programs 1986 research paper "Increasing
Wheat Productivity in the Context of Pakistan's Irrigated Cropping
Systems - A View from the Farmers' Field". It demonstrated the
need for improved management practices to exploit the genetic
potential of the planted varieties especially in cultivation
practices and water management; and
(6)   ineffective linkages with the extension services for effec'ive
transmission of demonstrated impzoved technology to farmers.
B. Present Status Research Organization. Management. and Financing
8.          Sugnort to Agricultural Research.  The Government of Pakistan has
recognized the importance of agriculture in the economy, and the necessity to
invest in research to continually improve agricultural productivity so as to
keep up with population growth and export needs, while maintaining or
improving the land and water resource base. In 1980/81 the rate of investment
in agricultural research was about 0.5% of AGDP (Review of Agricultural
Research Institutes in Pakistan). By 1983/84 this rate had increased to about
0.8% of A(JP, reflecting an increase in emphasis on agricultural research.
The increase in allowances to scientists in July 1987, also points to the
continuing supportive policy to the role of science and research in national
development.
9.          Research Institutions.  The agricultural research system in
Pakistan is very complex. The National Agricultural Research Plan (NARP)
(1986) complied a list (Table 1) of research institutions dealing with
agricultural research and allied subjects based on the results from two major
surveys.'/ This shows a total of 101 research institutes or stations, some of
which have substations in addition, however at least 18 of those listed had
less than five research staff in 1984. No routinely updated data base is
Review of Agricultural Research Institutions in Pakistan, Agricultural
Research Division, Islamabad, 1984.
Agricultural Research Manpower Survey, PARC, Islamabad 1985.



- 55 -
ANNIE 2
Page 4
currently maintained, however there are estimated to be about 100 significant
research groups. The 1984 review showed 3,431 research scientists, of grade
NPS 16 and above, distributed around these stations, in 19 different
Ministries, Departments or Commissions as indicated below:
Ministries      Number of          Number of
Departments     Institutions       Scientists
Location                     Commissions     or Stations        June 1984
Federal                          5               24               1,176
Punjab                           4               36                 866
Sind                             3               '9                 324
NWFP                             2               11                 273
Baluchistan                      2                8                 103
Agricultural Universities        3                3                 689
Total                  19             101                3,431
Source: Agricultural Research Manpower Survey, 1985; and National Agriculture
Research Plan, 1986.
10.       Structure and Style of Management.  The federal research
institutions such as those of PARC, the Atomic Energy Commission and the
Pakistan Central Cotton Committee PCCC, have independent budgets, have
reasonable independence in operations, the staff have a scientific scheme of
service and the possibilities of a collegial style of management focused
primarily on the research task.
11.       The provincial research institutions are, in general, part of
development departments responsible for Agriculture, For6stry, Livestock,
Fisheries, Irrigation, etc., oriented primarily to development and production.
The research institutions operate under hierarchical, in-line control from the
Head of the Department, within department budgets, and with a scheme of
service that relates to hierarchical values of years of experience, number of
staff supervised and resources controlled, rather than research capabilities
and performance, which may not involve much administrative responsibility.
Many research scientists are dispirited with reported shortages and delays in
operational funding for research also contributing to low morale.
12.       The Universities which are funded federally under the Ministry of
Education fall between these two patterns in that a collegiate style of
management, appropriate to research, is understood, and the role and nature of
research well appreciated. Moreover, qualifications for recruitment are
similar for research and teaching. However, the teaching function is given
the highest priority, and research is not regarded as the primary task of



- 56 -
ANNEX 2
Page 5
staff even though it may play a significant part in assessment for promotion.
In the North West frontier Province a major step has been taken recently in
the organization of research, in that the provincial research service has been
merged with the Agricultural University but funds for the research institutes
come from Provincial resources.
13.       Research Coordination.  The coordination of agricultural research
activities is a major task of management.  At Federal level, PARC is assigned
this responsibility.  Its central mechanism is the Board of Governors with
representation from all the major research organizations, and its principal
operational wings for coordination are the four divisions of Crop Sciences,
Animal Sciences, Natural Resources, and Social Sciences with support from the
Multi-Divisional Services. PARC has coordinated the production of the
National Agricultural Research Plan (1986) which sets out the perspective for
the contribution of research to development, and defines broad goals and
objectives for future research ard component research programs to overcome
major constraints to increased productivity. The National Coordinated
Research Programs and their associated Technical Committees are also valuable
in coordinating research nationwide on major national commodities and
production factors.
14.       It is also recognized that there is a need to coordinate research at
the provincial level, especially amongst the Universities and the Provincial
Research Institutes. However, the only active body has been the Punjab
Agricultural Research Coordination Board, which has a very large membership
under the chairmanship of the Vice Chancellor of the University of
Agriculture, Faisalabad. Without the equivalent executive arms of PARC, the
PRCB has been unable as yet to be very effective in spanning the wide range of
functions from setting broad priorities for the province, to detailed
examination of research projects for support. In NWFP the merger between the
research service and the Agricultural University should help ensure close
coordination of research, but again, appropriate authorized bodies will be
needed at different levels to ensure integrated programming.
15.       Research Programming.  The process of research programming varies
substantially amongst institutions. Generally, proposals for experiments and
studies are put forward from individuals and sections in response to
suggestions from section and divisional leaders. These are then reviewed in
the disciplinary section, and are forwarded for scrutiny and approval at a
senior inter-departmental group. The aggregated program is finally approved
by the Director or Director-General and may or may not require clearance from
a higher board. The program is liable to be tr'mmed if expected budgets do
not materialize, but such reductions are often not documented. No institution
visited required the preparation of budgets for experiments in the annual
program. However, if research activities have to be financed through special
project funding, then details of the project and its cost requirements have to
be submitted and approved at peer group review boards.
16.       Source of Finance.  Most financing of research operations at
institutes comes from government resources through the parent ministry or
organization. A significant amount of research funding also comes from major
projects, such as ARP I and BARD, and, in the past, PL 480 funds. All



- 57 -
ANNEX 2
Page 6
research organizations lack sufficient funds for carrying out research and for
transport to work off main stations, but the facilities of farms, buildings
and equipment appear to be reasonably adequate in comparison with operational
funding. Most provincial institutions in line Departments experience delays
in release of funds and of arbitrary cuts in funding during the year, which
makes research planning difficult. Especially in the provincial research
institutions, low level of funds for research operational activities severely
constrained the effectiveness and efficiency of trained research scientists.
17.       Overall Funding.  In 1983/84 the total investment in agricultural
research was PRs 786 million, which was about 0.21% of GDP, or 0.82X of
Agricultural GDP which is the more usually quoted index. A recent analysis of
other developing and developed countries investment in agricultural research
is shown in Table 2. It shows that Pakistan was below the average rate of
investment for 52 developing countries in 1983/84, but not by much. However,
it was well behind the investment rate for developed countries (even those
where agriculture is not very important in the economy), and below the general
recommendation of the World Bank of a rate of 1% AGDP as soon as possible, and
2% AGDP by 1995. Clearly there would appear to be room for improvement in the
overall rate of investment in agricultural research for the agricultural
sector, especially in investment per scientist.
18.      Oterational Funding of Research.  Summary information from the
Manpower Survey of 1983/84 is given in Table 3, and is presented in Table 4 in
terms of expenditure per researcher, and for selected research institutions in
Table 5.
19.       The overall figure per scientist includes salary and allowances
(scientist, technical and administrative support staff, and labor); capital
development; and operational funding for research (materials, transportation,
utilities, rent of buildings, vehicle and machinery maintenance,
communications, replacement of small-scale and consumable equipment, share of
maintenance of buildings, roads and farms, etc.). There is no distinction
made between station overheads, and individual research activity expenses or
variable costs.
20.       The overall national average expenditure per scientist of PRs
229,000 (US$17,000) p.a. in 1983/84 was heavily influenced by relatively high
capital and development project funding through federal institutions. From
the non-development budget (i.e. regular or recurrent budget) alone, the
expenditure rate was only PRs 111,000 (US$8,200) per scientist p.a. Both
figures were lifted substantially by the relatively high level of vacancies
against sanctioned positions: if all positions had been filled, the total and
non-development rates would have fallen to PRs 173,000 (US$12,800) and
PRs 84,000 (US$6,200) respectively. To attempt to interpret these figures,
three comparisons can be made: internally in cross-comparison among
institutions; externally with other countries; and intrinsically, in relation
to costs of minimal effective research activities.
21.       Internal.  There is considerable variation amongst institutions.
Some are well below the average. The reasonably (but not excessively) well
equipped and supported research centers at Dokri, and the Nuclear Institute



- 58 -
ANEXnE 2
Page 7
for Food and Agriculture (NIFA), Tarnab have more than twice the rate of
non-development expenditure per scientist than any of the provincial
Agricultural Research Institutes (ARIs). This measis a very considerable
difference in operational funds after scientists' salaries are deducted.
22.       External.  An unpublished analysis of 1980 data by Oram and Bindlish
is the most appropriate comparison currently available, and comparative
information for 67 countries is given in Table 2. The details may not be
precise due to some differences in pattern of returns, but it is clear that in
terms of average expenditure per scientist, in 1980, Pakistan, at US$9,000
p.a., was in the bottom five out of 67 countries. Moreover, the overall
average level at US$50,600 per scientist was over five times the level in
Pakistan, and in only 14 countries was the level less than US$20,000.  Salary
scales differ considerably from country to country and have a large effect,
but many of the costs of research operations are international - chemicals,
fertilizers, equipment, transport, training, etc.  The conclusion cannot be
avoided that in 1980 Pakistan was funding agricultural research scientists at
a much lower level than that which most developing countries considered
appropriate.
23.       More recent data show an improvement since 1980, reflecting the
improved investment in research in Pakistan in recent years. The latest
figures from the ISNAR/IFARD review for nearby Asian Countries are given in
Table 6. In comparison with Table 2, they suggest India may have slipped back
while Pakistan has improved. However, both are still very low compared to
Indonesia and most other African and Latin American countries (Table 2),
especially since half of the U$17,000 of expenditure per scientist in Pakistan
in 1984 is due to capital and project development funding.
24.       Intrinsic.  It is also possible to make an estimate of the basic
requirement for funding an agricultural research scientist from some idea of
research input costs and operational needs. The conclusion is that US$8,000
per research scientist would be a modest level of operational funding in 1984.
Actual average levels are about US$3,000.
25.       Ratio of Salaries to Operational Punds.  A commonly used mechanism
for estimating the level of operational funds for research, is the ratio of
salaries to operational funds. In the USA it was calculated some years ago,
that a ratio of about 70X on salaries to about 30X on operational expenditure
was optimal - for USA conditions. In translating this ratio elsewhere, it is
important to note that while salary levels may drop dramatically, many of the
costs of operational materials do not: unit costs of equipment, petrol,
vehicles, chemicals, in fact, rise. Allowing that some operational costs are
indeed lower (labor etc.), a reasonable target ratio of salaries to
operational funds in Pakistan might have been 40:60, in 1984.
26.       A more specific analysis has been done for the non-development
budgets for some of the research institutes of PARC for the period 1985 to
1988. Details are given in Table 7 (expressed in 1987 US dollars for purposes
of comparison with information from other countries). For the PARC research
institutes, the budgeted level of funding for salaries (of about US$7,000
p.a.) would cover roughly a research scientist, one technical support staff



- 59 -
ANNEX 2
Page 8
and one administrative or general support staff. The level of funding for
carrying out research operations is targeted at about US$3-4,000 per
researcher, (although budget allocation tended to be dropping below US$3,000
per researcher in 1987/88). This staffing pattern, April 1987 salary scales
and level of operational support leads to a ratio of salaries to operational
funding of about 60:40. At current salary scales November 1987 (after the
recent increase the salary complement is nearer US$10,000) the ratio is nearer
75:25.
27.       Ogerational Funding for Agricultural Research in some other
Developing Countries. A general review (by Rene Devred, ISNAR (unpublished))
includes the following specific cases:
(a) Peru, 1931: WB project supporting agricultural research allocated
operational funding per researcher at US$9,500 p.a. (1981 dollars).
(b) Laos, 1987: recent planning is using US$11,000 p.a. per researcher
for operational funds.
(c)  Cameroon, 1985-87: operational funding ranges from US$12,000 to
US$20,000 p.a. per researcher.
(d) Tunisia, 1985: US$8,860 p.a. per scientist, plus US$8,000 p.a. for
operational costs.
28.       Research Manpower Supply.  Recruitment of staff at graduate level
from the Universities is not a serious problem. Some 50X of the research
staff have had post graduate training to master's level (2,181 out of 3,431 in
June 1984). However, a large number (951 in 1984) in research positions have
only a bachelor's degree with no formal training in research methodology. The
capacity in-country for specialist research development to doctorate level is
very limited, and overseas training, opportunities are limited. The number of
research and teaching staff trained to PhD level by 1984 was only 299, half of
whom were in the Universities. A substantial increase in this number has
however been made possible through ARP I and MART projects.
29.       Research Planning and Programming.  Determining a national or
provincial agricultural research plan is a three stage sequential process:
(a) determining broad research priorities and allocating resources to major
commodities, factors of production, and/or areas, etc. ("commodities");
(b) within a "commodity" priority allocation, at institution level, deciding
on sub-allocations to high priority technical research thrusts to reduce major
constraints to production, and developing a long range plan involving planning
of manpower development and of large physical resources, and finally; (c) at
research station level, deciding which are the most urgent and relevant
experiments and studies to be done in the near future with available manpower
and financial resources to make progress in the plan. The three stages
require different groups of different people, using different criteria to
reach conclusions, hopefully with a common thread of values running
throughout. A discussion of the process is noted in the Project File B.1,
Annex 2, Appendix 2.



- 60 -
ANNEX 2
Page 9
30.       Broad Priority Setting for Commodities.  The major exercise,
organized by PARC, in producing a National Agricultural Research Plan, with
extensive interaction amongst institutions, has been successful in integrating
provincial and national broad priorities. However, the priorities for
long-term research thrusts are not quantified by allocations in the NARP: it
is not apparent whether a research program can have 2 or 20 researchers
assigned to it; and therefore, for instance, what kind of specialist training
or recruitment can be planned. Nor is there any chance of quantification
possible at this long-term planning stage without some prior quantification of
priorities (and inferred allocation) amongst "commodities" at a higher level
of decision than research institutions.
31.       In the absence of a unified federal and provincial service, there
can be no completely hierarchical, authoritative allocations, and "agreed
guidelines" might be a more appropriate target, to give some quantitative
basis for long-range planning. The most appropriate body for reaching such
quantified guidelines on priorities for "commodity" research is the Board of
Governors of PARC. It has a broad and responsible membership of research
users with high level political, financial and development interests, together
with technical and educational insights on possibilities and constraints for
research. The Board would also be the most appropriate body to present the
national case for a policy for an appropriate rate of investment in research
for the agricultural sector.
32.       The Board, given this mandate, would need to be serviced with
appropriate information from PARC to help it reach decisions. A substantial
amount of information is already available from the work of preparing NARP.
However, an additional, regular, source of information frcm analysis of the
ongoing national needs and opportunities in agriculture should be available to
the Board through a permanent servicing committee. Such information could
usefully be presented formally and objectively on the basis of a 'weighted
criteria" method for guiding decisions on research priorities for commodities.
33.       National p-:iorities will guide the PARC Board of Governors in
reaching priorities for research, but the provinces will have their own
priorities to guide their own provincial allocations for research within
national guidelines. They will therefore need equivalent bodies for reaching
such broad priority decisions. If a merging of University and provincial
research components into an autonomous research service took place, then a
Research Council similar to that proposed in NWFP would be appropriate.
Otherwise a reorganization of provincial research with sufficient autonomy
similar to PARC would be required. The precise form of mechanism is secondary
to the importance of the function being carried out.
34.       Long-Term Planning.  The NARP, in its analysis of objectives and
programs, has given a strong lead for long-term planning in research
institutions and PARC has instituted strategic Technical Committees to cover
major research areas. Most organizations have appropriate groups of Heads of
Departments meeting to advise the Director-General on technical research
opportunities and constraints for long-term planning. However, the absence of
quantified priority guidelines from a policy Board makes realistic planning
difficult: it is especially difficult when trying to arrive at far-reaching



- 61 -
ANNEX 2
Page 10
decisions on what manpower resources should be allocated for training in
specialized fields to meet research needs in the longer term. Rates of
increase of research staff must be related closely to the likely rates of
funding for research, if operational funds per scientist are to reach levels
for efficient performance.
35.       The key resource for long-term research planning is trained
manpower. It is important therefore that there should be complete inventories
of research manpower at institution, provincial and national levels.
Previously this was a difficult task, but with computers, it is a very much
easier process and a computerized register of research manpower is a powerful
management tool for long-term planning of research. This responsibility has
recently been allocated within PARC to the Planning Division.
36.       Short-Term Programming.  Procedures for deciding on short-term
programs and proposals for experiments and studies were found to be very
diverse in the research institutions. An important criterion at this level of
decision is the intellectual satisfaction of the researcher. Procedures range
from tacit immediate acceptance of a researcher's proposals, to discussion in
disciplinary groups, to discussion in commodity-based interdisciplinary
groups. Only a few institutions acknowledged that information from AERUs on
the conditions of the farmers' enterprises influenced their choice of
treatments or experiments. A few scientists protested their independence to
choose experiments that they thought most interesting and were related to the
major crops. Such a range of procedures inevitably leads to inconsistency of
standards in research proposals and projects or sub-projects.
37.       It is therefore recommended that in future to standardize criterion
all proposals for experiments and studies should be discussed in detail at
interdisciplinary program committees at a relatively low level of management,
addressing issues of relevance and quality. Management should require a
minimum number of disciplines to be represented in each committee, with
someone representing, as a proxy, the interests of the farmer, and another,
the interests of the extension service. The socio-economists cannot, at
present, be very well represented as their numbers are small indeed.
38.       Monitoring and Evaluation.  A concommitant element of good
management after program determination is the arrangement for monitoring and
evaluation of the program when it is implemented. There has always been a
considerable amount of informal monitoring and evaluation of agricultural
research at various levels in Pakistan, but formal monitoring has tended to be
limited to the requirements of external donors who have supported research
projects. For instance, the Project Implementation Unit (PIU) has maintained
quarterly accounts of physical and financial progress in components of ARP I,
and systematic reports of technical research progress have also been made.
The NAC preliminary recommendations call for more systematic evaluation of
research performance as an intrinsic part of research management. The
Pakistan Agricultural Research council (PARC) is now resolved to introduce
more formal monitoring and evaluation procedures to provide a better
management information service that will facilitate better implementation of
ongoing projects, and will help policy makers, planners and research managers
to plan, design and implement sound and productive projects to utilize scarce



- 62 -
ANNEX 2
Page 11
resources more efficiently. Presently, PARC lacks a proper system of
monitoring and evaluation, and lack of skilled manpower in this specialized
field has resulted in underdevelopment of this area at PARC, and at the
national level in general and also in the provinces. Thus there is a clear
need to establish Mo-itoring and Evaluation capabilities at both the national
and provincial levels.
39.       Research Environment.  Efficient and effective agricultural research
requires creative and operating environment different from that of
development, administrative, and regulatory Departments. Research does best
under a collegiate style of management rather than a hierarchical style. The
intrinsic uncertainty in research requires more flexibility in financial
control (over relatively small amounts, and fully within national accounting
rules) than is usually encountered in a development ministry bureaucracy.
Because much of operational expenditure is within the research institutes
themselves, the ratio of operational funds for research to salaries is usua]ly
much higher than for advisory, developmental or administrative activities.
Furthermore, criteria for performance in research and its general system of
values are very different from those in administrative, hierarchical
organizations. On all these grounds, there is a case for having a distinct
management and financial system for agricultural research.
40.       In a typical industrial enterprise, the management style of the
innovative R&D Division is quite different from the management style of the
production and sales divisions. The latter are closely planned and controlled
and have a hierarchical style of management. The planning in an R&D division
is much more flexible, and the management style for the research programs is
essentially collegiate, involving a "first amongst equals" approach that
reflects the reality that in their own specialist fields, low level
researchers often have more detailed knowledge than their director. Many
countries have recognized this essential difference ar.d have set up autonomous
research organizations with their own budget, but often still within the
ministry responsible for agriculture (e.g. UK, India, Kenya, Ghana, Indonesia,
Argentina, Zimbabwe, etc.). Others have merged large elements of the national
research system within Universities because of the common collegiate style of
management, value systems, and similar requirements in qualifications (e.g.
USA, Nigeria, Philippines, India). They usually also have an appropriate
scheme of service for staff, with reward related to research performance
rather than, or as well as, level of hierarchical responsibility for funds and
supervision of personnel. In some cases, different methods have been used
within a country (e.g. USA, India). Pakistan has currently gone part of the
way in establishing automous research regimes in PARC and its institutes, and
in Nuclear and other Federal research institutes; while in NWFP the provincial
agricultural research has beern taken into the Agricultural University
governance system.
41.       However, a large part of agricultural research in the other
provinces still resides in the hierarchical line control in the Agriculture,
Livestock, Forestry, and Fisheries Departments, with all of their budgetary,
organizational and - omotional procedures, that might be appropriate for
development activities, but which reduce research efficiency. It has been
pointed out earlier than rates of funding for research operation are very low.



- 63 -
ANNEX 2
Page 12
However, at least the Federal research institutes have striven to maintain a
ratio of salaries to operation funds of 60:40. In provincial research
institution the Departments, the ratio is often nearer 80:20 which may be
appropriate for administrative or extension jobs but, as discussed earlier, it
is not adequate for efficient research. A different concept of funding levels
for research operations is necessary which can best be established in a
semi-autonomous institution with its own budget and financial control.
42.       The case for changing the status of the proviacial research
institutions into more independent organizations with their own budget.
administrative, and recruitment procedures and schemes of service seems very
strong, considering experience in other countries and within Pakistan. The
case for the development departments maintaining a very firm influence on the
research program is also strong as it is essential for the program to be
focused on develc,ment objectives. However, this does not imply standardized
bureaucratic control over other research operations. An appropriate structure
or mechanism is essential to improve the research environment of the
researchers in the provincial researrn institutions.
C. Government Policy on Agricultural Research
Oreanization and Management
43.       The National Commission of Agriculture (NCA) in its Report of March
1988, in Chapter 16 on Agricultural Research, Extension and Education clearly
recognizes and enunciates the constraints on organization and management of
agricultural research in Pakistan as described above. They note that most
provincial and federal agricultural research institutes are subject to rigid
government administrative and financial controls. They indicate that these
rigidities result in delay and lack of flexibility which greatly reduce their
effectiveness. They also recognize weaknesses in the organizational structure
of research institutions and that most lack planning, resource allocation,
monitoring and evaluation capabilities. The Commission presents four
recommendations for improving agricultural research efficiency as follows:
(1) "Agricultural Research Institutions should be given greater autonomy
in staff recruitment and promotion as well as financial management
to provide flexibility required for their effective operation and
enable them to respond to emergent needs. A pattern of
administration similar to the one followed by Universities or
autonomous bodies like PARC and Agriculture Prices Commission should
be adopted. There should be sufficient delegation of powers down
the lina for efficient operation";
(2)  "the organized structure of the Institutions should be reviewed in
order to make them more compact by eliminating unnecessary dichotomy
of sections or disciplines";
(3)  "with each institute there should be an effective mechanism for
planning, resource allocation and monitoring and evaluation of
research"; and



- 64 -
ANNEX2
Page 13
(4) as part of the overall research system, provision should be made for
external program and management reviews of all major institutions,
every five years, with the participation of national and
international scientists of repute. The review should be conducted
in relation to the targets and goals set for research output".
44.       It is therefore evident from the information presented in paras
7-42, that the ARP-II need to emphasize the strengthening of the provincial
agricultural research systems in organization and management. Discussions
with government officials at the federal and provincial levels have nearly
unanimously indicated top priority for support to provincial agricultural
research systems.
D. The ARP-II Project - Organization and Management
45.       Organization.  Concomitant with Government of Pakistan policy
enunciated in the 1988 NCA Report, the project would foster reorganization and
strengthening of agricultural research in the provinces. Reorganization would
be assigned to provide greater autonomy in staff recruitment, promotion and
financial management in order to create a stimulating environment for
producing technological improvements for the benefit of the provinces farmers
and the agricultural sector.
46.       The ultimate goal should be the merger of provincial research with
its agricultural University as accomplished in NWFP. Until then, the other
three provinces will reorganize its agricultural research entities to improve
by varying degrees:
(1) autonomy in staff recruiting;
(2) promotion of professional staff based on objective criteria of merit
and research achievement;
(3) financiai management along administrative lines followed by
Universities and other autonomous bodies in Pakistan;
(4) collegiate style of management; and
(5) provision for planning, programming, resource allocation, monitoring
and evaluation of research.
47.       Punjab and Baluchistan would change existing Public Service
regulations to (a) improve the recruiting of research staff through making the
system more responsive in terms of time to recruit and appoint new staff, and
the suitability of staff recruited, with the criteria for recruiting research
staff giving more weightage to academic and technical knowledge and research
achievement; (b) increase the opportunities for promotion within a researchers
area of expertise by increasing the number of higher research positions within
the different research areas, and introducing floating posts awarded on merit;
(c) and give more weightage to merit and research achievement in promotion of
staff. Objective criteria would be introduced to measure merit and research



- 65 -
ANNEX 2
Page 14
achievement. Outstanding merit could be recognized by promotion to a higher
position or by more than usual annual salary increments.
48.       To allow an integrated approach to research planning and resource
allocation, the two provinces would establish new Research Boards chaired by
the provinces Planning and Development Department. The Boards would have
limited membership including, for example, the Secretaries of Agriculture,
Livestock, Forestry and Finance, Vice-Chancellor of the Agricultural
University, Director Generals of Research and Extension, and the Chief of
PARC's Planning Division. The Boards would be responsible for:
(a) identifying provincial research priorities and broad thrusts (within
national guidelines), and establishing guidelines for allocating research
funds to these priorities; (b) collating, producing, and annually updating
provincial research master plans; and (c) monitoring and internally evaluating
provincial research. Sind province has agreed to the establishment of a
semi-autonomous research organization, to be called the Sind Agriculture
Research Organization, merging agriculture, livestock, fisheries and possibly
forestry research, and to rapidly obtain the required provincial enactments.
Suggested provisions for such a Provincial Research Organization (PRO) in
conformity with other Pakistan semi-autonomous bodies such as PARC, is shown
i        in Appendix 1.
:        49.       Planning. Programming. Monitoring and Eval-iation.  These functions
would be strengthened at both the national and the provincial level.
Elaboration of these functions may be noted in Project File B.l, Annex 2
and 3. At the national level PARC's Planning Directorate will be expanded to
include the functions of planning, implementation including the project
implementation unit (PIU) for the ARP-II, monitoring, evaluation and
inventory. The Planning Unit would assist in preparation of: (1) National
Master Agricultural Research Plan; (2) Annual Development Program for
Re.search; (3) annual Portfolio of research proposals requiring foreign
exchange; (4) PC-ls of major development projects for research; (5) assisting
Tech.nical Divisions in developing project proposals; and (6) processing of
projects through local and foreign agencies. The Planning Unit would also
offer assistance to provincial research institutions or organizations
requesting such help.
50.       The Implementation Unit of PARC's Planning Dirente1rate would:
(1) monitor all its research projects receiving funds from external donors;
(2) process monthly, quarterly and annual progress reports; (3) expedite rund
release, staff appointments, equipment, procurement and staff training; and
(4) liaise with international agencies for specific assistance. The
Monitoring and Evaluation Unit would: (1) monitor research progress in PARC
projects; (2) establish data processing and appropriate procedures required to
monitor progress; (3) evaluate projects and institute commodity/subject
reviews; (4) assist in establishinig M&E units at other research institutions
as requested; and (5) assist in evaluation missions of other research
institutions.
51.       The Inventory Unit would be a new one whose function would be to
develop and maintain a data base on all agricultural research personnel in the
country (current field/commodity; research institutions and their facilities;



- 66 -
ANNEXr 2
Page 15
and total resource allocations. These would be essential elements of
information for long-range planning and coordination of national agricultural
research by PARC. Thus the design and development of: (1) national inventory
of agricultural research; (2) register of research personnel; (3) register of
agricultural research institutions; and (4) register of resource use in
agricultural research will be the key activities of the Inventory Unit.
52.       To carry out the above expanded functions, the project would provide
funds for incremental staff to the Planning Division of PARC to increase its
professional staff by an additional nine officers in grades 17 to 19 plus
support staff, procure PC computers, office furniture and transport. Training
abroad for one PhD and two MSc and 6 mm of technical assistance will also be
provided. Details may be noted in the Pro4ect Implementation Volume.
53.       At the provincial level the research organization would be
strengthened by establishing a secretariat attached to its Director or
Director-General of Research to carry out planning, programming, monitoring
and evaluation functions. The project would provide funds for incremental
staff for officers in grades 17 through 19 with support staff to be hired to
set up these activities. It would also serve as the provincial project
Implementation Unit during the six years of ARP-II. Funds will be provided
for training to the Masters degree level in planning, programming, monitoring
and evaluation functions and for computer, furniture, transport and technical
assistance.
54.       Research Master Plan.  It is essential that each Province establish
an overall agricultural research master plan (PRMP) based on the provincial
government's delineation of its agricultural, livestock, fisheries and
forestry priorities and consistent with national goals and priorities. Such
PRMPs would determine programs, manpower requirements, training needs,
facilities, equipment, operation and maintenance, and capital and recurrent
budgets on a five year basis plus an additional indicative five years. Such
planning would be an ongoing process in each PRO's secretariat and would be
refined and updated on annual basis. Terms of Reference for the preparation
of the PRMPs are shown in Appendix 2. Once such a PRMP is produced, it serves
as a useful source of information for determining funding by federal and
provincial governments and external maltilateral and bilateral donors.
55.       The first PRMP produced by each of the four provinces would be
completed by the end of the second year of ARP-II and would be then updated
annually by the established PRO in each province. It is recognized that
preparation of the first PRMPs will require considerable time and effort as
well as setting up functional provincial planning, programming, monitoring and
evaluation units. Accordingly, ARP-II would provide technical assistance of
national and internationally recruited experts to assist provincial staff in
preparation of research programs and projects, manpower and training
requirements, staff incentives improvement, research facilities and equipment
needs, establishing effective linkages with extension and internal and
external research organizations, improved research and management information
systems, and capital and operating budgetary requirements. Technical
assistance, including both internationally and locally recruited experts, for



- 67 -
ANNEX 2
Page 16
each province has been provided under ARP-II for the abovementioned purposes.
Terms of Reference for the technical assistance is shown in Appendix 3.
56.       Standardization.  Since new methologies will be introduced at the
national and provincial levels in programming, planning, monitoring and
evaluation and th.e preparation of master research plans, there will be a need
to assure standardization of techniques used. Accordingly PARC will need to
coordinate such activities by periodic meetings of concerned staff and
consultants to ensure a degree of uniformity in approach. Such meetings
should be at least once every three months during the first two years of
ARP-II.
57.       Incremental Operating Funds.  Incremental operating funds are being
provided under ARP-II for establishing and strengthening management service
units described in para 49-55, and for previously determined priority research
in horticultur:e, livestock, soils and social science. It is recognized that
there are other areas of priority research at the provincial level which have
yet to be identified and which would be identified as a result of preparing
the Provincial Master Research Plan (PRMP). Accordingly, ARP-II will provide
incremental operating funds for Rs 115.1 million for Punjab Province, Rs 43.2
million for Sind Province, Rs 24.3 million for NWFP, and Rs 9.1 million for
Baluchistan Province. However, these funds will be released only for research
identified and specified in PRMPs as approved by IDA.
58.       The amount of capital and incremental operating funds available to
the PROs are insufficient to cover all essential research requirements in
agriculture, livestock, fisheries and forestry. However, it is assumed that
the preparation of the first PRMPs by the end of the second year of ^RP-II
will identify the extent of additional needs to further strengthen r incial
research. It cculd then be the basis for an ARP-III by the third o. ,.ourth
year of implementation of ARP-II.

PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH PROJECT II
Provincial Agricultural Research Organization
1.        A Provincial Agricultural Research Organization (PRO) would be
established (consistent with the laws and regulations of the province and the
Government of Pakistan). Once the ordinance is published, all agricultural
livestock, fisheries, and forestry research entities, staff and facilities
presently within the provit.-ial government would be transferred to the
jurisdiction of the PRO.
Functions
2.        The function of the PRO would be to:
(1) undertake, assist and promote research and its effective utilization
for the benefit of the province;
(2) strengthen the research activities as required to solve problems
facing the agricultural, livestock, fisheries and forestry sectors
to fulfill provincial goals consistent with national priorities;
(3) upgrade its research, technical and administrative staff through
training in Pakistan and abroad;
(4) promote linkages with extension and other indigenious and
international research organizations for the dissemination of
research findin,s;
(5) allocate funds received for the proper operation of its research
activities; and
(6) institute regulations approved by the provincial government
affecting appointments, terms and condition of service of its
employees to attract and retain highly qualified scientific
personnel, and to create an environment (collegiate style of
management) conducive to conducting research for solving the
provinces agricultural, livestock, fisheries and forestry problems.
Management
3.        The overall direction and control of the PRO will be by a Board
appointed by the Chief Minister of the Province. Its Chairman would be the
p Provincial Additional Chief Secretary or equivalent.  The Board would consist
of not more than 15 members and in addition to the Chairman, would include the
Director General of Research, the Vice Chancellor of the Agricultural
University, full-time PRO staff representing crops, livestock, fisheries,
forestry, and finance, and members from PARC, and provincial departments of
Planning and Development, Finance, Agriculture, Livestock, Fisheries and
Forestry.



- 80 -                              ANNEX 2
Appendix 1
Page 2
Appointments would be for three years. The Board would meet at least twice a
year presided over by the Chairman or, in his absence, by a member of the
Board designated by him. The first Board meetii.g of the year (January) would
consider policy issues relating to scientific and technical matters whereas
the second meeting (July) would consider budget and administrative matters.
4.        An executive committee would be established to serve as the
principal administrative body of PRO responsible for executing policies and
discharging all functions of PRO.  It would be headed by a chief executive,
and would include the five full-time PRO staff appointed to the Board.  The
executive committee would establish a secretariat to exercise control over
financial, administrative, planning, programming, monitoring and evaluation of
PRO operations.  The executive committee would meet once a month and at other
times as required.  The Board may delegate to the Executive Committee such of
its functions and powers it may deem necessary.
5.        The executive committee would also establish technical committees to
review research proposals and on-going research in designated commodities or
disciplines and recommend the means for conducting such to the Executive
Committee.
6.        The chief executive of the PRO would be a senior scientist in BPS 20
or more, and his appointment would be made by the Board through open
competition from all eligible senior research scientists of the province.
Criteria for selection would include demonstrated scientific and
administrative capabilities, a minimum grade of BPS 20 with at least three
years of service in that grade, a minimum of ten years of service as a
research scientist, a PH D degree from a university of good standing, and
other provincial government regulations required of such a higher level
position. The chief executive of the PRO would serve for a fixed term of five
years.
Finance
7.        A Fund will be established to which all sums received by the PRO
shall be credited and from which all expenditures of the PRO will be debited.
The Funds of PRO would consist of:
(1) grants from the Federal and Provincial governments;
(2) grants, donations, endowment, contributions, aid and assistance
given by other organizations;
(3) foreign aid and loans obtained or raised with the approval of the
government; and
(4) receipts from other sources.
PRO funds should be non lapsable. PRO may open accounts in any scheduled bank
in Pakistan. The accounts would be audited in accordance with government
procedures.



-81 -                               ANNEX 2
Appendix 1
Page 3
Reports
8.        The PRO shall furnish to the Provincial government by the end of
each financial year a report on the activities of PRO and a summary of
accounts for that year. An updated Research Master Plan for PRO for the next
ten years including estimated budgets for the first five years will also be
submitted annually. Also, every five years, national and international
scientists of repute would conduct an external review, in relation to
previously set targets and goals, of the agricultural research programs and
their management.

PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL REsEARCH PROJECT II
PROVINCIAL RESEARCH MASTER PLAN
Proposed Terms of Reference
A. Objectives
1.        The main objectives of the provincial research master plan (PRMP)
would be to:
(i) establish the provincial and agro-ecological priorities to be used
in developing agricultural, livestock and fisheries and forestry
research programs for the next ten years based on sound economic
analyses and agreed provincial and national sector goals;
(ii) define the means for strengthening research organization,
management, planning, budgeting, financial control and monitoring
and evaluation;
(iii) estimat.e the manpower anu training requirements for the next ten
years to effectively conduct the provincial and zonal agricultural
research programs and projects;
(iv) assess means for improving research staff incentives, promotion and
remuneration, to attract and retain highly qualified and capable
research scientific personnel;
(v) in the light of (i), assess the functions of all agricultural,
livestock, fisheries and forestry research facilities in the
province and provide specific recommendations for their retention,
improvement, or discontinuation, in order to minimize duplication
and promote consolidation;
(vi) detezmine means for promoting more effective linkages with the
extension services, universities, Federal level research
institutions such as PARC, APC and so on, other local institutions,
private sector and with international agricultural research centers
and other institutions;
(vii) determine the means for improving the research and management
information systems for all activities through computer hardware and
software, radio, audiovisual, publications and journals;
(viii) determine funds needed for capital investments and recurrent
expenditures for a finite five years and an indicative further
five-year period to achieve targeted goals;



- 83 -                           ANNEX 2
Appendix 2
Page 2
(ix) collate all the above information in the preparation of a Research
Master Plan to serve as a blueprint for further institutional reform
and development; and
(x) issue the Masterplan no later than the end of the second year of the
Agricultural Research II Project, to serve as the basis for support
by government and external donors.
B.  Scope of Review for Research Master Plan Preparation
2.        Define Provincial Agricultural, Livestock, Fisheries and Forestry
Research Programs and Projects. All research should have a defined purpose in
light of established Provincial and agro-ecological priorities for Pakistani
agriculture, livestock, fisheries and forestry. Therefore, the study should:
(i! review all existing research projects and programs, and advise on
their need in light of provincial priorities to be established at
the outset of this Masterplan preparation and recommend on
additional research needs as deemed necessary;
(ii) assess the research projects and programs to eliminate duplication
and promote consolidation of effort; and
(iii) recommend additions in scope, where necessary to strengthen the
research effort (that is, economic assessments, farm management,
farming systems research, and so on).
3.        Define research organization and management requirements:
(i) assess existing organizational structures and recommend, as
required, further needs for strengthening, to more effectively
conduct the research priority programs;
(ii) assess and make recommendations for improving research management;
and
(iii) review existing planning, budgeting, financial control and
monitoring and evaluation procedures, and make recommendations for
strengthening these procedures.
4.        Estimate manpower and training requirements.  Some research staff
require additional training in their particular disciplines and research
methodology. Therefore there is a need to assess manpower requirements and
training to effectively conduct the agreed research programs and projects over
the next ten years. Similarly, assessment is necessary on the manpower and
training needs to adequately carry out the management, administration,
planning, information, budgeting, financial and monitoring and evaluation
functions.  The review should therefore:
(i) assess the qualifications and experience of all researchers,
management, administrative and support services as to training and
experience;



- 84 -                             ANNEX 2
Appendix 2
Page 3
(ii) estimate staff requirements by discipline and/or functions,
training, location and agro-ecological region for a ten-year period;
and
(iii) recommend additional training needs in Pakistan and abroad,
inclusive of graduate degree training, for ten years and their
estimated costs.
5.        Assess staff incentives improvement.  There is a need to review
means of improving staff incentives to attract and retain professionals of
high caliber. Accordingly, the review would:
(i) review all existing staff salaries, incentives and promotion
procedures;
(ii) recommend realistic means for improving staff incentives to retain
qualified personnel; and
(iii) estimate the incremental costs to recurrent expenditures resulting
from enacting recommended staff incentives improvements.
6.        Assess functions of all research facilities conducting agricultural,
livestock, fisheries and forestry research in the Province. All research
institutes, stations, farms and laboratories need to be individually visited
and assessed for:
(i) justification for its operation as a research facility and its
appropriateness for conducting national and zonal priority research
programs;
(ii) adequacy of existing facilities for offices, laboratories,
greenhouses, workshops and housing;
(iii) adequacy and recommendations on existing laboratory and farm
equipment and vehicles, and for the proper maintenance of all
facilities, research plots, trials and access roads;
(iv) recommendations on consolidation of research facilities, where
possible, for improved efficiency of operations; and
(v) recommendations on restructuring of existing networks among
institutes, stations and farms, to provide a smooth blending of
national research programs, research programs with a provincial
focus, and farmer-oriented applied research
7.        t4ore effective linkages to extension, indigenous institutions and
external research. Linkages between research and extension in the province
need to be strengthened. Similarly, contacts with indigenous institutions
which can assist researchers, especially through training (that is,
universities) need to be enhanced. Contacts with external research
institutions and especially the International Research Centers have been
unsystematic and need to be improved. The review would assess and recommend:



- 85 -                              ANNEX 2
Appendix 2
Page 4
(i) means for more effective linkages with extension, especially through
farming systems research, on-farm trials and periodic meetings to
exchange information;
(ii) methodology for on-going training of extension subject matter
specialists at various research facilities
(iii) methodology for periodic reviews of research progress and farmers
problems;
(iv) methodology for establishing farmers' field days at research
facilities, on-farm trials and on-farm demnnstrations;
(v) methodology for effective research input to mass media releases,
audiovisual aids and documentation to be used by the extension
services;
(vi) means for more effective contacts with universities through
contracts for more basic research geared to specific problems facing
farmers, advanced degree training of research staff, and
collaborative efforts by conducting thesis research programs at
research institutes; and
(vii) establishing improved contacts with external national and
international research organizations, scientific exchange, attending
scientific meetings and presentation of significant scientific
contributions to international societies and in publications.
8.        Improving research and management information systems.  The research
and management information systems are weak, uncoordinated and require
strengthening. Therefore the review would:
(i) review all library and documentation facilities of all research
facilities and recommend measures and costs over a ten-year period,
for strengthening same;
(ii) review all systems for management information, such as planning,
budgeting, accounting, inventories of capital and recurrent items,
and assess feasibility (costs, benefits) of
computerization/mechanization and recommended computer hardware,
software, radio linkages of all research facilities, audiovisual,
and printing facilities; and
(iii) review research station requirements to serve similar needs as well
as for specific research programs.
9.        Budgetary requirements.  Based on the findings and recommendations
with their costs determined in paras. 2 through 8 above! recommendations
should be made on the capital and recurrent expenditures to achieve stated
goals of the Province's agricultural, livestock, fisheries and forestry
research programs for a finite five years and an indicative period of a
further five-year period. Recommendations would include:



- 86 -                             ANNEX 2
Appendix 2
Page 5
(i) capital and recurrent expenditures for each research program and
project;
(ii) capital and recurrent expenditures for each research facility and
headquarters;
(iii) capital expenditures for civil works, laboratory and farm equipment
and vehicles;
(iv) recurrent expenditures for existing and incremental staff salaries
(professional, technical, administrative and labor), materials
(chemicals, fertilizers, pesticides, etc.), office, laboratory and
farm supplies, operation and maintenance of buildings, greenhouses,
workshops, research farms, livestock, equipment, and vehicles;
(v) training, including advanced degree training and attendance of
international scientific meetings and visits to recognized
international research centers and institutions; and
(vi) installation of computer, communications, documentation and
information facilities, to optimize research, administrative,
accounting, monitoring, evaluation and financial management.
C. Research Master Plan Arrangements
10.       The Province would appoint a Head of a Research Working Group and
Subgroups outlined in paras. 2 through 8 above within a secretariat of the
proposed PRO, and with major inputs of research directors and professional
staff. The in-depth review and preparation of the Master Plan would be
conducted with technical assistance provided through nationally and
internationally-recruited consultants. These consultants could be hired
through a recognized international organization such as ISNAR or a private
firm with international experience in conducting such in-depth reviews. Terms
of reference would be def Ined before arrival in the Province and with full
approval of government.
11.       Such a review would be initiated soon after ARP II becomes
effective. It is estimated that, except for the individual designated to lead
the consultants and with prime responsibility for assisting in collating and
preparing the Master Plan, most other consultants would be involved for about
three months each. The final Master Plan approved by government should be
ready ior review by the World Bank no later than the end of the second year of
the project.

PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH PROJECT II
Terms of Reference for Technical Assistance
To Strengthen Planning. Programming. Monitoring and Evaluation
Methodologies and Preparation of Master Research Plans
at National and Pruvincial Levels
A. Introduction of Methodologies for Priority Assessment
in Agricultural Research
1.        A formalized methodology for setting national and provincial
priorities for agricultural research in major commodities and production
factors will be introduced at PARC and the four provincial research
organizations to provide information to guide decision making on such
priorities.
2.        The consultant would help in the process by:
(a)  presenting options for a methodology to be followed;
(b)  initiating social and technical scientists in the detail of the
selected methodology;
(c)  introducing appropriate economic analytical techniques for
determining some of the factors involved in the methodology for
assessing research priorities, and
(d)  assisting a planning team to present to the Research Boards a first
indicative allocation of agricultural research resources amongst
commodity and production factors, as an initial frame of reference
for assessing the size of national and provincial research thrusts.
3.        At the national level, the consultant would be primarily assigned to
PARC (Social Sciences Division) for a period of two months, but would have
close contact also with the Planning Directorate. At the provincial level,
consultants would be appointed for four months in each of the provinces (three
months in Baluchistan) attached to the Secretariat of the newly organized PRO
or Research Board but with close contact with. the provincial AERU.
4.        The consultants would be an agricultural economist/economist with
wide experience of agricultural policy analysis in developing countries, and
experience in determining national and regional research priorities.
B.  Introduction of Proeram-Budgetini s
5.        A formalized program-budgeting methodology will be introduced at
each PRO as a management tool for defining clearly the resources required for

Page 2
6.        The consultants would help in the establishment of a program
budgeting system by:
(a) establishing appropriate procedures for collecting relevant
information on research operational activities;
(b)  producing manuals for procedures in program-budgeting and arranging
training courses for management and research staff in their use;
(c)  testing the procedures with suitable formats for details of
experiments, surveys, and studies;
(d)  assisting in the development of a micro-computer capability to

Plan.

in each case (in one or two periods).
8.        The consultant would be agricultural scientists with research and
management experience in developing countries, preferably with experience of
accounting and spread sheet techniques.
C. Development of Monitoring and Evaluation Methodologies
9.        A Monitoring and Evaluation Cell has been established at PARC and
will be expanded to a Monitoring and Evaluation Unit. Evaluation procedures
for periodic reviews of research institutions have been developed: a further
range of monitoring and evaluation procedures are necessary. In the provinces
monitoring and evaluation units will be established in each PRO.
10.       The consultants would help in the development of new monitoring and
evaluation procedures, and in monitoring performance of evaluative activities
by each Unit, by:
(a) establishing appropriate procedures for collecting relevant
information on research progress;
(b)  working with the research groups to develop and test suitable
indications of research progress at different management levels;
(c)  reviewing completed evaluation of institutions and assisting in
arranging further evaluations;
(d)  assisting in training courses in monitoring and evaluation at PARC
and in Provincial research institutions.
