PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Research Institutions
Federal Research Institutions
1.    Pakistan Agricultural Research Council.  PARC is the major federal
agricultural research organization. Its functions broadly include promoting
and coordinating agricultural research in line with Pakistan's economic and
social development policies, initiating and conducting basic research, and
developing new research in priority areas currently neglected. PARC evolved
from the Indian Council of Agricultural Research, first, as the Food and
Agricultural Council, and in 1977 as the Pakistan Agricultural Research
Council. In 1981 PARC received greater independence and responsibilities.
PARC is continuing to evolve in terms of its capabilities and mandate. PARC
is an autonomous body within MINFA, and works closely with the provincial
Agricultural Departments (PADs), provincial Agricultural Research Institutes
(ARIs), and Agricultural Universities. It is directed by a Board of
Governors, which is headed by the Minister of Food, Agriculture and
Cooperatives, and selected from federal and provinci:l research organizations
and farmers. An Executive Board, consisting of a chairman and five full-time
members representing crops, natural resources, animal sciences, social
services, and finance controls PARC's day-to-day operations.
2.    PARC Institutes.  PARC operates eight institutes, and employs 568
scientists, of whom 97 have PhD degrees (May, 1989). NARC, situated on 560 ha
just outside Islamabad, is PARC's newest (established 1984) and largest
institute, and main operational arm. Its staff undertake a wide range of crop
and livestock research, with emphasis on plant introduction and breeding,
plant protection, agricultural machinery development, economics, and livestock
feeding and crossbreeding. To date NARC's achievements are limited, mainly
because of start-up difficulties, insufficient research prioritizing and
planning, an inadequate management structure, and failure to appoint a
permanent Director-General during the last two years. The USAID-assisted
Management of Agricultural Research and Technology Project (MART) is providing
NARC technical assistance to help prepare a long-term research plan and
improve research administration and managemetLL. As well, NARC still requires
some additional facilities including a new crop sciences building, and
research facilities in Pakistan's northern upland areas.
3.    National Coordinated Research Programs.  Under its mandate to promote,
aid and coordinate agricultural research, PARC currently funds some 30
National Coordinated Research Programs (NCRPs), covering crop production, land
and water resources, forestry and environment, and animal health and
production. They bring together researchers from federal and provincial
organizations, including universities, to plan and undertake coordinated
research. Each NCRP involves at least three participating organizations, and
one researcher from a cooperating unit, who is designated as coordinetor, to



- 91 -
ANNEX 2
Appendix 4
Page 2
advise, encourage, facilitate and support the work.  Several NCRPs have
oubstantially contributad to increasing production and/or efficiency in their
areas, especially the wheat program.  These NURPs havb also iuteracted well
with the international research instetutes (particularly CIMMYT and IRRI under
the wheat and rice programs). However, most NCRPs have insufficient
operational funds, and several do not meet the basic criteria for coordinated
programs, that is, they are extremely small and narrowly focussed, and/or do
not cover complementary, coordinated work but are really a series of
independent research projects, and/or do not address Pakistan's priority
research needs. Nevertheless, PARC is currently reviewing these programs, and
will either redesign or discard them.
4.    Other Federal Agricultural Research Institutions.  Other important
institutions undertaking agricultural research at the federal lezel include:
the Pakistan Central Cotton Committee (PCCC), which is responsible for
research and development in cotton growing, manufacturing and marketing; AEC
with three institutes studying the application of nuclear techniques to
agriculture and biological, research; Pakistan Forestry Institute (PFI), which
is responsible for forestry research throughout Pakistan; Water and Power
Authority (WAPDA) which does research on waterlogging and salinity, and the
Ministry of Science and Technology which undertakes research on irrigated
agriculture through the Pakistan Council of Research in Water Resources
(PCRWR).
Provincial Research Institutes
5.    General.  All the provinces have their own agricultural research
services covering crops, livestock, forestry, and in some instances,
fisheries. These services are generally administered within separate
provincial line departments, which usually conduct research in isolation from
each other. In North West Frontier province (NWFP) the Agricultural
University is responsible for crop and livestock research. All provinces
place major emphasis on crop research, and each has an apex agricultural (that
is, crop) research institute (ARI) with a supporting network of substations
and experimental farms.  Some have commodity-oriented institutes.  Punjab,
Sind and NWFP have agr'cultural universities, however, the r.search programs
at Punjab and Sind are mainly limited to MSc and PhD thesis preparation.
While several research institutes employ large numbers of staff, they have few
experienced researchers with PhD qualifications, or trained research leaders.
6.    Puniab.  Of the four provinces, Punjab has by far the largest and best
trained agricultural research staff. The Ayub Agricultural Research Institute
(AARI) is Punjab's main crop research institute. AARI is headed by a
Director-General (DG) who reports to the Secretary of Agriculture.  Besides
its main campus at Faisalabad, AARI has 18 substations.  It employs 820
scientists of whom 25 have PhDs (October, 1988). AARI's main programs are



- 92 -
ANNEX2
Appendix 4
Page 3
wheat, rice and vegetable breeding, and in collaboration with CIMMYT,'/ IRRI,2'
and PARC, has successfully introduced high yielding varieties (HYV) of wheat
and rice to provincial farmers. Nevertheless, the provincial citrus industry
is presently facing a serious problem of citrus decline in many orchards, and
work is urgently required to properly identify and treat this outbreak.
7. A separate Livestock Department is responsible for livestock research.
Its main research center is the Livestock Production Research Institute at
Bahadur Fagar, while its other institutions include the Animal Nutrition
Center, Lahore; Veterinary Research Institute, Lahort; and Poultry Research
Institute, Rawalpindi. A DG Livestock Research heads these institutions, and
reports to the Secretary, Department of Livestock.
8. Sind. The Sind Agriculture Department is responsible for both crop and
livestock research. It has three agencies undertaking crop research, that is,
ARI at Tandojam, which has eight substations and five research farms; the
Horticultural Research Institute at Mirpukhas; and the Rice Research Institute
at Dokri. Institutions undertaking livestoc'. research include the Poultry
Research and VaccinA Unit, Karachi; Central Veterinary Laboratory, Tandojam;
and six livestock experimental farms throughout thie province.
9. Rice research at Dokri has been Sind's main, and most successful, crop
research program. ,The Institute was considerably improved in 1977, and
maintains a linkage with IRRI. Other research programs have generally
undertaken routine, repetitive work with little attention paid to farmers'
problems. The Department currently has many unfilled sanctioned research
positions, and this is severely constraining crop research, particularly in
horticulture. Livastock research has been limited. The Department has also
conducted some fisheries investigations.
10. Baluchistan. Baluchistan Agriculture Department has a single crop
research institute (ARI) which has concentrated on horticulture, with some
work on irrigated wheat breeding. The Institute is weak, with only 43 of its
85 scientific positions filled. Some 18 months ago, about 40 of its
scientists working on horticulture were split from the Institute to form a
separate Deciduous Fruits Development Center (DFDC)(FAO-assisted). A DG of
Research heads ARI and reports to the Secretary of Agriculture, while DFDC
staff report to DG of Agriculture. A separate Livestock Department is
responsible for livestock research, but has not yet undertaken any. It does,
however, produce vaccine at its Veterinary Research Institute. The Arid Zone
Research Institute (AZRI), a PARC institute located near Quetta, is
undertaking much needed livestock and rainfed cropping research, and has some
good progzams.
11. NWFP. Recently, with assistance from the USAID-financed Transformation
and Integration of Provincial Agricultural Network Project (TIPAN), the
International Maize and Wheat Improvement Center, Mexico.
International Rice Research Institute, Philippines.



- 93 -
ANNEX 2
Appendix 4
Page 4
Government of NiFP (GONWFP) transferred resFonsibility for crop and livestock
research from the Department of Agriculture to the Agricultural University,
Peshawar (AUP). This is an evolving situation and will take several years yet
to complete. AUP's main crop research group is ARI at Tarnab, which has five
substations. ARI employs 220 scientists, of whom 9 have PhDs (June 30, 1984).
Its major achievements have been in breeding temperate fruit varieties, while
it has also worked on oilseeds, maize, and fodders. The Veterinary Research
Institute at Peshawar is AUP's main livestock research group.  So far,
however, they have been more involved in livestock husbandry and vaccine
production than in research.
Private Sector Agricultural Research
12. The private sector invests little in agricultural research, although some
large cotton farms and specialized agroindustriaL businesses (for example,
Rafthan Maize Products) test, adapt, and apply research results and help to
disseminate improved techniques to cooperating and/or neighboring farmers.
Private sugar mills in Sind have established a small research and advisory
organization to investigate and advise upon agronomic practices.

PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH PROJECT II
Provincial Agricultural Research Board
1.        A Provincial Agricultural Research Board (PRB) would be established
(consistent with the laws and regulations of the province and the Government
of Pakistan). Its main objective would be to foster an integrated approach to
research planning in agriculture, livestock, irrigation, fisheries, and
forestry to provide for more effective allocation of resources for generating
improved technology to solve the provinces' agricultural sector problems.
Functions
2.        The functions of the PRB would be to:
(1) provide overall planning, programming, resource allocation,
monitoring and evaluation of research;
(2) approve all research programs for funding by the provinces'
Department of Finance and from externally financed projects;
(3) identify provincial research priorities and broad thrusts (within
national guidelines);
(4) establish guidelines for allocating research funds to identified
research priorities;
(5) provide overall coordination and annual issuance of provincial
research master plans (PRKP);
(6)  conduct overall monitoring and external evaluation of research
programs' impact on the provinces' agricultural sector;
(7) conduct monitoring and overall coordination of the provinces'
externally financed agricultural research projects;
(8) institute regulations approved by the provincial government
affecting appointments terms and conditions of service of
professional agricultural research personnel to attract and retain



- 95 -
Appendix 5
Page 2
highly qualified scientific personnel and to create an environment
(collegiate style of management) conducive to conducting research
for solving the provinces' agricultural, livestock, irrigation,
fishery and forestry problems; and
(9) enter into contracts or agreements for the supply of technical
services, assistance or other services to the agricultural research
system of Punjab.
Management
3.        The overall direction and control of the PRB will be by a chairman
and 13 members. Its Chairman would be the province's Planning and Development
Depa'rtment's Chairman. The other PRB members would be limited to the
Secretaries of Agriculture, Livestock, Fisheries, Forestry, Irrigation,
Finance, Vice Chancellor of the Agricultural University, Director General for
Agricultural Research, Director General for Livestock Research, Director
General for Extension, Chief of PARC's Planning Directorate, senior researcher
in agriculture and senior researcher in livestock. Appointments would be for
three years. The PRB would meet at least twice a year presided over by the
Chairman or, in his absence, by a member of the Board designated. The first
Board meeting of the year (January) would consider policy issues relating to
scientific and technical matters whereas the second meeting (July) would
consider budget and administrative matters.
4.        An executive committee would be established to serve as the
principal administrative body of PRB responsible for discharging all functions
of PRB as described in paragraph 2 and any additional functions designated by
the Provincial Government. It would be headed by a chief executive and would
include five full time PRB staff appointed to the Board. The Executive
Committee would establish a secretariat to oversee all financial,
administrative, planning, programming, monitoring and evaluation functions of
PRB. The Executive Committee would meet once a month and a other times as
required.
5.        The Executive Committee wouid establish technical Committees to
review research proposals and ongoing research in designated commodities and
disciplines and recommend them to PRB's Board for approval with its
concomitant allocation of resources. It would manage and disburse funds
allocated to the PRB from externally financed projects in accordance with
project agreements and assume overall control of implementation of such
projects. It would also collate and issue annually updated PRMPs and conduct
impact evaluations of research programs on the agricultural sector of the
province.



- 96 -
ANNEX 2
Appendix 5
Page 3
6.        The chief executive of the Executive Committee of PRB would be a
senior scientist in BPS20 or higher, and his appointment would be made by the
Board through open competition from all eligible senior research scientists of
the province. Criteria for selection would include demonstrated scientific
and administrative capabilities, a minimum grade of BPS20 with at least three
years of service in that grade, a minimum of ten years of service as a
research scientist, a PhD degree from a University of good standing, and other
provincial governmental regulations required of such a higher level position.
The Chief Executive of the PRB would serve for a fixed term of five years.
7.        An alternative to establishing an executive committee would be
establishing a series of full time chdrtered committees to carry out clearly
defined functions such as new technology investigation, planning and finance,
monitoring and evaluation, recruitment and human resource development, and
professional education. These functions could be divided into three distinct
classes, that is: (i) productive; (ii) regulatory; and (iii) cocrdinative.
The PRB would still have a chief executive or Secretary-General, plus a full
time secretariat, to handle the day-to-day Board operations. The
establishment of full time chartered committees could reduce inter-
departmental and inter-personal rivalries, which are often associated with
Boards which have members from several different government departments. This
option could be more fully explored during the preparation of the provincial
research master plans.
Finance
8.       A Fund will be established to which all sums received by the PRB
shall be credited and from which all expenditures of the PRB will be debited.
The funds of the PRB would come through the provincial department of finance
and would consist of:
(1) funds from the Provincial and Federal Governments for operations of
the Board, Executive Committee and Secretariat members and staff.
Also for funding technical committees, and technical assistance;
(2)  funds from externally financed projects for disbursement and for
overall implementation as per project agreements;
(3) funds, donations, endowment, contribvtions, aid and assistance given
by other organizations; and
(4) receipts from other sources.
PRB funds should be non-lapsable. PRB may open accounts in any scheduled bank
in Pakistan. The accounts would be audited in accordance with government
procedures.

Reports
9.        The PRB shall furnish to the Provincial Government by the end of
each financial year a report on the activities of PRB and a summary of
accounts for that year. An updated Research Master Plan collating the
research plans from each Provincial Secretariat (Agriculture, Livestock,
Irrigation, Fisheries, Forestry) for the next te: years including estimated
budgets foir the first five years will also be submitted annually. Also, every
five years, national and international scientists of repute would conduct an
external review, in relation to previously set targets and goals of the
agricultural research programs and their management.

PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Horticu!tural Research Programs
1.        This Annex is part of a longer report in the Project Implementation
Volume which details the horticultural research programs, including giving
cost estimates for each individual agency undertaking horticultural research
under the project.
2.        The project will strengthen horticultural research at the provincial
and federal levels to meet the internal demand for high-quality fruits,
vegetables and ornamentals, and for export markets. It would provide support
for key research programs through training abroad, field and laboratory
equipment, transport, some minor civil works and incremental operating funds
for the six years of the project.
3.        Horticultural research, especially in the provinces, must be
oriented to working on problems facing its fruit and vegetable growers to
maximize quality and productivity. Production of nursery stock of fruit
seedlings and vegetable seeds for sale to farmers, although an important
service, should not be a function of the research stations and should be
phased out and transferred to private nurseries and the extension services by
the end of the second year of the project. Research stations would be the
source of lasic nursery stock and seeds which would be given to the private
nurseries and extension services for propagation of certified materials.
General
4.        The overall goal of horticuitural research is increased and more
efficient production and supply of high-quality fruits, vegetables ard cut
flowers to satisfy local demand and export markets. Achieving this aim will
require good cooperation and collaboration between researchers, not only
within horticulture but also between disciplines, to develop production
packages for priority crops that will give good results under a range of
conditions. Horticultural research capabilities in Pakistan are adequate for
low-quality horticultural production for present local needs, but must be
improved to satisfy th, demands of export markets and an expanding population.
Increases in research facilities, equipment and operational funds, togAt-bhr
with staff redeployment and training are necessary for increased production
and higher quality standards in horticulture.
5.        Research managem-nt must be improved at all levels so that programs
will be more effective and the best use is made of available resources.
Careful attention must be given to establishing priorities which will be
refined and updated annually in the national and provincial master research
plans, so that research results will be of the greatest value to horticultural
industries. Good management vill have a mechanism for problem identification



- 99 -
ANNEX 3-
Page 2
and planning of appropriate research and development strategies. Interaction
with agricultural leaders, growers, extension personnel and other researchers,
along with personal observations and literature review will help in this
regard. Proper research management will include active and effective research
coordination programs, both nationwide and within provinces, to avoid
excessive overlap, to compare results and to exchange information. Joint
projects could be established between institutions within provinces and
nationwide to address problems of national importance.
6.        The shortage of adequately trained and experienced personnel for
research projects is a restraint in most areas. Optimum use of present
personnel is essential, and additional training will be required for greater
effectiveness in future programs. Overseas training would be primarily for
post-groditatA training to the Ph-D level over a 4-year period with provision
for returning to Pakistan in the second and third year in order to do Ph.D
thesis research on a Pakistan horticultural research problem. Short-term
training would also include special training or visits at appropriate leading
research centers. This will involve substantial planning and arrangement.
Returning trainees would benefit by collaboration with experienced researchers
to help them in establishing their programs. On-the-job training by
short-term technical advisors would also be beneficial.
7.        It is vitally important for researchers to keep up with current
development in their own and related disciplines. This can be accomplished in
various ways. Libraries, documentation centers, personal books, journals,
trade magazines, and newsletters provide the necessary literature. Short
courses, seminars, conferences, meetings and personal contacts can be just as
valuable. Linkages with leading research institutes where the same or similar
crops are studied is also extremely useful to research programs.
8.        Cultivar and rootstock improvement has received the greatest
attention in research programs.  These efforts should continue because of the
importance of superior cultivars, especially for export. However, a more
efficient system of screening, seleccion and development must be used since
the program is being carried out at the expense of other projects of equal or
greater importance. Breeding should not occupy a major part in cultivar
improvement programs since this is an expensive undertaking and numerous new
cultivars can be obtained from breeding programs elsewhere. The National
Agricultural Research Center (NARC) should undertake a national role in
cultivar and rootstock introduction and distribution with the advise and
coliaboration of the worid s leading experts. Researcel it) propagation should
be directed toward developing more rapid and efficient production of healthy,
high-quality, true-to-type fruit trees. Research institutes should also be
responsible for maintaining healthy, true-to-type parent trees to be used for
multiplication and sale by private nurseries.
9.        Research in horticulture and ornamental plants has largely been
neglected in spite of the increasing opportunities for export markets. This
research should be increased in all provinces but concentrated more heavily
where potential is greatest. Post-harvest crop losses and reductions in



- 100 -
hOEX-3
4age 3
quality from improper handling, storage and shipping are problems ir. all
provinces. Research in post-harvest physiology and technology should be
stressed to address these problems. NARC should take national leadership in
this work. Marketing problems are related but should be carried out in
conjunction with research workers in the AERUs.
10.       Research programs of different institutions will vary according to
their responsibilities and capabilities. NARC should concentrate mostly on
basic research requiring highly trained scientists and sophisticated equipment
to obtain basic information and to develop higher technology. Some related
applied research would also be done. Provincial research institutions would
concentrate more on applied research on problems affecting production in their
respective areas. Some basic and adaptive research could be done, depending
on need and their capabilities. The universities should undertake both basic
and applied research according to their capabilities and in line with their
responsibilities as educational institutions. A brief resume of research
topic priorities and requirements is provided for NARC and each province in
the Project Implementation Volume, Annex I, Table 17, pages 1-17. It is based
on submissions from the provinces and NARC and from mission observations.
11.       Technical assistance by successful research-rs in various aspects of
horticultural science will be required to ensure the success of the various
programs described here. A horticulturist with a strong background in fruit
production, especially tropical and sub-tropical, but with a good knowledge of
other horticultural crops will be the key person. Also needed for short terms
will be a breeder/geneticist, a crop/ost-harvest physiologist, and a plant
tissue analysis/niutrition specialist. The various technical advisors would be
responsible to PARC and be housed at NARC. They would be part of the
nationally coordinated research effort in horticulture and, as such, would
have nationwide responsibilities with their time divided between NARC and the
provinces according to need. PARC would receive support for and administer
the program to ensure an effective, coordinated effort. Details on technical
advisors are presented in Appendix 1. In addition, between 12-18 man-months
of short-term technical assistance would be provided to each province to
assist in bringing outstanding horticulturists to advise and train on specific
research problems.
12.       PARC and NARC.  The Pakis!an Agricultural Research Cosincil (PARC)
will establish its role as national coordinator for horticultural research.
NARC will be the main source for introduction and distribution of cultivars,
rootstocks and seeds to the provinces. It would be strengthened by
establishing facilities for biotechnology and post-harvest horticulture to
conduct basic research in maintaining fruits, vegetables and ornamentals in
good condition between producer and the ultimate consumer. This would involve
biochemical, physiological and tissue culture studies on ripening, senescence,
pest control, storage and processing techniques to produce high-quality
products for market. NARC would appoint a Horticultural Research Director
(Principal Research Officer) who would concentrate on pre-harvest technology
and an Assistant Director who would concentrate on post-harvest technology.
Funds would include training abroad of six Ph.Ds, 36 mm of short courses and



- 101 -
ANNEX 3
Page 4
25 mm of technical assistance (Appendix 1). It would include field equipment
transport and laboratory equipment to establish post-harvest physiology,
biotechnology (including tissue culture laboratorias, processing, storage and
greenhouse facilities and incremental operating funds.
13.       Horticultural Research at NARC should be reorganized for greater
effectiveness and efficiency (Chart 1). A Horticultural Research Director
(Principal Research Officer) would have overall responsibility for research
activities. An assistant director would be involved in planning of activities
in post-harvest technology in addition to his own research activities.
Research activities would be divided among three sections: (a) horticulture,
(b) biotechnology, and (c) post-harvest physiology. Each section would
involve investigations in fruit, vegetables and floriculture. Appropriate
projects would be undertaken to address problems of national importance.
There would be a section leader within each section who would report to the
Director or Assistant Director as well as carry on some research activity.
There will be a need to redeploy some existing staff.
14.       PARC would have the responsibility of coordinating horticultural
research in Pakistan and must become much more active in this regard. The
coordinator would t.ave key contact peopls within the provinces who are working
on related projects. Coordination would include planning, review and
organization of discussion groups, meetings and conferences. It would also
Involve data collection, monitoring all horticultural research programs and
dissemination of results and information. Joint projects should be developed
on provincial and national levels and regular conferences should be held to
discuss programs and results in similar research afid rotated among the
provinces and PARC. Coordination of horticultural research in Pakistan will
involve considerable travel, correspondence and printing of informational
materials.
15.       The Horticulture Section would be involved in cultivar improvement
by both traditional and non-traditional methods, in developing new propagation
and nursery practices that would result in more rapid and efficient production
of higher quality nursery plants. They would also establish and maintain a
nucleus of pathogen-free stocks of superior new cultivars, or see to their
distribution to the appropriate agroecological zones in the provinces. The
Biotechnology Section would determine the role of endogenous hormones in
controlling vegetative growtt, flowering, fruit set and fruit drop and
discover practical methods of obtaining desirable crop load and higher quality
by application of plant grow-ch regulators.  Tisstte nulttire and
micropropagation could be used for propagation of plants which are difficult
to propagate by other means and for obtaining disease-free plant material from
heat therapy or other means. Criteria for determining the nutritional needs
of major crops through plant tissue analysis could also be developed. The
Post-harvest Physiology Section will undertake basic and some applied research
to address the problems of post-harvest fruit quality and loss. This will
involve studies to determine the effects of cultural and climatic factors and
handling, storage and shipping methods on the physiology and quality of the
fruits and vegetables. Methods of ensuring higher quality produce for foreign



- 102 -
ANNEX 3
Page 5
and eomestic markets will be developed. Coordination within and between these
sections and with the provinces is essential for maximum success.
16.       Funjab.  The project would provide for strengthening of fruit
cultivar improvement and vegetable multiple cropping research at the Ayub
Agricultural Research Institute at Faisalabad and for developing the citrus
research station at Sahiwal, using a multidisciplinary approach for
determining the cause and control of citrus decline, which is significantly
reducing citrus production. Funds of about one million rupees would also be
provided to the Agriculture University's Department of Horticulture, based on
proposals acceptable to the Bank for studies on malformation control of mango
fruit and control of flowering, fruiting, dwarf rootstocks, interstocks and
nutrition of citrus. For horticultural research at the institutes, fur.ds
would be provided for training abroad of 5 Ph.Ds, 33 mm short courses and for
18 mm of technical assistance. Field and laboratory equipment, transport and
incremental operating expenses would be provided over the project's life.
17.       Particular attention should be devoted to supporting citrus research
in Punjab because of its importance and because there are some major problems
which can affect its future.  Citrus decline is the most serious problem
because it is affecting a large number of trees and its cause and contributing
factors are unknown. This research will require a concerted effort, not only
by horticulturists, but also by scientists in other disciplines. A cultivar
improvement program that will find a suitable seedless replacement for Kinnow
and extend the cropping season for citrus should also be supported. An
expansion of post-harvest physiology also is needed to deal with problems in
fruit loss and quality which will be supported with U.K. funds. This should
be part of the Nationallyr Coordinated Program. Research into vegetable
multiple cropping systems for increasing per acre yields and grower incomes
should also be supported.
18.       Sind.  Funds will be provided for strengthening horticultural
research at the vegetable research substation six km from Mirpurkhas; for
mango and citrus at the new research station at Sakrand; for banana, papaya
and chiku at a new station to be purc%ased by the Sind Government; for guava
at Naudero; and jujube at Tandojam.  Laboratory and field equipment will be
provided for pre-harvest and post-harvest research on the above-mentioned
r-ommodities. Training abroad would include 5 Ph.Ds and 30 mm of short courses
as well as 12 mm of short term technical assistance. Incremental operating
expenses would also be made available during the project's life. About one
million rupees would be provided to the Agricultural Univerity's Horticulture
Department at Tandojam based on p:oposals acceptable to the Bank for research
on control of malformation of mango, and control of flowering, fruiting, dwarf
rootstocks, interstocks and nutrition of mango.
19.       The vegetable research substation, 6 km from Mirpurkhas, would be
provided with a tubewell to lower the water table from its present level of
0.75 to 1 m from the surface and the pumped brackish water would be disposed
of in an acceptable manner, which should improve the vegetable research on the
available 8 ha. No funding is presently being provided for the main



- 10: -
Page 6
horticultural research station at Mirpurkhas because of the high water table,
waterlogging and salinity which is seriously affecting the mango trees and
stopping any viable research programs. However, an outreach program in
farmers' fields for Mirpurkhas research staff could be considered when
preparing the provincial research master plan and financing the costs of such
a program from the incremental operating funds being provided to Sind province
under the project.
20.       The Mango and Citrus Research Station appears acceptable with water
table depths averaging 6 m. A tubewell will be provided co supply additional
water to the research station since the existing tubewell is not considered
sufficient for the total area. However, salt encrustation of the soil around
the tubewell area and the ridges of the irrigation channels and the total
soluble salts (TSS) of 700 to 1,400 ppm indicates that the water is not very
satis~factory. Accordingly, funds have been provided for constructing a
connector channel to the surface canal system to mix this sweet water with or
substitute it for tubewell water, which should be periodically tested with the
provided electric conductivety meter. The 8 ha presently available is
insufficient for mango and citrus research. Agreement has been reached that
the adjacent 8 ha presenLly with the Agricultural Training Institute would be
transferred during 1989 to the Sakrand Mango and Citrus Research Station.
21.       Bananas are the second most important fruit crop (after mango) in
Sind Province accounting for about 84% of total production in Pakistan. The
proposed research station at Sujawal is unacceptable for such banana research.
Firstly, it is in an area remote from the main banar.a producing areas of Sind
Province and research's primary purpose is to assist existing banana giowers
with improved technology. Secondly, the water is quite saline according to
the Soil Chemist Report which shows from 3,500 ppm to 6,100 ppm. Accordingly,
it has been agreed that the goverrnment of Sind will purchase a minimum of 10
ha and preferably 16 to 20 ha in the banana growing region. Funds will be
provided for building an office block and these funds will be made available
upon the purchase of suitable land, acceptable to IDA, for banana, papaya and
chiku r:esearch.
22.       Major support should go towards strengthening programs of the Sind
Horticultural Research Institute with its various tropical fruit research
stations. The main aim of horticultural research in Sind is to improve yields
and quality of priority crops, particularly those that have the best potential
for export markets. T-he particular studies needed will depend on the species.
23.       Since high water tables and salinity are common in some parts of
Sind, it would be beneficial to conduct cooperative experiments with the soils
sectiont at ARI Tandojam on low volume irrigation systems and water control.
Benefits could include:
(i) greater water efficiency and greater land area irrigated;
(ii) lowering the water table and improving tree growth;



- 104 -
ANNEX 3
Page 7
(iii) reductions in salinity problems; and
(iv) reduction in nitrogen losses.
24.       NWFP.  Horticultural research in the province will be strengthened
by support to fruit research at Mingora station, vegetable research at the
Tarnab Agricultural Research Institute and floriculture research at the
University's Department of Horticulture. Research on mature fruit trees would
be concentrated at Mingora and additional land obtained of at least 20 acres
to augment the existing 26 acres. Tarnab should focus on introduction of new
fruit scions and rootstocks and improved propagation techniques as well as
expanding its vegetable research. At Mingora, research would involve
evaluation of improved fruit cultivars, cultivation practices, propagation
techniques, development of low chilling varieties for peaches and other fruits
and improved quality and shelf life. Vegetable research at Tarnab would
develop high yielding cultivars including hybrids, early forcing using plastic
tunnels and improved seed production techniques. Development of new and
improved ornamentals through introduction, hybridization, evaluation and rapid
mass production techniques would be tested using tissue culture, greenhouse
and propagating equipment and other facilities at the University Department of
Horticulture. Training abroad in the research areas mentioned would include 6
Ph.Ds and 30 mm of short courses. Technical assistance of 12 mm would be
available as well as equipment, transport and incremental operating funds for
six years.
25.       The Agricultural Research Institute at Tarnab is better suited to
vegetables than fruit, and research on priority vegetable crops should be
expanded there with appropriate equipment and transport for off-station
trials. Tree crop research should be phased out with the possible exception of
screening new low-chill deciduous fruit cultivars on the best drained land; a
study on growing f-tit trees in soil with a high water table by using better
water management or flood tolerant rootstocks; and research to develop more
rapid and efficient propagation of nursery trees. Mingora is climatically
much better located for most deciduous fruit crop research. Research on
apples, pears, plums, walnuts, apricots and possibly some other deciduous
fruits should be expanded there, particularly with cultivars which mature
before the rainy season starts in July. This will require an increase in
off-station experiments with cooperative growers since there is little land
available at the research station at present.
26.       Baluchistan.  Vegetable and ornamental horticultural research w3uld
be supported at the Agricultural Research Institute, Sariab. It would include
studies on introduction of vegetable and ornamental materials and developing
improved technology for high quality production which is adaptive to the
agroecological conditions which exist in the province. The present project
would provide for training abroad of 4 Ph.Ds, 24 mm of short courses and 12 mm
of short-term technical assistance. Field, laboratory and transport equipment
and incremental operating costs for six years will also be provided.

1.      Consultant on Horticultural Research
Oualifications and Experience. ;he consultant should .old a research degree
in horticulture and have a strong backgr,.lmd in fruit crops, particularly
sub-tropical and tropical fruits, and good knowledge of vegetable production.
He should have at least ten years experience in applied horticultural research
and at least two years experience working with fruit crops in developing
countries.
Station. The consultant will be responsible to the Pakistan Agricultural
Research Council, Islamabad.
Period of Work. The consultant will work in-country for 18 months over the
six year-project period, preferably spending six months in-country during the
first year and with flexibility in the use of the remaining time to coincide
with critical developmental stages of priority crops. At least six weeks
would be spent in-country in any one year during the project period.
Duties. The consultant will assist in the planning and implementation of
research in horticultural crops at both national and provincial research
institutions and in the interpretation and presentation of research results.
He will serve as resource person for information and programs in horticultural
crops, making available to Pakistani scientists the latest technology in
horticultural science and all relevant documentation.
He will aid in the development of more effective coordinated horticultural
research programs for Pakistan and assist in seminars, conferences,
information exchange and publication.
He will aid in estab.ishing a more effective linkage between research and
extension and in providing for a more effective transfer of appropriate
production technology.
He will review horticultural research programs and provide advice for making
them more effective.
He will help identify problems, determine research priorities and assist in
designing experiments which will provide effective answers.
He will advise in the selection of appropriate equipment and materials and
provide training in their use.

He will provide advice and aid in the introduction of appropriate plant
materials, help establish a more efficient system of cultivar evaluation and
aid in establishing an effective clonal protection program.
He will assist in liaison with related disciplines and programs with the

and to developing better crop managemint systems.
He will aid in establishing stronger contacts with leading horticultural

2.  Consultant on Ph_siologv of Horticultural Crops
Oualifications and ExRerience. The consultant should hold a research degree

experience in crop and post-harvest physiology of horticultural crops.
Station. The consultant will be responsible to the Pakistan Agricultural
Research Council, Islamabad.
Period of Work. The consultant will work in-country for four months over a
period of two years.
Duties.  The consultant will assist in the planning, establishment and
operation of crop and post-harvest physiology laboratories at NARC and aid in
developing better capabilities in the provinces. He will also assist in
designing experimental programs.
He will, with the collaboration of the Horticulture Section, assist in
evaluating environmental and cultural practices that influence post-harvest
quality.
He will aid in studies to determine the biochemistry and physiology of
chemical regulators for growth and development of horticultural crops.
He will assist in studies on the mov,,ication of growth and development
processes in horticultural crops through application of chemicals.
He will aid in studies of biochemical and physiological processes during
maturation and post-harvest storage that influence quality.
He will assist in evaluating the effects of post-harvest prestorage treatments
and storage conditions on biochemical and physiological processes that
influence storage life.
He will train Pakistani scientists in instrumentation and proper techniques
used in crop and post-harvest physiology studies.

PAKISTAJN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Livestock Research Programs
A. Proiect ComRonents
General
1.          This annex is part of a longer report in the Project
Implementation Volume which details the livestock research programs. The
report covers, among other things, present status of livestock research in
Pakistan and research priorities, and includes detailed cost tables for each
individual component the project would finance.
Nationally Coordinated Programs
2.          Dairy cattle Drogram.  The project will support research to
increase milk production from dairy cattle conditional upon a review of past
activities and the formulation of a research plan for the future. The budget
has been framed on the basis that many of the present participating
institutions would continue to be involved in the program and the items of
equipment are of a general nature and not dependent on specific details of the
program. Support for this program will be conditional upon a review of the
program.
3.          Buffalo program.  The project will support research to increase
the productivity of buffaloes. Support will be conditional upon the program
containing elements of reproductive physiology, selection, nutrition of
breeders, fattening excess male calves and cull cows and animal health. Cost
estimates include transport for an out-reach program and equipment.
PARC/NARC
4.          The project will support the development of the AZRI sub-station
at Umarkot, plus the provision of key items of equipment, transport and
operating expenses.
5.          The project will assist AZRI Headquarters in the purchase of
equipment, staff training and research operating funds. These components are
complementary to the ICARDA-AZRI project.
6.          The Range and Watershed Section at NARC will be given support in
the form of field equipment, transport and operating expenses for the
development of a ranching model for Pothwar tract, Islamabad district.
7.          The project will provide technical assistance for the
forage/pasture, rangeland feed resources research program under the auspices
of PARC.
8.          The project all also provide funds for a review of research on
indigenous breeds of livestock - particularly sheep, goats and cattle.



- 110 -
ANNEX 4
Page 2
Puni ab
9.          The project will provide equipment for the Faculty of Veterinary
Science, Agricultural University, Faisalabad, to improve post-graduate
training. The Faculty of Animal Husbandry will be similarly assisted.
10.         The project will provide key items to equip a virus laboratory at
the College of Veterinary Sciences, Lahore, and also some new items of
equipment for the Nutrition Department at the same Institution.
11.         The Feeding and Management Division at the Livestock Production
Research Institute will be assisted by the provision of transport and
operating funds.
12.         The project provides for the training of four PH.D. students and 4
M.Sc. students overseas. Details of the areas of training and the
Institutions involved are provided in Table 1. There is also provision for
attendance at short courses, overseas post-doctoral tr.ining, and local
training. Six man-months of technical assistance is also provided.
Sind
13.         Laboratory equipment will be purchased for the Department of
Animal Nutrition, Faculty of Animal Husbandry, Agricultural University,
Tandojam, and equipment associated with livestock feeding studies will be
purchased for the Livestock Experimental Station attached to the same Faculty.
14.         The project will purchase equipment for the Animal Reproduction
Department which will improve the standard of post-graduate training.
15.         The project will :issist the Livestock and Fisheries Department to
establish r_search facilities at the livestock farms at Tando Muhammed Khan
and Rohri by providing key items to equip a nutrition laboratory, and
operating funds to enable the laboratory to function adequately.

22.         The implementation of the livestock component of the project will

23.         The purchase of vehicles and equipment is, in general, phased for
the first year or two of the project. In most cases funding has been provided
for the more robust and suitable 4WD vehicles (approximately PRs450,000 each)
rather than the cheaper, unsuftable models costing approximately PRsl70,000.
This has been done with the clear intention of providing the most suitable
vehicles for the purposes for which they will be used.
C. Staff Training Needs
24.         This is a major cost in this component,  It was only at the time
of the pre-appraisal mission that information became available on the number
of staff in educational and research  organizations and their qualifications.
Information also became available at that time on the numbers of students who
had completed their training under PARC sponsorship and those currently
undertaking training.  It was pointed out during project preparation that:-
(i) three times more students have been/are being trained at Ph.D.
level compared with M.Sc. level.
(ii) three times more students are sent for animal production courses
rather than animal health, and that
(iii) three out of every four students are domiciled in the Punjab.
25.         The proposals under ARP II do not fit this pattern for the
following reasons:-
(i) for some Provinces and for some areas of training requested, M.Sc.
tr_.ning is more appropriate, resulting in a higher proportion of
M.Sc. trainees compared with Ph.D. trainees under ARP II,
(ii) more trainees were requested for animal production courses than
for animal health courses, and
(iii) there was a conscious decision to offer more trainees positions to
Sind, Baluchistan and NWFP than Punjab, in the hope that, with

AMENX 4
Page 4
additional in-country training, these traineeships would be filled
and in time these Provinces would have a more appropriate number
of scientists with overseas post-graduate training.
26.       In relation to overseas post-graduate training, Ph.D. candidates
would undertake their experimental work in Pakistan, and the student's
supervisor would be encouraged to travel to the Institution in Pakistan where
the student would work, in order to familiarize himself with all aspects
associated with that part of the Ph.D. program. For the purposes of the
experimental work, there should be a co-supervisor based in the Pakistan
Institution where the work would be carried out. Funds for travel and
expenses of both supervisor and student are included in training costs. Costs
for Ph.D. programs overseas are based on four years of study. It is
considered that this is a more realistic figure than three years, based on
past experience.
27.       Funds have been provided for staff to undertake short-term studies
and post-doctoral training overseas, without specifying the details. It is
expected that the normal length of these programs would be between three and
six months. Funds provided for local training are intended for use at both
Pakistan Institutions and for the attendance at Workshops and similar
activities organized by consultants under the Technical Assistance provided
for in the livestock component.
D. Technical Assistance
28.       The project will provide funds for technical assistance in a number
of areas as follows:-
(i) Dairy Cattle Breeding: Data Analysis and Breeding Programs;
(ii) Pasture, Forage and Rangeland Feed Resources Research;
(iii) animal Breeding: Development of Indigenous Breeds;
(iv) Ruminant Nutrition;
(v) Animal Health.
The Terms of Reference for Consultants are provided in Appendix 1.
29.       The allocations for technical assistance in dairy cattle (cross)
breeding, development of indigenous breeds, and pasture, forage and rangeland
research have been assigned to PARC as this assistance would be for national
research programs, and the intention is that, both NARC and the Provinces
would be the beneficiaries of this assistance. On the other hand, the intent
is that for other technical assistance nominated for livestock research, the
Provinces determine what proportion of their total allocation they use for
ruminant nutrition and what proportion for animal health.

TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE - TERMS OF REFERENCE
A.  Consultant on Pastures. Forages and Rangeland Feed Resources
Qualifications and Experience.  The consultant should have, a research degree
and preferably be a staff member of an International research institute or a
University department or equivalent, and working at the animal production -
feed resource interface. The consultant should have experience working in
developing countries with different climatic environments and where rangelands
are communally grazed.
Duties. The consultant will be responsible to PARC, Islamabad. The
consultant will work in-country for 5 months over the 6-year project period.
The consultant will undertake a review of research, from an animal production
perspective, on feed resources in the pasture, forage and rangeland
categories. The review should contain appropriate recommendations in relation
to this research.
The consultant will assist in the planning and implementation of research on
pasture, forage and rangeland feed resources.
The consultant will organize workshops for Pakistani scientists involved in
research on pasture forage and rangeland feed resources.
The consultant will liase with Pakistani scientists working in other areas of
feed resources and be consulted on aspects of integrating research on feed
resources.
B. Consultant on Animal Breeding: Development of Indigenous Breeds
gualifications and ExRerience. The consultant should have a research degree
and preferably be a staff member of an International research organization, a
University or equivalent. He should have experience in animal breeding in
developing countries, in relation to development of indigenous breeds.
Duties. The consultant will be responsible to PARC. The consultant is
required to review research on indigenous breeds of sheep, cattle and goats
and make appropriate recommendations about the research effort in this area.
One man-month has been funded for this review.
C. Consultant on Dairy Cattle Cross-breeding Research
Qualifications and Experience. The consultant should have a research degree
and preferably be a staff member of an International research organization or
University department or equivalent. He should have experience in developing
countries in tropical areas with practical knowledge of the problems of
introducing Bos taurus dairy breeds into climatically hostile environments and
cross-breeding with Bos indicus breeds. He should be conversant with computer
programs designed to collect, collate and interpret data on animal breeding.

DuiesI&. The consliltant will be responsible to PARC, Islamabad. The
consultant will work in-country for 6 months over the life of the project with
flexibility in the use of time.
The consultant will undertake a review of past research of dairy cattle
cross-breeding programs in Pakistan and make recommendations on future
directions for research.
He will assist in the planning and implementation of research on dairy cattle
cross-breeding.

research on the major disease of livestock in Pakistan.

pAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Soil Science Research
Introduction
1.        Soil Science has been identified as a priority area in Pakistan's 1988
National Commission of Agriculture. This is rightly so, since crop output is
a mainstay of the national economy and crop output is ultimately dependent on
the optimal supply of water, nutrients and solar energy -- and the study of all
three of these is included within the soil science discipline.
2.        The Soil Science component of the APR-II prcject is designed to
strengthen existing provincial research -- which is mainly concerned with crop
nutrient supply (Soil Fertility and soil Chemistry) and also to add a new
capability to do research on soil and crop environment physics (soil physics).
3.        Soil physics is concerned with such factors as the structure of the
soil, the movement of water (and salts) into, through and out of the soil and
with the need for and supply of water to the crop. Thus, it has implications
in tillage and seedbed production, in irrigation scheduling and crop water-use
efficiency, in the reclamation of saline soils, in the methods and design of
drainage systems and in techniques for water harvesting and storage in rainfed
areas.
4.        The detailed proposals for these two thrusts -- Soil Fertility and
Soil PhysLcs -- are elaborated in the sections below.
Soil Physics
5.        The project will set up a soil physics 'unit' as part of the Soil
Science section of the main research center of each Province. Because of the
major concentration of intensive irrigated agriculture in the Punjab, it is
proposed to set up soil physics units at both Lahore and Fai. alabad. There will
also be a similar unit at NARC, Islamabad, and a nat5onal coordinator established
in PARC from where the national program of soil physics research will be
coordinated.
6.        Each of these six units will be staffed by two senior scientists
(1 grade 18 and 1 grade 17), supported by four junior staff (2 grade 14,
1 administrative, grade 12 and 1 driver, grade 5). Provision has been made for
these posts to be filled by additional staff, but it may be possible to fill some
posts by transfer from areas of lower priority.
7.        Provision has been made for the senior scientist to be sent for
training, to PhD level. It has been assumed that this will take place in years
2-4, the first year being needed for selection and preparatory training.
Provision has also been made to enable the person to carry out his research
project on a topic of importance to Pakistan and to actually do the practical
work in Pakistan. Funds are also available for his Supervisor to make short



- 117 -
Page 2
visits to Pakistan; such visits should also be used to guide and train the other
soil physics staff in the section. Since the discipline of soil physics is new
to existing Pakistan scientists, the research topic and the appropriate overseas
University will be chosen during the first year by the Expert appointed to
provide Technical Assistance (see para 2.14).
8.        Additional training will also be provided to each unit as 6 man-months
of training available for short courses. This is so that the other scientist
within each unit can acquire appropriate technique skills, for example in
instrument operation.   It may also be appropriate for the head of the Soil
Science section to have some exposure to soil physics research, possibly at the
same University as his soil physicist is attending, to help him with his research
direction in this new field.
9.        Funds for the purchase of equipment for soil physics research have been
made available to each unit.   Since the precise equipment needed will vary
somewhat deperding on the likely research topics to be investigated, the funds
have generally been scheduled for year 2, to allow time for the visiting Expert
to advise on both the most appropriate research programs and the equipment
needed. Although the full amount of money has been scheduled at that time, it
would be very reasonable if not all of it were spent initially, but some retained
for additional equipment identified as necessary as the research progresses.
10.       Equipment likely to be widely needed includes a neutron problem for
field studies of water profiles and pressure plate apparatus for laboratory
studies of soil water holding properties. Meteorological equipment (preferably
with output in machine-readable form) will also be essential as will computer
facilities to handle the enormous amounts of data that such research generates.
A double-cab pick-up has been allocated as essential for transporting the
electron problem to the field and for handling the soil samples.
11.       Operational expenses, for both field and laboratory work have been
scheduled from year 3 onwards as it will take some time before the units are
staffed, equipped and ready to embark on a research program.   Funds have,
however, been provided earlier to the coordinator (grade 19) to enable him and
the Expert to initiate the national program.
12.       Funds for equipment and operating expenses have also been allocated
to the Agricultural Universities at Faisalabad and Tandojam to help them to
provide teaching and research in Soil Physics.
13.       Details of the funds allocated to each unit are shown in Appendix 1,
and detailed cost estimates are shown in the Project Implementation Volume,
Annex 3.
14.       A total of 24 mm of technical assistance nas been allocated to enable
the coordinated program to be initiated smoothly and promptly.   The Expert
appointed would assist the coordinator to:
- assess the immediate priority areas for soil physics research in each
Province;
- select suitable staff for PhD training;



- 118 -
Page 3
* select suitable universities for the research topics chosen;
- decide on the equipment needed;
- initiate the training of support staff;
- prepare an indicative plan for the first ten years program of research
in soil physics.
Detailed terms of reference for this Technical Assistance is given in Appendix 2.
Soil Chemistr~/Soil Fertility
15.       There has been an ongoing program of work on soil fertility for the
last three decades.  This program has established the widespread requirement for
additional nitrogen and phosphate, shown the need for zinc on rice and has
established general fertilizer recommendations for the major crops.  However,
much of the activity is based on single replicate trials with only a limited
range of nutrient rates and so is aimed more at popularizing fertilizer use than
at research on precise fertilizer requirements.
16.       There is also a strong bias towards laboratory work.  Such work is
appropriate to widespread survey activities -- but this has largely been
completed.  Laboratory analysis is also relevant to assessing the quality of
irrigation water and to estimating the need for gypsum application to prevent
salinization or for reclamation. Soil analysis also has some value in adjusting
the fertilizer recommendations for phosphate and pouash, but its value should
not be over-estimated. Soil analysis is not a tool for giving precise fertilizer
recommendations; for the major nutrient nitrogen it is of very little value.
Hence PARC's request to set up a soil analysis control laboratory at MARC was
not supported in the Project Preparation Report11 and this refusal is endorsed
by this mission.
17.       The preparation report did suggest that there should be a nationally
coordinated program to assess the results of the data collected so far and to
calibrate the soil analytical methods ir. use. This is not necessary for several
reasons.
- The National Fertilizer Development Center (NFDC) has already assembled
and coordinated much of the data, without being able to derive much
value from the compilation.
- Unless the scientist collating the data is aware of the program in the
field, there is no way of eliminating spurious effects which, if
included, invalidate the sound data.
- The interpretation of the experimental results requires the
simultaneous collection of supporting data, such as past cropping and
fertilizer use, weather conditions before and during crop growth, water
if   Agricultural Research II Project, 165/87 CP-PAK 41, 18 December 1987, Annex
5.



- 119 ^
Page 4
use and possible leaching, and s. on. The supporting data required
will vary with the nut:ient being studied. It is certain that much
of this supporting data, essential for interpretati in, is not available
for past experiments.
18.       Instead, there should be a nationally coordinated program to produce
more site-specific fertilizer recommendations. This should be a new research
program of field work that is based on measuring response curves and determining
economic optimal and the program should be designed to understand the reasons
for the differences in response that are found.
19.       This   will   require   a   clearer   separation   of   the   current
extension-orientated activities (e.g., advisory analysis and fertilizer
demonstration trials) from the research activity proper. The research activity
should be a joint effort by Chemistry and Fertility staff (where these are
currently separated) and the research team should also include the soil physicist
where appropriate. It will also require the scientists to closely monitor the
field work so that they are fully aware of all that is happening.   This is
essenitial to obtain high quality results and to aid the scientists in their task
of spotting possible reasons for observed differences.
20.       Coordination should be carried out jointly by the National Fertilizer
Development Center (NFDC) (if willing) and PARC. NFDC have much to offer such
a program, they have easy access through FAO to international activity in this
field, they have considerable expertise in fertilizer use research themselves
and also considerable expertise in computer-based data storage, handling and
interpretation. They can also supply expertise in computer modelling -- which
will be an essential part of correctly interpreting and using the research data.
21.       The first priority for research, and the major part of the program,
should be on nitrogen. This is the nutrient which costs the country and its
farmers the most amount of money and where the greatest financial gains are to
be had from optimizing its use.  Since nitrogen is so mobile a nutrient and
undergoes so many transformations in soil, it is necessary to base the research
program on the nitrogen cycle with the twin objectives of estimating the amount
of nitrogen that becomes available to the crop from soil sources and how best
to add the additional fertilizer nitrogen that is needed for optimum crop growth.
22.       It is generally the experience round the world that soil nitrogen
supply cannot be estimated by soil analysis but requires estimates based on such
factors as residues from previous cropping, losses by leaching, denitrification,
and so on. Similarly, the value of the added fertilizer frequently depends on
minimizing the various loss mechanisms - - particularly on minimizing ammonia loss
from urea.  Thus, there will have to be a detailed program of basic work on
nitrogen transformations in soil alongside the field work on response to
nitrogen, so that all the necessary supporting data from the field work is
collected. It is also likely that an approach based on modelling the nitrogen
cycle and quantifying the components in specific locations will be the most
fruitful.
23.       Next in financial impo-tance is work on phosphate.  Since fertilizer
residues accumulate in soil, palt of the work needs to be very long-term in
nature (that is, research station based) and part needs to be in farmers' fields



- 120 -                         ANNX
Page 5
but rotation-based. This makes the determination of economic optimal somewhat
difficult, but the long-term effects do need to be taken into account. It is
likely that soil analysis may be found useful in making site-specific
recommendations.
24.       There is considerable work currently on potash.   Under the arid,
irrigated conditions in Pakistan, potash is likely to remain the least deficient
of the three major nutrients but some basic work on the quantity and release rate
of non-exchangeable pocash is warranted as is the determination of economic
optimal in those crop and soil conditions which have already been identified as
potash responsive. Work also needs to continue (until such time as a decision
can be taken) to evaluate the use of potassium chloride as potash source since
this is much cheaper than currently used sulphate.
25.       Work carried out so far should have already identified those crop and
soil conditions where secondary nutrients are currently important. For example,
zinc problems in rice and iron in fruit production. At current levels of crop
yield it is unlikely that responses to secondary nutrients in general will be
very economic and therefore a widespread program of fertilizer trials on such
nutrients is unlikely to be rewarding.
26.       An alternative approach is to carry out some multi-discipline research
which has the aim of identifying the constraints to achieving potential yield.
Potential yield can be determined, for a given crop, from solar energy input
during its growth period, photosynthetic efficiency, and harvest index. If this
potential is not being approached under research conditions, then it is a very
instructive research project to try to attain such yields.  The constraints are
as likely to be aspects of the cultivation system (irrigation schedule, plant
density, disease control) as they are to be nutritional, but an increased
nutrient supply, including supplementary secondary nutrients, should be included
as possible factors. It is, however, vital that the research should be wider
than simply nutrition.
27.       The ARP II project will provide funds to support the provincial
research centers continue with a soil chemistry/soil fertility research program.
Most provinces have already suggested that new Soil Science Institutes should
be formed. The mission feels strongly that new Institutes are counter-productive
in creating unnecessary barriers to team-work in research and would prefer to
see soil scientists working together in research groups or sections. Although
some staff expsnsion is necessary, in some provinces, to provide viable research
groups, this should be done gradually and should spread into the next project
-- ARP III. Consequently, only limited expansion of soil science staff has been
supported in this project.
28.       The first essential in this project is to formulate programs of work
that are research (rather than extension) orientated and which address the
national and provincial priority areas within soil science.
29.       The mid-term review should examine these plans for their relevance,
using them as a monitor of progress of the project.
30.       The funds will provide training (to PhD level) where there is a lack
of trained staff, equipment for laboratory and field work related to a research



- 121 -
Page 6
program (but not for advisory analysis) and operating funds to support the
research program. A sum has also been allocated to each province to supply
approprice scientific journals (for both soil chemistry and soil physics) to
enable the scientists to keep up to date with their subject.  The details of
allocations to specific Centers are shown in Appendix 3.
31.       In addition to its joint coordinating role, the research at NARC should
provide more basic supporting information to supplement the provincial work.
For example, any work on clay mineralogy, in support of provincial work on
non-exchangeable potassium, should be done at NARC where appropriate facilities
exist. Similarly, any N15 studies in the nitrogen program or P32 studies in the
phosphate program should be either commissioned by or carried out at NARC, to
avoid proliferation of such facilities. Funds to equip and operate such programs
are provided in the project.
Soil Biochemistry (ARI, Faisalabad)
32.       The  Soil  Biochemistry  research program  at  the Ayub  Institute,
Faisalabad includes work on the use of sewage effluent in agriculture. This is
relevant to the aims of the project in that it seeks to use waste water without
causing damage to the soil. Funds have therefore been provided for equipment
and operating costs to support this research.
Soil Microbiology (ARI, Faisalabad)
33.       The project proposal contained a small component to provide equipment
for measuring biological nitrogen fixation. This equipment has already been
obtained from other sources and therefore this component has been deleted.
Soil Survey (Lahore)
34.       Information from the existing soil survey can be of value to the soil
fertility research program in identifying major soil types, characterizing
research sites, providing background soil properties, etc. Such information is
tedious to extract from the current printed reports but could be more readily
available if the information were on computer. The original proposal was to
supply both computer hardware and software for this computerization to occur.
However, the Soil Survey Institute has already obtained equipment and has made
a start on transferring the data to computer.  What is needed now is the means
to communicate this data to potential users and therefore the project will supply
funds for appropriate digitizing and plotting equipment and software, also
Technical Assistance or, alternatively, short-course training in its use.
Irrigation Research
35.       The Mission feels that the efficient use by farmers of Pakistan's most
scarce resource -- water -- is so vital that research support should be given
in addition to that being provided by the establishment of a research program
on soil physics. Consequently, although no specific component in this area was
prepared and included in this project, the mission has included some funds (Rs.
10.0 million) in project costs for such a component.



- 122 -
ABUE 5
Page 7
36.       Work on water use efficiency at farm level is being conducted by the
Pakistan Council of Research in Water Resources at their Drainage and Reclamation
Institute of Pakistan (DRIP). The mission have therefore requested PARC have
the Pakistan Council of Research in Water Resources, in association with the
Bank's Resident Mission, prepare a suitable component that can be included in
this project.
Monitoring
37.       The Soil Physics planned sequence of development is set out in paras 5
to 14. Critical stages are:
(a)  the appointment of an Expert for TA early in year 1;
(b)  sending appointed staff for overseas training in year 2; and
(c)  the preparation of detailed research plans by end year 2.
38.       Since Soil Chemistry/Soil Fertility research is part of an on-going
program, the monitoring of the part funded by this project is quite difficult
and will require close supervision. Initially, success can best be judged by
the quality and relevance of the annual research prograns and the extent to which
these recognize the separate (but complementary) functions of research and
extension.
39.       Success would also be indicated if the various branches of soil science
(chemistry, physics, fertility, biochemistry, microbiology) were all combining
in their attack on research problems such as quantifying the nitrogen cycle and
even more so if real multi-disciplinary teams had been created for project work.
40.       .n the longer term, the success of the training activity can only be
judged subjectively by an improvement in the quality of research management,
leadership and planning -- which hopefully will then lead to research results
of increased value to the agriculture of Pakistan.

SOIL PHYSICS FUNDS BY INSTITUTE 1/
Federal - NArG
1.        Provision has been made for a coordinator (grade 19), supporting staff,
equipment (years 1 & 2), and a vehicle. The operating funds have been increased
for years 1 and 2 to allow ample funds for the Expert as well as coordinating
expenses and research expenses.   The PhD training is available for the
coordinator or for the support staff, as needed.
Puniab - Lahore (Soil Fertility)
2.        Funds for a standard 'Unit' have been provided.  It has been assumed
that the senior scientist will be appointed in year 1 and sent for training in
years 2 to 5.  Since he should be doing his research in Pakistan in years 3
and 4, funds for equipment and the vehicle have been made available in year 2
so that they are available on time. Operating funds and support staff have been
scheduled from year 3 onwards.
4.        Funds for short-course training have been made available in years 2
and 4. This is to allow introductory training of the section head, if necessary,
before support staff arrive.   Training of support staff,  for example in

for training should be used very flexibly, as needs are identified and not
necessarily used only in the years specified.
