PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Integrated Pest Management Component
Introduction
1.       It is estimated that over one third of the potential yield of crops
in Pakistan is lost to insect pests and disease attack every year. Crop
protection, therefore constitutes an integral part of crop production
planning. Effective crop protection reduces annual yield fluctuations and
increases farmers' confidence in agricultural performance. Past research,
particularly by international pesticide companies, has developed methods
which allow farmers to achieve economicaily viable yields of many crops, even
when pest attack is severe. Much of this research has, however, been directed
to the development of synthetic chemical pesticides. There is, however,
growing concern worldwide that a total reliance on chemical pesticides for
pest control leads to the development of non-sustainable systems. Whereas
previously farmers had used a variety of means - resistant varieties
destroying crop residues, etc. to minimize pest build up, the 'modern'
approach de-emphasizes cultural control in favor of using pesticides to
eliminate pests as soon as they appear. However, insect pests and diseases
are becoming increasingly and more rapidly resistant to modern pesticides;
insect predators are being destroyed, and the natural balance between pest and
predator is being upset. The 'modern' approach is therefore seen as being
damaging to the environment and not sustainable.
2.       Pesticides valued at around US$100 million (Rs 1.8 billion) are
imported annually into Pakistan. About 80% of these chemicals are applied to
the cotton crop in an average of about 5 sprays; that is about Rs 288 million
for the cost of pesticides in a single spray to cotton alone. About 280
pesticide products including carbamates, organophosphates, pyrethroids and
even organochlorines are used in Pakistan, and despite Federal registration
and Government legislation there is uncontrolled application and undoubtedly
environmental contamination.
3.       In an effort to begin the process of decreasing reliance on chemical
pesticides, the Government, through the Report of the National Commission on
Agriculture, has directed research to focus on Integrated Pest Management
(IPM) as a priority topic and has specifically recommended expanding or
up-grading facilities for IPM studies at the Ayub Research Institute, Punjab,
and at the ARI Tarnab, NWFP, and has recommended the formation of a ne-w
multidisciplinary departments of IPM at Tandojam, Sind, and Quetta,
Baluchistan.  The World Bank strongly supports this positive action by GOP.
4.       The essential feature of IPM is that it employs a combination of pest
management tactics to bring about pest control. These tactics include: the
use of resistant varieties; biological control; changing cultural practices to
avoid or limit pest build up; the use of trap crops; employing pheromones to
reduce pest breeding; pest scouting to determine economic threshold levels for



-129 -                                ANNEX 6
Page 2
effective spraying; and of course, the use of selective pesticides once
threshold levels are reached. All of these techniques are used in varying
combinations in many countries. However, the efficient use of IPM strategies
requires detailed knowledge of crop growing conditions and of the life
histories of diseases, pests and predators. IPM depends essentially on an
undetstanding of the local agricultural ecosystem (the basic biology and the
interactions among crops, pests and natural enemies). Successful and
sustainable IPM requires sophisticated and imaginative research to disentangle
and explain the many facets of crop and pest relationships. Such research is
rare in Pakistan at present and scientists must be trained in IPM
methodologies and re-oriented in their thinking in order to bring about the
changes in pest control strategies envisaged in the National Commission Report.
Project IPM Proposals
5.       It is impertant to note that the recommendations in the National
Commission Report were developed after proposals for ARPII were submitted.
Thus specific proposals for IPM were not included in the preparation documents
and the details presented below weie produced during pre-appraisal. They must
therefore be seen as indicative only. Provision has been made in the project
for detailed IPM research planning at national and provincial levels during
the first year of the project, using Pakistan scientists supported by
short-term international IPM experts. Provision for funding the start of this
planned program has been made for the remaining five project years. At the
same time scientists from Provincial and Federal research institutes would be
trained Lt sustain IPM work beyond the project period.
National and Provincial Coordination
6.       The proposed IPM component would aim to bring about a coordinated
program of planned research and training at national and provincial levels.
It would be important to plan strategies for IPM for each Province and to
commence funding these strategies in clearly identified, separately funded and
monitorable provincial programs. However, the limited scientific manpower and
financial resources in Pakistan do not allow for any duplication of effort.
Furthermore, results achieved at one laboratory or field trial must be shared
as rapidly as possible with all scientists working on IPM. There are great
numbers of IPM research topics to be tackled and scientific programs should
aim at synergism not protectionism. Coordination of planning, skill gap
identification and subsequert t:aining programs also requires strong linkages
and cooperation oetween federal and provincial scientists.
7.       Coordination of effort will be the key to successful IPM research and
the proposed IPM component therefore contains provision for a National IMP
Network Coordinator and a small but technically skilled coordination staff in
entomology and pathology - the most important disciplines needed in IPM at the
present time. This unit would need to be centrally based and PARC
headquarters would be the logical site. This coordination staff would be
facilitators, planners and advisers to the network. They would have no
executive authority over the network but would provide the leadership and
administrative function for organizing workshops and seminars, coordinating
the inputs of technical assistants, maintaining a directory of all in-country
studies relevant to IPM, distributing information on IPM to provincial and
national scientists and administering the IPM training programs allocated



- 130 -                              ANNEX 6
Page 3
specifically for the benefit of che Provinces. In addition, .he IPM Network
Zoordinator would administer a research fund for providing operational
expenses allocated specifically for IPM studies at provincial and federal
institutes. These operating funds would be released to specific Provinces
once appropriate IPM plans had been produced.
8.       Each Province would produce proposals in PCI format in accordance
with the priorities set out in the IPM Plan. The IPM Plan would subsequently
become an integral part of the overall Provincial Research Plan called for by
the Project.  Release of funds for the PCI proposals would require
confirmation in writing to IDA by the Network Coordinator that (a) the
proposals conformed to the 1PM Plans and that the proposed research did not
duplicate other IPM studies in Pakistan.
9.       The Network Coordinator would seek to interact directly with all
institutions in Pakistan undertaking work related to IPM.  Such institutions
would include but would not be limited to the following:
Agricultural Research Institute:  Quetta
Agricultural Research Institute: Tandojam
Agricultural Research Institute:  Tarnab
Agricultural Universities: Faisalabad, Tandojam and Peshawar
Atomic Energy Centers: Tandojam, Faisalabad and Peshawar
Ayub Agricultural Research Institute: Faisalabad
Cotton Research Institute: Multan
Department of Agriculture, Azad Jammu and Kashmir
PARC - CIBC Station: Rawalpindi
Pakistan Central Cotton Committee: Karachi
Plant Protection Institute: Faisalabad
Rice Research Institute: Dokri
10.      In addition it would be important for the IPM Network Coordinator to
maintain close links with private sector pesticide companies operating in
Pakistan, one at least of whom conducts pesticide research on an independent
research farm.
11.      The Network Co.'tdinator would prepare a computer-based inventory of
all IPM work being undertaken in Pakistan. As first-step in preparing the
inventory and in developing provincial IPM plans and proposals, annual IPM
Workshops would be organized which would bring together senior scientists and
management from all agencies involved in IPM, including those agencies listed
above. While the first workshop might, for convenience, be held at PARC/NARC
facilities in Islamabad, subsequent Workshops/seminars would be rotated
amongst Provincial institutes.
12.      Technical assistance will be required to help provincial scientists
and the network secretariat to prepare and consolidate IPM plans and costed
action programs which respond to the needs stated in the National Commission
Report and which prioritize IPM work according to agricultural needs and to
the resources available. Planning, monitoring and evaluating annual work
programs will involve close liaison between agencies and workshops organized
by the Network Coordinator. Funds for technical assistance transport and
travel and for workshops and seminars have been provided under the project.

PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Social Science Research
1.        This Annex is condensed from a report in the Project Implementation
Volume concerning social science research proposed in the project. Besides
overviewing the existing structure of agricultural social science research ir.
Pakistan (centered mainly on agricultural economics research), and a social
science research agenda, the report details the cost estimates of proposed
social science research programs under the project.
Overview of Existing St_uctures
2.        Agriculture is so important in the Pakistan economy that virtually
all agencies dealing with economic analysis must have some capacity for
agricultural social science research. This includes the Federal and
Provincial Ministries of Finance, Planning and Development, Agriculture and
Food, Water and Irrigation, Rural Development, the Water and Power Development
Authority (WAPDA), the National Agriculture Commission, the Agricultural
Prices Commission, the universities with social science departments including
Quaid-e-Azam University and the International Institute of Islamic Economics;
and several research institutes, but notably Pakistan Institute of Development
Economics (PIDE), Applied Economics Research Center of the University of
Karachi (AERC), Pakistan Agricultural Research Council (PARC), and Punjab
Ece.nomic Research Institute (PERI).
3.        Some of the above institutions are linked through common
participation in governing boards and program committees - particularly the
broadly representative Technical Committee on Social Science in PARC - but
most are not. Indeed, most operate independently and rather jealously
possessive of their "own" data and analyses, and remain largely ignorant of
others' data, research agenda and research reports.
4.        The Social Science Division of PARC is linked through its Technical
Committees to (a) the Provincial Economic Research Institutes; (b) the
agricultural university faculties; (c) the Departments of Agriculture, and
Planning and Development; and (d) Ministry of Food, Agriculture and
Cooperatives (MINFA), PIDE and Agricultural Prices Commission (APCOM).
However, the influence of these groups on furthering agricultural economic
analysis on the main agricultural research system is probably small as the
National Agricultural Research Plan (PARC, 1986) does not mention a single
social science research institute in the 90 institutes listed as dealing with
agricultural research and allied subjects.
5.        Current Social Science Research Agenda in PARC.  Social science
research in PARC is relatively recent. Some of the needosd research is being
done by the Agricultural Economics Research Units (AERUs), but the scope of



- 132 -
ANNEX 7
Page 2
these investigations has been limited, mainly due to their restriction to
selected technologies or farming activities. There is yet in PARC little
research available on the "whole farm" business or on household or
agribusiness firm decision-making.
6.        The social science headquarters staff in PARC responsible to the
Member (Social Science), that is, the Director of Research, two Deputy
Directors and three scientific officers - are initiating some data collection
to support overall reseatch planning. This includes information on five major
crops, the major livestock, farm population, and fertilizer use. A farm
management survey has been carried out in the Barani areas of the Punjab and
separate studies are under way on the role of women in livestock and crop
production. This is a somewhat heterogenous mix of data collection, and the
research is mostly of a descriptive type. However, the activities started
offer good opportunities for expanding experience. Some additional well
trained economists in the headquarters Social Science Division (and, of
course, elsewhere) are of crucial importance and a number of these will become
available from the group of trainees sent to U.S. Universitites for PhD
training under the Agricultural Research I Project (ARP-I). In addition,
short-term technical assistance would be required to estab1ish and operate an
economically-based system for determining major research priorities at Council
level. A start has bteen made on this in early 1988 through a research project
conducted in collaboration between PARC Social Sciences Division and Dr Robert
Evenson of Yale University.
Agricultural Economic Research Units (AERUs)
7.        Achievements and Challenges.  The creation of several AERUs is an
important achievement of PARC. It is disturbing, however, to learn that, as
of November 1988, the Units at Faisalabad and Tandojam have been without
operational funds since January 1988 with consequent reduction in research and
publication activity. The existing AERUs are small units, especially
cuuisidering the magnitude of the research agenda that they face, but they are
generally staffed with energetic researchers, and work quite efficiently. The
AERUs focus on micro-level technological research that the other research
institutions mentioned before are presently poorly equ,pped to handle.
8.        The productivity of the AERUs varies considerably depending on the
interplay of several factors. Positive elements have included:
(a) Their considerable autonomy in research implementation and their
usually easy access to travel and computer assistance. Th-s has
allowed zhem to "get on with the job". The incorporation of AERUs
initially within the provincial Agricultural Research Institutes
(ARIs) would probably have made this virtually impossible.
(b)  The influence of CIMNYT (the International Maize and Wheat
Improvement Centre) economists, clearly in evidence at the Tarnab
and Ayub Units, and somewhat also at NARC, has greatly assisted in
making their work especially productive. The CIMMYT economists have



- 133 -
ANNEX 7
Page 3
played the important role of mentors, and have stimulated much good
work at the AERUs. The absence of these outside resources for
several months in 1987-88, along with recent funding problems, was
sorely missed and was reflected in low morale of staff early in
1988.
(c)  The research staff members have been additionally spurred to
enthusiasm by a reward system of (i) acknowledged authorship of
well-produced publications; (ii) favored access to personal
computers and vehicles; (iii) the provision of convenient housing;
and (iv) the expectation, and in some cases already the realization,
of advanced degree training in the United States.
9.        There have also been negative aspects:
(a)  The focus on interactions between crops and en cropping technology
has restricted attention to other important farm interactions,
especially to those involving livestock and forage production, soil
conservation, and to non-technological issues such as commodity and
factor markets. Some of these issues are, however, now being
addressed. Research in cooperation with CIMMYT, building on earlier
work including crop-livestock-fodder issues in NWFP and Berani
areas, will address some of the wider issues (PARC/CIMMYT 1987).
The AERUs are still fledgling, and it is thus appropriate presently
to dodge the development of costly and diff'cult research involving
livestock. At present, NARC has only one livestock economist and it
must also be recalled that the provincial ARIs have no mandate for
livestock research, which generally falls into the responsibilities
of departments other than the Department of Agriculture.
(b)  Dependence on outside assistance can be justified in the short run,
but care must be (and seemingly is being) taken to ensure that it
will not be required in the long run.  This will depend on the
disposition and turnover of AERU staff, particularly after returning
from advanced training.
(c)  The once-special treatment of AERU staff did doubtlessly rouse
jealousies among researchers in other research establishments.  This
has been manifested in several ways but most significant might be an
increasing isolation of AERUs. AERUs have been designed to assist
natural science researchers with the economic assessment of their
work, but their acceptance will depend on a continuing demonstration
of achievements and on regular feedback of results to the
collaborating research workers.  At least ac the level of the
Director General and Director of Research of the ARIs, acceptance
seeminrly has been achieved as they make many calls on the services
of the AERUs. On balance, to date, AERUs have been a worthwhile
institutional experiment and have achieved useful and valuable
results. They warrant continued support.



- 134 -
ANNEX 7
Page 4
10.       AERU Establishment.  The AERUs are still small and hardly
established. The ones at Tarnab and Faisalabad have made a good start.
However, key staff are undergoing post-graduate training, and the productivity
of these two institutions has suffered. Also, the AERU at Tandojam is young
and it is in urgent need of leadership and stimulation, preferably through
appointment of experienced staff but perhaps also combined with some external
technical assistance, some of which is being provided by CIMMYT on rice-wheat
cropping systems.
11.       The AERU at NARC has shown a variable performance.  Staffing
difficulties have been encountered and considerable resources were absorbed by
the numerous non-research-related tasks and activities which have made up
about 50X of potential research time. Clarification is required as to the
attention which this Unit is supposed to give to national issues that are part
of 1,-ARC's activities, and to the solution of more local problems pertaining to
Barani agriculture. A 30/50 split may be appropriate, on the understanding
that capability for establishing the economic justification of the national
research portfolio is to be established in PARC itself.
12.       The facilities provided to the AERU at NARC create problems.  The
available space was not sufficient to accommodate all the major externally
funded projects. It has been proposed that the Data Processing and Biometry
Unit and the Technology Transfer Unit be incorporated into AERU under the
control of NARC, and housed with AERU in a new office building. Steps need to
be taken to avoid in future a similar crowding-out process. Consideration
should also be given to accommodating the other agricultural economists of
NARC in part of the accommodation of any new building, or in the vacated old
cafeteria once a new one Is built in any other re-allocated space. NARC has
economist staff working on livestock (1), mechanization (1), pulses (1), the
Barani project (1), potatoes (2), and on crop "maximization" (1). The
productivity of these researchers would probably be enhanced if they were put
together, while preserving their line iesponsibilities to the various other
institutes and projects. The success of any such major physical re-assembly
of social scientists will depend on the appointment of a strong research
leader as director of the AERU. The construction of a new Crop Sciences
Building at NARC may make it possible to physically reassemble all social
scientists.
13.       The situation of the AERU at Quetta is clarifying.  Staffing the
unit with Baluchi economists, as is required by the provincial government, has
been difficult, but two appointments were made early in 1988. During the
initial period, the professional experience available at the Arid Zone
Research Institute (AZRI) and the International Centre for Agricultural
Research in the Dry Areas (ICARDA) should be used on a gratis basis to help to
bridge gaps at the Quetta AERU.
14.       The merged research services of the University and the province
which has occurred in North-West Frontier Province (NWFP) presents a
particular challenge to the development of an AERU. Meantime r:emaining under
the control of PARC, the merged social science research unit offers the



- 135 -
ANEX 7
Page 5
possibility, and as of early 1988 seemingly the reality, of close working
relations with the University's Department of Agricultural Economics and its
Institute of Development Studies. Considering their lack of cooperation in
recent years, these two institutions probably will gain more through this
proposed development than the AERU itself.
15.       The housing completed under ARP-I during 1988 at Sariab, Tarnab,
Faisalabad and Tandojam, will be assigned partly for better office
accommodation for the AERUs. This will be satisfactory as long as staff
numbers remain low, that is, until 1989-90 when some of the trainees return.
This additional accommodation will, however, not remove the isolation noted
before; it may even exacerbate it. A test of the units will be their final
acceptance by the ARI Directors as integral parts of their Institutes,
evidenced by their regular contribution to the Institutes' work, by the end of
ARP II but, bureaucratic independence and inertia being what they are, the
test will be difficult to pass.
16.       Strengthening of AERUs in ARP II.  Social scientists should bring
the economic reality of farming, especially small-scale farming, into the
framework of agricultural research. The Mid-Term Review of ARP I (World Bank
1986a) reports that a start has been made in this direction in Pakistan.
However, the report also points out that a much larger investment than
hitherto is required to establish and integrate social sciences within the
research system. This will become increasingly evident as farming systems
research is taken up by PARC and the provincial institutes.
17.       AERUs are expected to play a role in research planning, design of
experiments, analysis of experimental results and the identification of
farmers' problems amenable to research. Specifically, their functions will
be:
(a) to perceive problems in farmers' fields and with farmers, and help
translate them into research initiatives for the institutes in the
context of problem-solving research conducted within delineated
agroecological zones;
(b) to determine the socio-economic viability of technologies planned
and released by the research stations, identify farm-level
constraints to their adoption and, in the process, specify
requirements for technology redesign, and highlight areas for policy
intervention;
(c) to evaluate the private and social benefits and costs of new
production methods and assess the socio-economic consequences of
their adoption in relation to national objectives;
(d) to assist in introducing a systems (multidisciplinary) approach, in
place of commodity-, discipline-, or technique-specific approaches,
to the identification of research problems and to the interpretation



- 136 -
ANNEX 7
Page 6
of research results, as already demonstrated in the PARC/CIMMYT
collaboration;
(e) to act as a consulting unit in designing experiments and analyzing
experimental data. This development, proposed by PARC, enlarges the
usual concept of social science but, under the present PARC/NARC/ARI
institutional divisions, biometrics is still a neglected field.
There are, in fact, some links that have developed through the
AERUs' involvement in on-farm research and their concern for
appropriate interpretation of experimental data. These two
functional activities are also likely to be the major users of
computers. The best reason for continuing the existing arrangement
is that it might serve to make the contribution of genuine social
science acceptable to biological research workers who presumably
require little sensitization to the usefulness of biometric
services. Social science "riding on the coat-tails' of applied
statistics is rather novel but, in the Pakistan context, it may be a
good means of getting it established.
18.       For effectively executing the above tasks, AERUs need continuing
support and strengthening in terms of human resources, equipment and
operational resources. The strengthening proposals are based on the following
arguments:
(a) The human resources of AERUs are too small to have a significant and
reliable impact on biological research programs, and the existing
small number of social scientists have difficulty in interacting
with the much larger number of biological scientists. The staffing
level of social scientists, therefore, should be raised to let them
become more visible, viable, effective and productive. It will be
difficult to increase staff numbers in the AERUs in the short run.
The units must concentrate on major commodities and
province-specific issues and cannot attend to the planning and
assessment needs of a wide range of biological research on a routine
basis. This is especially a problem in the Punjab where the AERU
staff relate in principle to rather more than 500 biological and
natural scientists working at more than a dozen research stations.
As additional people become available for appointment to AERUs,
consideration should be given to outposting most of them to some of
the smaller provincial institutes such as the Maize Research
Institute. This should not interfere with the professional
productivity of economists, provided that strong links are
maintained with the "headquarters" of the AERU at Ayub ARI. It is,
of course, vital for the individual social scientists to develop
considerable technical competence regarding the agricultural
enterprises that are at the focus of their research programs.
(b) The technical skills and the level of training of the AERU social
scientists are at present not adequate for the role and functions
they are expected to play. There is a need for advanced training in



- 137 -
ANNEX Z
Page 7
areas such as biometry, agribusiness, resource economics, production
economics, agricultural marketing, and systems analysis. Such
specialized training will enable a broadening of research activities
according to the needs of the province concerned. Post-doctoral
fellowships are also needed for furthering initiative and program
leadership skills. In considering these institutional enhancements
there is a need for careful consideration of (i) the limited
absorptive capacity of the existing institutions, and (ii) a
realistic scheduling of enlarging the AERU capacities. A fifth AERU
has been proposed by the Azad Government of the State of Jammu and
Kashmir (AJK). This could combine well with other acti-ities in the
Integrated Hill Farming Development Program (IHFDP) (Cr. 1461-PAK)
and seems a worthy additional element in the AERU system.
Accordingly, ARP-II would fund a small AERU research unit in AJK.
It will probably take most of the ARP II project period to achieve
essential expansions, and PARC (Social Sciences) needs to prepare
the detailed work plans of the proposed AERU development program
well before project implementation.
(c) Existing equipment resources of AERU are hardly sufficient to meet
present research requirements.  In future, AEFJs will, and
understand that they will, also have to serve as training centres in
computer applications, biometry and research methods and thus will
generate demands for considerable additional data-processing
equipment.
(d) The AERU and NARC has a shortage of office space. To remedy this
problem and to give better visibility to social sciences of NARC, a
rearrangement of existing space should be organized to house
agricultural extension, biometry and, most importantly, the AERU.
The three disciplines are proposed to be headed by a Director
(BPS-19)(an existing position), who must have a good understanding
of, and responsibility for the functioning and development of the
three programs above.
19.       Planning Procedures.  PARC should continue to require the AERUs to
put forward an annual work plan together with an arnual report. Presently,
this is prepared first in consultation with scientists in and heads of
provincial research and is then discussed and refined in the annual program
meeting in which all AERUs participate.  PARC does serve as a final clearing
house concerning the general direction of the proposed work, but it is
important to develop the skills of critical self-appraisal and planning within
each of the AERUs. This process might in due course be aided by instituting,
for each AERU, a Program Advisory Committee that would meet at least annually
with the AERU Director and staff to discuss achievements and plans. Such
Committees might consist of:  (a) the PARC Member (Social Science); (b)
Director General, or Director of Research of the relevant ARI; (c) perhaps a
nominee of the closest university Department of Agricultural Economics; and
(d) a co-opted member from another interested organization (for example, an
economics research institute, MINFA, the provincial extension service). Such



- 138 -
ANNEX 7
Page 8
an arrangement would assist in building needed institutional bridges. In
recent years much of this coordination and planning role has been played by
the CIMMYT Regional Economist, but this cannot be regarded as a permanent
setup.
Social Science Research at PARC Headguarters
20.       General Roles.  The tasks of social scientists within PARC
headquarters would include:
(a) to foster the development of social science within the agricultural
research system of Pakistan through such means as (i) assessing
future manpower and skill needs of relevant institutions,
(ii) assisting with manpower training to provide effective research
staff, and (iii) supporting the promotion of professional
capabilities through meetings, activities and scientific
publications in social sciences relevant to the agriculture of
Pakistan.
(b) to support and supervise the work of AERUs;
(c) to plan, manage and assist in executing contract research involving
social sciences, and review the research results obtained and thus
assist in the dissemination of results within and outside PARC,
although scarcity of human resources has of recent times severely
circumscribed these roles;
(d) to carry out a limited and, relative to existing research
institutions, a complementary program of economic and sociological
research on neglected topics of importance for agricultural
development.
21.       The latter two tasks in para 20 (e) and (d) are open-ended and great
skill and care will be needed to choose topics of critical importance.  The
main thrust of the studies will be to provide information on the development
opportunities offered by the persistent under-investment in agricultural
research (Oehmke 1986). Some of the studies will be of past achievements
(such as those of Pray, 1983 and Nagy, 1986), and some will be on future
prospects. The general purposes will be to present evidence to convince GOP
of the value of research investment for development and to guide assignment of
priorities and allocation of resources into major areas of research effort.
Pakistan should take advantage of the growing body of quantitative approaches
to priority setting in agricultural research, and some technical assistance in
this area will be valuable:
(a) The "science" of the economics of research resource allocation is
still in its early days, so too much should not be expected of it
too early. The perennial problems of (i) the uncertain time to
execute "successful" research, (ii) the uncertain results that can
be yielded through research, and (iii) the uncertain pattern of



- 139 -
ANNEX 7
Page 9
adoption and exploitation of supposedly successful results, all
imply that the implementation of any "rational' planning system will
be plagued by frustration. That PARC economists are not presently
working on research resource allocation with outstanding achievement
is understandable. Better work can, however, be done but it will
require considerable resources and time. Not only are effective
expert people from Pakistan required but there is also a need for
resource people from outside if substantive progress is to be made
and some such assistance is planned to be forthcoming from USAID and
can be supplemented by a modest amount under ARP II.
(b) The analysis must take account of many factors, most of which are
difficult to quantify. The size of the industry is the easiest
aspect to pin down, but even this is subject to considerable
uncertainty in Pakistan. The critical parameter is an index of
research achievement, often encapsulated in the proportional
reduction attributable to research in costs of production. A decent
analysis also incorporates the probability distributions attaching
to the several uncertain quantities and this element of quantified
uncertainty presents severe methodological challenge. These latter
two considerations essentially constitute the estimation of a
"stochastic research production function" - an empirical task that
has not yet been dealt with very adequately in any national or
international institution. PARC, as well as its counterpart
institutions elsewhere, must develop competence in these areas but
it will take time and may require eventually considerable technical
assistance from bodies such as the International Service for
National Agricultural Research (ISNAR) and the Australian Centre for
International Agricultural Research (ACIAR), which are already
developing analogous planning activities for other countries in
collaboration with national responsible authorities.
Social Science Research at the Agricultural Universities
22.       Background.  To qualify as a university, an institution of higher
learning must not only transmit knowledge to staff and students, but it also
needs to add to knowledge through research. Using this criterion, at least
insofar as the social sciences are concerned, the agricultural universities of
Pakistan hardly qualify for the title, as little genuine research is now
undertaken. Vis-a-vis several rather well developed disciplines in biological
sciences, agricultural economics remains undeveloped or underdeveloped (Aslam
and Amir 1985). It has been extremely difficult to attract and retain well
trained staff. There are only a few senior people, and most of these
undertook doctoral studies overseas in the 1950s.  They are in high demand in
the private as well as the public sector of Pakistan, and also in national or
international institutions overseas.
23.       Apart from issues related to salary and other benefits, the major
disincentive for a young PhD to join a university staff is the lack of
facilities and data to pursue a research career. Also, teaching loads are



- 140 -
ANNEX 7
Page 10
heavy and leave generally little time for research. The small research fund
available from the university administration hardly covers the demands for
supporting M.Sc. dissertation work in the academic departments. It is also
claimed that the promotion system does not provide an incentive for university
teachers to pursue research.
24.       The social science departments at universities have responded with
what is seen in them as due caution to the opportunities presented by PARC
contract research. Some people indicated an aversion to being "responsible"
to a fund-controlling federal authority. Interpretation of relative
"seniority" also seems to interfere with the relationship between
institutions. Other staff pointed to the difficulties caused by slow,
inadequate, and late release of research monies in contract research. Other
pertinent criticisms made of contract research were that PARC (a) interferes
"excessively and unnecessarily" in staff selection under contract funds and
(b) is overly concerned with return of capital items, such as furnishings, at
the end of a contract.
25.       Universities in general and, given the present and prospective
policies for staff appointment, agricultural universities in particular are
crucial to the evolution of a long-term self-sustaining research system. This
must be recognized as a matter of urgency even though present lines of funding
for most agricultural research and for universities are different. A strong
budgetary support for the research system at large needs to include a
significant proportion for universities. How to effect this needs careful
further consideration. The present observations on the contemporary
deficiencies of the universities are by no means novel. Consider, for
instance, the still-cogent summary remarks of a country review report of the
World Bank (1986b, January 27, paras 3.97-3.99):
"At present the strongest scientific capacity in agriculture is
located at universities where it performs poorly because of lack of
... research ... to complement graduate training. And the research
that is funded at the provincial research institute makes little
contribution to the generation or maintenance of professional
capacity or motivation."
"The Bank has funded projects in each of the three areas of
education, research and extension, but it does not appear that the
Bank or other donors have givan the subsector a consistent high
priority. There was a period of more than a decade when no projects
were initiated by the Bank. There have been difficult political and
procedural obstacles to Bank involvement. The authoritarian nature
of the Pakistani government and the administrative transfer of the
agricultural universities from the Ministry of Agriculture to the
Ministry of Education have presented an environment that is not
conducive to either education or research. And the rivalries
between the provinces present an obstacle to the types of
administrative reform that are necessary to produce a more
productive agricultural education/research/extension system.



- 141 -
Page 11
Finally, the constraint on lending to individual provinces imposes a
serious obstacle to Bank efforts to support research and extension."
"The Pakistan agricultural research and extension systems are
inadequate to meet the needs of the Pakistan economy. The effect of
years of neglect of the training and encouragement of agricultural
scientists, and the neglect of the provincial research institutions
will not be quickly remedied and cannot be overcome without a
serious commitment from the federal and provincial governments to
removing the administrative obstacles to better coordination of the
overall research effort. A further necessary condition for the
effective transfer of improved technologies to farmers is an effort
to improve the level of education in rural areas."
26.       If the agricultural universities were reestablished in the
Department of Agriculture, it would put the universities under more pressure
to do research and it would also simplify funding arrangements. However, such
an arrangement would also require a review of provincial budgeting procedures
and budget flows. The provision of financial support through the provinces
seems difficult because of problems resulting from scale and proliferation.
It is, however, clear that ARP II needs to strengthen provincial capabilities
after the establishment under ARP I of the central PARC/NARC administrative
and physical facilities.
27.       Strengthening under ARP II.  Contract research provides only limited
opportunities for overcoming the difficulties with which social science
research in the agricultural universities is faced at present. Accordingly, a
more tailor-made arrangement needs to be sought to facilitate sustained and
long-term research at the universities.
28.       A solution to the problem could be found in negotiating memoranda of
understanding between PARC and each university. These could be valid for
three years in the first instance, with a rolling 3-year commitment in
principle. All the agricultural universities should be involved, but
initially under ARP II only Tandojam and Faisalabad would be involved as they
seem to provide the best opportu-nities for success. An arrangement with
Tandojam would probably be relatively easy, while an arrangement with
Faisalabad would probably be more significant in the longer term. Needless to
say, any such arrangements will only function well if there can be a much
smoother and faster flow of funds from GOP to PARC to the provinces and
universities than has proved to be possible under existing contract research
arrangements.
29.       The arrangements possible could differ considerably in the degree of
control to be vested in PARC. At one extreme, the funds for contract research
would be handed over to the university with virtually no program requirements
beyond the stipulation of "fostering social science research", and with
accounting restricted to an annual report on plans and progress. Considering
the recent record of: (a) research activity in the universities and the lack
of interest among professors in seeking outside resources, and (b) the urgent



- 142 -
ANNEX 7
Page 12
need for applied research in Pakistan, this approach does not appear to be
very promising.
30.       At the other extreme, PARC through its technical committees could
help to set the research agenda, establish in discussion with university staff
detailed work plans, and monitor and supervise the work in progress. This
would be impractical with PARC's present professional resources and would
prevent self-reliant strengthening of research capacity at the universities.
This approach would create severe problems for all those involved on the
university side.
31.       An intermediate arrangement (with similarities to the prevailing
Technical Committee procedures) would, therefore, appear appropriate for
ARP-II whereby PARC and provincial analogous bodies set broad guidelines as to
needed research areas and offer priorities as to the urgency of particular
topics. These should be discussed in annual research program meetings between
the research councils and a university project management committee, at which
agreement is reached as to the activities for the coming year. Such research
can obviously include projects planned to run over z.veral years.
32.       It is important to get university teachers involved in research, and
to see that staff hired primarily for research are also involved in teaching.
Only this interaction will revitalize the essential functioning of
universities in the medium term. For its part, PARC will need to recognize
that this institution-building function is more important than short-run
research achievements. PARC, therefore, needs to be careful in its endeavours
to promote research at universities. It must be noted that these perceptions
of the need for and importance of social science research in the agricultural
universities are shared by people in those institutions. The Faculty of
Agricultural Economics and Rural Sociology at AUF, for instance, has developed
an ambitious proposal for the establishment of an Agricultural Social Sciences
Research Ldentre. This Centre has many laudible features that, if implemented
effectively, could go far in achieving the ambitions held for social science
in the university system. Implementation, however, would require vigorous
reform of many aspects of university policy on research funding and management
and their interactions with the teaching program. It is to be hoped that
these issues can be addressed in Punjab's planned review of AUF.
33.       This process could certainly benefit from technical assistance in
the form of outside researchers working collaboratively in the university
departments. The consultants hired for this purpose should also participate
in formal teaching and dissertation guidance.
34.       Apart from the direct enhancement of university research, there is
evidently a need for further advanced training in social science disciplines
(including rural sociology and anthropology). This is indispensable if
universities are to develop their own PhD programs. The technical assistance
components would sustain the teaching and research function while the trainees
are pursuing their studies. Meantime, priority should be given to



- 143 -
ANNEX 7
Page 13
strengthening considerably the Master's degree programs before resources are
diverted to PhD programs.
35.       The provision of technical assistance under ARP II for AERUs and
social science u'iits at agricultural universities could also assist in
strengthening research and graduate training capabilities in social science.
However, it will be difficult to find suitably trained counterparts for this
purpose, and to make arrangements for their appointments in the institutions
concerned.
36.       Attempts should be made to introduce novel incentives to the reward
system for social science professionals. To be effective, these incentives
need to be significant. The following measures would be instituted.
(a)  A national prize in agricultural economics research could be
awarded, say, every three years for a contribution to social science
development. This would probably consist of an account of a major
research thrust and be supported by a number of publications. The
award might, for instance, consist of funds for travel to and
attendance at the International Association of Agricultural
Economists' 3-yearly conference plus some study tours (say,
US$5,000).
(b) An annual prize in each agricultural university for the best Master
of Science thesis submitted in the field of social science. The
merit would be judged by an appropriately constituted national panel
of, say, three persons. The prize (say, US$1,000) would be for the
purchase of professional books. A visiting program for promising
local scientists could also be used to enhance the capability and
motivation of Pakistani social scientists for research. Under such
a program, a research worker or university teacher-researcher could
pursue an approved program of research work at an appropriate
overseas institution. This should be operated as a well-advertised
competitive program with emphasis on establishing collabcrative
research links. At a cost of about US$20,000 for three-month work
programs, one scholarship per year appears appropriate.
37.       The main objective in the project is to develop and expand the
social science human resource base and to provide operational funding for
these researchers. The project would also provide funds for a limited amount
of staff accommodation and minor items of capital equipment.
Training
38.       Under the project it is proposed to provide 11 fellowships for
overseas PhD study for PARC's Social Division and AERUs. In addition 60
months of short-term training, including post-doctoral and refresher courses
overseas, would be provided. Details are provided in Table 2. The project
would also provide PARC's Social Science Division and AERUs 22months of



- 144 -
ANNEX 7
Page 14
technical assistance (Table 2 gives details). Table 1 shows the total planned
staffing of these groups during ARP-II.
39.       The project would strengthen the Faculty of Agricultural Economics
and Rural Sociology at the Agricultural University of Faisalabad, and the
Faculty of Agricultural Social Sciences at the Sind Agricultural University.
To upgrade these faculties, each university would enter into a twinning
agreement with an eminent, suitable overseas university, which would provide
assistance such as, among other things, teaching inputs, staff training
including MSc, PhD and post-doctoral studies, collaborative research, and
technical assistance for establishing currictla and research programs. The
figures shown in Tables 1 and 2 for training and technical assistance are only
indicative for costing purposes, and actual training and technical assistance
will depend on the twinning arrangements into which the two universities
enter.

PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Diagnostic Surveys and Adaptive Research
1.        There is considerable misunderstanding of the functions, roles and
responsibilities of different levels of research in Pakistan. This
misunderstanding is particularly prevalent amongst research scientists who see
adaptive research as their responsibility and any other involvement as
duplicative. There is also a worldwide traditional feeling that basic
research is 'senior' in the hierarchy, demands greater intellectual ability
and technical skills, and should therefore be isolated from the less
statistically rigorous forms of research; especially adaptive and on-farm
research.
2.        It is important to realize the interdependencies of different stages
in the development of new technology; basic research provides the building
bricks for constructing the technology, e.g.. a new gene, a new pesticide,
etc.; applied research takes the component and builds it into a piece of
technology, e.g., a saline tolerant variety or a technique for controlling a
disease; adaptive research modifies the technology to fit country, zonal or
local needs; and on-farm testing works at farm level to determine problems
associated with grafting the innovation into farming systems. It is clearly
difficult and often counterproductive to draw distinctions between different
stages of research and in the above scheme it will be seen that research
scientists, subject matter specialists and even front line extension workers
all have a part to play in developing and testing technology and in finding
out if it works at the farm level. The stages of technology development are
all essential; they are reinforcing and should not be duplicaLive.
3.        There is a real need to educate scientists worldwide into the role
of adaptive and on-farm research and into the importance of feedback in
research program planning. Unfortunately there are no procedures or manuals
to achieve this. In Punjab, Sind and Baluchistan adaptive and on-farm
research is being conducted by extension staff operating out of Adaptive
Research Stations. Development of these Adaptive Research Stations under
Bank-supported extension projects has formed an excellent base for zonal work,
and linkages between adaptive research and extension are strengthening.
However, linkages between applied research being undertaken at Research
Institutes and adaptive research are very formal and not very productive.
They involve discussion of work programs at coordination meetings held twice
each for kharif and rabi crops and mostly concern the endorsement of trial
proposals. Applied scientists are not involved in the diagnostic surveys nor
are adaptive research staff involved in the early identification and design of
applied research. Thus the interactive synergism is lost. Extension staff



- 149 -
ANNEX 8
Page 2
should be more closely involved in applied research activities. Research
scientists should be directly involved in multi-location testing and should
also participate fully in diagnostic survey work currently being undertaken
almost exclusively by extension staff.
4.        Diagnostic surveys refer to a process of data collection and
analysis to describe and understand the agro-climatic and socio-economic
conditions under which farmers in a given location make decisions so as to
identify farmers' priority problems and develop an appropriate research
program to solve them. The process usually begins with the collection of
secondary data, followed by an exploratory survey and then a verification
survey. This work can be undertaken relatively quickly (within several
months) and produces data which allows researchers to either establish an on-
farm research program testing existing technology at a particular location, or
aecide whether or what farmer problems must be worked on at an earlier stage
in the research cycle (for example, applied research at the research station)
to find solutions. To fully understand farming systems and farmers' problems
it is important that multidisciplinary teams of crops, livestock, forestry and
social scientists undertake the diagnostic surveys. The mingling of extension
and research staff on these teams would greatly assist the two-way
communication of farmer problems and appropriate technological solutions of
them between researchers, farmers and extension workers.
5.        There is a serious lack of social scientists skills on adaptive
research sites yet many of the problems being tackled have socio-economic
constraints at their base. The 'economists' sited at many farms are farm
management specialists rather than social scientists. Adaptive Research Farm
staff need to work with staff from the Agricultural Economic Research Units
(AERUs).
6.        In order to tackle these problems, it is proposed that under ARP-II,
ARIs at Faisalabad, Tandojam, Sariab, and the NWFP Agricultural University
should each appoint an Adaptive Research Coordinator who should be a social
scientist. The coordinator would (a) visit Adaptive Research sites and on-
farm trials and prepare brief reports on ongoing programs, with particular
emphasis on farmer reactions, to be distributed to all staff of the University
and Institutes; (b) wouid arrange for appropriate scientists to participate in
the diagnostic surveys conducted by AERUs and Adaptive Research; (c) would
ensure that eventually all major agroecological zones of the province were
covered by diagnostic surveys; (d) would ensure that the diagnostic study
results were published and disseminated; and (e) would act as the point of
contact for applied/adaptive linkages between staff below DG levels.
7.        At present some of the Adaptive Research Stations are taking a
somewhat narrow view of cropping problems and are not looking at farming
systems. To Adaptive Research staff 'Trees' are 'shade' and therefore a
problem to crops, and 'weeds' are directly competing for crop nutrients.



- 150 -
ANNEX 8
Page 3
Neither trees nor weeds are seen as a fodder resource because farm animals are
outside the Adaptive Research domain. It is essential to bring the livestock
perspective to adaptive research. It is recommended therefore that a
Livestock Specialist be included when necessary in the diagnostic survey team.
The project would provide operating funds to cover his expenses.
8.        No analysis of adaptive or on-farm trials' results or production of
individual research reports (other than annual reports) is undertaken at AR
stations. The SSMSs are expected to send their results to headquarters for
analysis, and have no 'feel' for ownership of their results. There is little
manipulation of on-farm financial data and technical. recommendations are
rarely checked for their affordability. It is essential both for the program
and for the motivation of AR steff that opportunities for data analysis are
provided at each ARS. This would require a desk top computer to be provided
in an air-conditioned office at each AR. The benefits of this provision
should be considerable. This provision was proposed earlier (Review of
Adaptive Research Programme and Future Recommendations - August 1988) and the
project would provide this equipment. Such facilities would again strengthen
the linkages between Bank supported research and extension. Research
scientists from ARIs would be able to undertake analysis of field results at
Adaptive Research Farms which would bring research and extension staff
together.

PAKISTAN
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Procedures for Collaborative Research Subproiect
Selection and Administration
1.        PARC would inform all suitable agricultural research institutes,
including agricultural universities, concerning the proposed collaborative
research program with eminent overseas research institutes, and would request
proposals for collaborative research subprojects from them.
2.        Suitable local research institutes would include those with a
nucleus of well trained staff who work as a group or groups to solve farmers'
priority productivity problems. Suitable overseas institutes would be those
with an extremely strong group of research staff, and with an international
reputation for doing world-class research in an area of vital interest to
Pakistan's development objectives and farmers.
3.        Subprojects to be submitted should:
(a) cover priority research as defined in the Provincial Research Master
Plans or the National Agricultural Research Plan;
(b)  be part of the submitting institutions's long-term research plan;
(c)  be prepared according to PARC's standard format for research
proposals including being based on a complete survey of related
research, both on-going and completed, in Pakistan and abroad, and
have adequately outlined the experimental design and procedures;
(d) be designed to have Pakistani and overseas scientists working
together in both Pakistan and the overseas country on common
research problems, not duplicate other ongoing or completed research
and have a stipulated time-frame for completion;
(e) inzlude the costs of travel, accommodation, subsistence and direct
operational costs of research (materials, fuel, vehicle O&M and
other incidentals) and a small amount for equipment and vehicles
(maximum 20X of subproject costs). Subproject costs should exclude
any civil works or staff salaries.
4.        PARC's technical committees would appraise the submitted
subproject's technical and financial aspects, and would recommend acceptance,
rejection or revision of them to PARC's executive Board. Institutes, whose
subprcjects the Executive Board approved, would enter into an agreement



- 155 -
ANNEX ll
Page 2
acceptable to PARC and IDA with the pertinent overseas institute.  The
institutes would have to agree to PARC's internal monitoring, evaluation,
accounting and auditing procedures for the subprojects.

PAKSTAN
AGRICULTUrAL RESEARCH II PROJECT
Suoervision Plan
Bank Supervision Input into Key Activities
1.        The staff input indicated in the table below is in addition to
regular supervision needs for the review of progress reports, procurement,
correspondence and so on. The estimated annual staff time for regular
supervision is four staff weeks per year for the first three project years,
then three staff weeks per year for the remaining four project years.
Borrower's Contribution to Supervision
2.        Starting in February, 1991, project agencies will submit progress
reports not later than end-February and end-August each year. The Project
Implementation Unit (PIU) of the Pakistan Agricultural Research Council (PARC)
will be responsible for coordinating and presenting progress reports for
national research agencies, and collating the provincial reports for
submission to IDA. At the provincial level, the Secretariats of the Punjab
Agricultural Research Board, Sind Agricultural Research Organization,
Baluchistan Agricultural Research Board, and the Research Coordination Board
of NWFP's Agricultural University, would be responsible for preparing and
presenting progress reports of their respective provincial agricultural
research agencies.
3.        PARC's PIU would be responsible for coordinating and arranging
supervision mission itineraries, arrangements, and wrap-up meetings.
